Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Zelman Cowen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, one of Her Majesty's Counsel learned in the law, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: SECOND PERIOD)
Advisory Council for Inter-government Relations Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.7 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Advisory Councilfor Inter-government Relations Act1976.
Airlines Agreement Act 1981 (Act No. 75 of 1981 )An Act to approve the execution of an agreement relating to air transport, and for purposes connected therewith.
Airlines Equipment Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 77 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Airlines Equipment Act 1958.
Airlines Equipment (Loan Guarantee) Act 1981 (Act No. 38 of 1981). An Act relating to guarantees of certain borrowings by a domestic airline.
Antarctic Marine Living Resources Conservation Act 1981 (Act -No. 30 of 1981 ) An Act relating to the conservation of marine living resources of the Antarctic and its surrounding seas.
Apple and Pear Export Underwriting Act 1981 (Act No. 14of 1981 )An Act relating to the underwriting of returns from the export from Australia of apples and pears.
Apple and Pear Stabilization Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 15 of 1981)An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Stabilization Act 1971, to repeal certain related Acts, and for other purposes.
Appropriation Act (No.3) 1980-81 (Act No. 55 of 1981 )An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No. I) 1980-81, for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1981.
Appropriation Act (No.4) 1980-81 (Act No. 56 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No.2) 1980-81 ,for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1961.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 16 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority Act 1981 (Act No. 116of 1981)An Act to establish a Gaming and Liquor Authority for the Australian Capital Territory and for related purposes.
Australian Federal Police Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 22 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Federal Police Act1979.
Australian National Airlines Repeal Act 1981 (Act No. 78 of 1981)An Act to repeal the Australian National Airlines Actl945 and for related purposes.
Australian National University Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 106 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian National University Act 1946.
Australian Tourist Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 27 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Tourist Commission Act 1967.
Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 49 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Act 1977.
Bounty (Non-adjustable Wrenches) Act 1981 (Act No. 18 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain wrenches.
Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 17 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Act 1978, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Printed Fabrics) Act 1981 (Act No. 102of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain printed fabrics.
Bounty (Textile Yarns) Act 1981 (Act No. 103 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain textile yarns.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 113 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 198) (Act No. 114 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964 to extend it to certain licences.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 48 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905.
Christmas Island Agreement Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 107 of 1981)- An Act relating to the Christmas Island Agreement.
Coal Excise Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 19 of 1981)An Act to amend the Coal Excise Act 1949, and for other purposes.
Commonwealth Banks Act 1981 (Act No. 29of 1981).An Act to amend the Commonwealth Banks Act 1959, and for related purposes.
Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 26 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act 1979.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Act 1981 (Act No. 74 ofl981 ) An Act to implement certain changes in Commonwealth functions.

Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 62 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Act1977.

Commonwealth Teaching Service Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.5 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commonwealth Teaching Service Act 1972, and for purposes connected therewith.

Companies Act 1981 (Act No. 89 of 1981 ) An Act to make provision for the government of the Australian Capital Territory in relation to the formation of companies, the regulation of companies formed in that Territory, the registration in that Territory of certain other bodies and certain other matters.
Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.2 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 94 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 95 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 4 of 1981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act (No. 2) of 1981 (Act No. 98ofl981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securit-ies (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies (Fees) Actl981 (Act No. 90of 1981 ) An Act relating to fees payable for the purposes of the Companies Actl98l.

Companies (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 92 of 1981 ) -

.

An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981 and for other

purposes.

Companies (Transitional Provisions) Act 1981 (Act No. 91 of 1981 )An Act to enact transitional provisions consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981.

Complaints (Australian Federal Police) Act 1981 (Act No. 21 of 1981)An Act relating to complaints made in respect of members of the Australian Federal Police, and for related purposes.

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 71 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.

Crown Debts (Priority) Act 1981 (Act No. 93 of 1981)- An Act relating to the priority of Crown debts.

Currency Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. II of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Currency Act 1965.

Customs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 64of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901.

Customs Amendment (Securities) Act 1981 (Act No. 67 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Customs Actl901 in relation to securities.

Customs Amendment (Tenders) Act 1981 (Act No. 45 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 to provide for tenders for rights to import goods at concessional rates of duly, and for related purposes.

Customs Securities (Penalties) Act 1981 (A.:t No. 46 of 1981 )An Act to provide for penalties for refusal or failure to give certain securities with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Customs Tariff Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 68 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Tariff Act1966.

Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 66of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Customs Tari.ff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975.

Customs TarilfValidation Act 1981 (Act No. 87 of 1981 ) An Act to provide for the validation of certain collections of duty of Customs.

Customs Undertakings (Penalties) Act 1981 (Act No. 47 of 1981)An Act to provide for penalties for breaches of undertakings with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Dairying Research Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 12 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Dairying Research Act 1972, and for related purposes.

Designs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 42 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Designs Act 1906, and for related purposes.

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2507

Tuesday, 26 May 1981
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 2.15 p.m., and read prayers.
DISTURBANCE IN KING'S HALL
Mr Sinclair-Mr Speaker, I raise a point of order. Quite obviously the noise that is presently coming from King's Hall will affect the proceedings of the Parliament. I wonder whether some action might be taken so that Parliament can meet in a proper way and without interruption.
Mr SPEAKER-The point of order raised by the Leader of the House is, of course, not a surprise to me because I heard the noise as I came into the chamber.. I have been aware of the noise all morning and I have considered very seriously what action should be taken. I have come to the conclusion that to take any action which would result in any forcible removal would probably be exactly what the protestors would wish. I am not prepared to allow the protest to prevent the Parliament from continuing to sit. Therefore, I think it would be best for us all if we proceeded with the business of the House forthwith.
PETITIONS
The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Trans-Australia Airlines
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans-Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services.
The Federal Liberal Party policy objective of selling Trans-Australia Airlines within 4 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines would inevitably result in:
(i) the creation of a private monopoly of major airline services in Australia;
(ii) higher domestic air fares.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans-Australia Airlines.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Campbell, Mr Humphreys, Mr Hurford, Mr Les Johnson, Mr Barry Jones, Mr Les McMahon and Mr Morris.
Petitions received.

1. The hotel industry in Australia is long-established and a valuable part of the community of this nation, providing places where the people may partake of the three basic needs of life: a place to eat, a place to drink, a place to sleep.
2. The hotel industry is accepted throughout the nation as occupying an important place in the social and economic activities of Australia.
3. The hotel industry is almost entirely within the private sector and makes a large contribution to the government in taxes of various kinds.
4. The hotel industry has a need to sustain an appropriate level of turnover and growth, to make profit, and to sustain and improve the level of employment.
5. The hotel industry of Australia is gravely concerned at published reports that the Government intends to increase excise charges on beer in the forthcoming Budget.
6. Sales of beer constitute the major source of income of most of the industry's 5811 hotels in Australia.
7. The very steep rises in beer excise in 1975 and 1978 severely retarded the growth of the Australian hotel industry.
8. Any further increases in excise on beer in the 1981 Budget would further retard the industry at a time when growth is essential to maintaining economic viability and the ability to serve the constantly growing needs of the community.
9. Such is its significance as a community requirement, beer occupies a place on the Consumer Price Index, and any alteration to its cost to the community would have an inflationary effect.
10. Any increase in excise would result in and force the hotel industry to pass on such increase to the consumer by way of higher prices for beer.
II. Higher excise wQuld be only the starting point for an upward spiral in beer prices because of the presence of other factors, such as State Licence Fee taxes, which are directly linked to excise rates.
12. The hotel industry of Australia already is gravely handicapped by heavy excise and other tax imposts, its growth is at a standstill; and, inevitably must decline if beer excise is increased.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives:
1. Recognise the importance and place of the Australian Hotel industry in the social and economic life of the nation.
2. Accept that the viability of the hotel industry is essential to the maintenance of employment opportunities and stability of the industry is essential to enable it to continue to provide for the hotel needs of the nation in this period of growing population and increasing tourism.
And further, that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives resist and reject any move to increase excise on beer.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Bendigo College of Advanced Education
To the Honourable, the Speaker and Memhers of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully petition:
That the government will act to reverse the decision to cease funding professional engineering education at Bendigo College of Advanced Education, since the removal of engineering studies from this College would seriously restrict educational opportunity for citizens in a large region of Victoria.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Bourchier.
Petition received.
Unemployment Benefit
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives of the Parliament of the Commonwealth of Australia, in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia, respectfully calls for:
The immediate and unconditional withdrawal of any proposals to deny unemployment benefits to unemployed people under ]8 years old.
That the level of benefits for all unemployed people, and including people receiving sickness benefits and pensions, be raised to at least 120 percent of the poverty line.
That future employment policies be devoted to job creation and the restoration of full employment rather than the evasion of this responsibility by hiding the unemployed in short term training and education schemes.
Public acknowledgement of the fact that unemployment is the result of insufficient jobs and not the fault of any minority group, the education system or the unemployed themselves.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Toxic Shock Syndrome
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives assembled.
The humble petition of the undersigned showeth that your petitioners are gravely concerned by the threats to women's health and lives posed by Toxic Shock Syndrome, and urge the following steps to be taken as a matter of urgency:
(1) Tampons, sanitary napkins, vaginal deodorants an.d similar sanitary products to be re-classified under Therapeutic Agents (not under "Cosmetics" as at present), in order to bring them under the scrutiny of the Australian Drug Evaluation Committee. We believe manufacture must be under the strictest hygienic conditions.
(2) After fe-classification, we urge an immediate government-funded inquiry into all aspects of manufacture and content of these products. This must lead to: (i) contents of product and instructions for correct usage to be shown on packaging; (ii) removal of synthetic fibres, chemicals or ingredients that are potentially or actually harmful to users.
(3) Allocation of extra funding for ongoing research by the National Health and Medical Research Council into these products, results of this research to be made public to ensure all health workers and potential users are fully informed. This campaign of public education would necessitate multilingual distribution of the information where appropriate.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly request that Parliament take immediate and positive action to avert possible threats posed to women's health by implementation of the above measures.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child.
Petition received.

by Mr Ewen Cameron.
Petition received.
Taxation: Child Care Expenses
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
A substantial contribution to Australia's economy by the payment of taxation and to the quality of life of fellow Australians, is made by parents in the work-force who have preschool children.
Taxation relief for adequate child-care is necessary to enable these parents to avail themselves of child-care facilities in order to take paid employment.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that tax-payers who incur child-care expenses in order to earn income should be able to have those expenses exempt from income taxation in the same way as other tax-payers can deduct business expenses from their assessable income.

Pensions
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled the petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That there is an urgent need to ensure that the living standard of pensioners will not decline, as indeed, the present level of cash benefits in real terms requires upward adjustment beyond indexation related to the movement of the Consumer Price Index, by this and other means your petitioners urge that action be taken to:
1. Adjust all pensions and benefits quarterly to the Consumer Price Index, including the 'fixed' 70's rate.
2. Raise all pensions and benefits to at least 30 per cent of the Average Weekly Earnings.
3. Taxation relief for pensioners and others oflow incomes by:
(a) The present static threshold of $75 per week for taxation purposes be increased to $100 per week.
(b) A substantial reduction in indirect taxation on consumer goods.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

by Mr Campbell.

Pensions
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned Austi:alian citizens showeth:
That the last increase in the Supplementary Assistance or rent allowance payable to Invalid and Age pensioners was made in 1974;
That since that time, average weekly rents, as gauged by increases in average Queensland Housing Commission rents, have risen by approximately 150 per cent and continue to rise at an alarming rate.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will at the earliest opportunity index Supplementary Assistance to increases in the cost of living by the same biannual process applied to increases in the pension itself.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.
Petition received.
Rosemount Repatriation Hospital
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled~ The petition of undersigned Australian citizens showeth:
Rosemount Repatriation Hospital has been of inestimable value to eligible repatriation beneficiaries for many years, and the closure or dispersal of the facilities available at the hospital would be a retrograde step.
The proposed decision to close Rosemount Repatriation Hospital through active discouragement by departmental administrative officers of any further referrals by medical officers of patients for medically prescribed occupational therapy is objectionable.
Closure of Rosemount Repatriation Hospital would severely disadvantage veterans in Brisbane's northern suburbs, as well as their relatives and friends who would be required to travel a great distance in order to visit hospitalised veterans at either of the two alternative repatriation hospitals.
Closure of Rosemount Repatriation Hospital and transfer of patients to Greenslopes would greatly add to the administrative burden of that repatriation and general hospital.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the government will reverse its announced decision to close the Rosemount Repatriation Hospital.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.
Petition received.
Tertiary Education Fees
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That public investment in tertiary education in the form of Federal Government funding is an investment in Australia's future and enables Australia to meet the demands of technological innovation.
That grave concern has been generated as a result of the Government's decisions to:
(a) abolish the education research and development committee,
(b) terminate special support for educational research, (c) scale down in-house and contracted research activities
of the Department of Education,

(d) scale down participation in international education activities, particularly UNESCO and OECD,
(e) further tighten already restrictive eligibility guidelines for TEAS,
(f) supplement TEAS assistance by means of a loan scheme,
(g) phase in tuition fees for students undertaking degrees and diplomas subsequent to an initial tertiary qualification,
(h) reduce grants to universities and colleges to take account of fee income,
(i) completely abolish 30 colleges of advanced education and
U) scale down various engineering schools in colleges of advanced education around Australia;
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will rescind these regressive decisions and desist from obvious attempts to pave the way for a complete re-introduction of tertiary fees.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.
Petition received.
Privacy and Personal Information
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
Paragraphs 107 and 108 of the Privacy and Personal Information discussion paper number 14, brought down by the Law Reform Commission in June 1980, undermines the family unit and the authority of parents, which is the Christian basis of our society, by proposing that "between 12 and 16 years, a child should be entitled to object to disclosure of information to a parent."
It is your petitioners sincere belief that:
(a) an adolescent between the ages of 12 and 16 is not at a mature enough stage of life to decide whether or not the disclosure of information to a parent will be in the best interests of the adolescent.
(b) as a record keeper has no responsibility for the child, and as his moral standards may be different to those of a parent, he should not be permitted to come between child and parent.
(c) a parent has a unique position which may enable him/her to perceive a situation that the record keeper does not. The parent must therefore be aware of the problem if remedial action is to be taken, and has the right to guide the child in the direction he/she feels fit.
Your petitioners therefore pray:
That the Members of the House of Representatives will reject the recommendation of the Law Reform Commission and find some other means to protect children from the excessive abuse of some parents.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Dobie.
Petition received.

Sydney (Kingsford-Smith) Airport
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of we the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That we oppose any expansion of the facilities of Kingsford-Smith Airport which entails the building of a new runway as it would have the following detrimental effects.
(1) The loss of one mile of waterfront including Lady Robinson's Beach and a huge part of Botany Bay.
(2) The loss of up to 1,230 houses and a drop in value of remaining neighbouring properties.
(3) The creation of more noise pollution in the area.
(4) The creation of more traffic congestion on streets leading to and from the airport.
(5) The forced diversion of Cook's River andfurther damage to the ecology of the area.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government will not consider satisfying the airport needs of Sydney by extending Kingsford-Smith Airport and that any decisions related thereto are not taken before there is an opportunity for adequate consultation with any community particularlyaffected.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Les J oltnson.

Tertiary Education Fees
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
(i) the financial situation of students enrolled at Australian post-secondary institutions is already extremely difficult (as indicated in the Commonwealth Department of Education's 1979 survey of student incomes and expenditure) and any substantial increase in the financial commitments of students would gravely worsen their financial difficulties.
(ii) The imposition of tuition fees for second degrees and post graduate degrees will deter many able persons from enr011ing in such courses and further preserve higher education for the rich.
(iii) The introduction of a loans scheme, subsequent review of the level of TEAS and the tightening of the eligibility criteria for TEAS will cause great financial hardship and will deter students from enrolling in tertiary institutions.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
1. Post-secondary tuition fees not be re-introduced for any courses or for any category of students.
2. The visa charges upon private overs,eas 'students be removed immediately.
3. The Government reverse its decision to introduce a loans scheme and instead increase and extend TEAS scheme.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr J ull.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Human Rights in Romania
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:

Petition received.
Soviet Passenger Cruise Vessels
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives of the Australian Parliament assembled, the petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Owing to an overwhelming and continuous demand by the Australian people for a cruise service within the economic scope of the working man and his family, we request the Government to lift the ban placed on Soviet Passenger Cruise Vessels entering Australian Ports.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

That the Romanian Government is committing genocide against the three million strong native born Hungarian population in Romania, as defined in Articles II and III of the United Nations Convention on the Crime of Genocide, which facts are well documented in the reports of the Amnesty International, the Minority Rights Group, the International League of Jurists, et cetera.
That the Article IV of the Convention states that: 'Persons committing genocide or any other acts enumerated in Article III shall be punished, whether they are constitutionally responsible rulers, public officials or private individuals.'
That the Article VIII of the Convention states: 'Any contracting party may call upon the competent organs of the United Nations to take such actions under the Charter of the United Nations as they consider appropriate for the prevention and suppression of acts of genocide or any of the other acts enumerated in Article III.'
As Australia is a Contracting Party to the Convention on Crime of Genocide, and that the Joint Committee for Foreign Affairs and Defence has already established precedents by having investigated Human Rights violations in other countries,
Your petitioners therefore pray:
(1) That the Joint Committee for Foreign Affairs and Defence to take up an investigation into the alleged genocide activities of the Romanian Government against its three million native born Hungarians in Romania; and
(2) That if such investigation should establish that Romania has been engaged in acts of genocide against its Hungarian minorities to call upon the competent organs of the United Nations to take such action under the Charter of the United Nations as they consider appropriate for the prevention and suppression of acts of genocide or any other acts enumerated in the United Nations Convention on Crime ofGenocide.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr McLean.

AirFares
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned electors of the Australian Capital Territory respectfully showeth:
1. That we oppose the massive increase of 30.7 per cent in air fares from Canberra to other capital cities compared with the average increase of7.4 per cent.
2. That this will cause serious hardship for many Canberra families who will not be able to afford to see their relatives from other parts of Australia.
3. That the ordinary families of Canberra should not be singled out for such massive increases and should receive equal treatment with the rest of Australia.
Your petitioners therefore pray that the Federal Government take immediate action to adjust air fares to and from Canberra in line with the average increase of 7.4 per cent or to compensate the citizens of Canberra for this massive increase in air fares.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Vehicle Testing Service: Phillip, Australian Capital Territory
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned electors of the Division of Canberra respectfully showeth that:
(1) Members of the public are seriously concerned at the drop of the high standard of vehicle testing, when this service is handed over to private enterprise.
It is well known that State testing of vehicles, where it occurs, is considerably inferior to that which takes place in the Australian Capital Territory.
(2) Considerable capital has been expended to further improve the service at Phillip. This taxpayers money should not be wasted.
(3) If vehicles are to be tested by private enterprise, the public will have to pay a fee.
If given the option, they would rather pay this fee to a government agency in the knowledge that the findings of the test would be completely un-biased, whereas through private enterprise, this may not always be the case.
(4) Southside population of Canberra are entitled to the service provided by the Phillip office. It would be difficult to give this service at Dickson due to the limitations of the building.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Kelly.

Staging of American B-52 Bombers
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned in the Northern Territory respectfully showeth:
That the proposed use of Darwin Airport as a refuelling and Rest and Recreation base for American B-52 bombers:
Jeopardises the welfare of the citizens of Darwin and of all Australians, ill that it established a stronger potential of Darwin being a nuclear target in any conflict between the USA and another power.
Impedes the sovereignty of the Australian nation in that Australian citizens would have neither control nor knowledge of whether B-52's: (a) Carried nuclear weapons (b) were engaged in bombing missions against another
State. Violates the Australian people's independence and links this nation irreconceivably with United States foreign policies thereby destroying the potential for independent Australian defence policy. Violates democratic procedures in committing the Australian nation to participation in future wars without due consultation and informed assessment by Australian citizens and further increases the militarisation of the Indian Ocean thereby increasing the likelihood of super power conflict in this region. Jeopardises the welfare of the Citizens of Darwin, in that noise pollution and the threat of accidental radioactive fallout will increase substantially to the residents. Negates the potential of the Australian nation to establish itself as a nation of Peace in the Pacific region and violates Australia's commitment to the Nuclear NonProliferation Treaty.
Your petitioners therefore pray that the Members of the House of Representatives will ensure that the Prime Minister withdraw the offer of Darwin, or any other location in Australia, as a base or depot for American B-52 bombers. And that furthermore will ensure that any request of such usage by the US Government be rejected.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrKent.
Petition received.

Questions Without Notice

percentage point'. The Minister for Primary Industry has since claimed that the statement referred only to a one per cent increase in the rate charged by the Reserve Bank of Australia's Rural Credits Department. Did the Government instruct the bank to increase the rate? If so, when will the Government table the instructions, as required by section 11 of the Reserve Bank Act? If not-that is, if it was an autonomous Reserve Bank decision-why was it misrepresented as a razor gang decision? Why did the Prime Minister refer only to the Australian Wheat Board when several other statutory bodies, including the Australian Dairy Corporation, will be affected by the Rural Credits Department's interest rates? Finally, why did the Prime Minister use the future tense-he said 'is to be'-when the Rural Credits Department rate was raised by one per cent a month before he spoke and by 0.5 per cent a fortnight after he spoke?
Mr HOWARD-It has been a long-standing practice, because of the significant policy implications, particularly to Australian primary industry, of variations in the interest rate charged on Rural Credits Department advances, for there to be very close consultation between the Government, normally through the Treasurer of the day, and the Reserve Bank of Australia regarding the level of interest although, as the honourable member has correctly implied, the final decision on the level of Rural Credits Department advances is a matter for the Reserve Bank to determine. There has been no section 11 notice, letter or whatever one likes to call it written; nor indeed is that in contemplation. I have written a letter to the Governor of the Reserve Bank informing him formally of the decision that was the subject of the Prime Minister's announcement.
Over the past few months there have been changes in the rate of interest charged on Rural Credits Department advances. There was one in March and there was one, I think, on 15 May. Also there have been some fluctuations in the market rate of interest charged on borrowings by the Australian Wheat Board to the extent that it is borrowing on the commercial market. The intent of the Review of Commonwealth Functions committee's decision and the intent of the Prime Minister's announcement was to indicate a view on the part of the Government that the differential ought to be less by reason of a further rise. The question of any additional increase which would at some time be needed to give full effect to the intent of the Government's decision-a further increase of one per cent-is something that the Reserve Bank itself must determine, taking into account a variety of factors, some of which have

been canvassed in the honourable gentleman's question.
WELFARE HOUSING PROGRAMS
Mr BOURCHIER-I ask the Minister for Housing and Construction whether his attention has been drawn to recent claims that this year the States will pay the Commonwealth more money in interest and redemption payments arising from the previous welfare housing advances than they will receive from the Commonwealth to finance current welfare housing programs. If so, can the Minister advise the House whether these claims are accurate?
Mr MeVEIGH-My attention has been drawn to the statement. I am grateful to the honourable member for Bendigo for drawing to the attention of the House the scurrilous statements made by people who obviously flirt with the truth. Let me state the facts. Each year since 1945, the Commonwealth Government has made available to the States certain sums of money to assist them with welfare housing. The total sum of money made available over the 35 years of the agreement has been in excess of $5,000m. The Commonwealth Government, particularly in recent years, has been very generous with its advances on highly concessional interest rate terms and also by giving a significant part of its contribution through grants. The net result has been that in each year-if we take 1980-81 as an examplethe States have been able to repay to the Commonwealth the interest and redemption on the year's loan in addition to meeting all their own administrative expenses and the cost of upkeep and maintenance on the dwellings. Yet they still have-I stress this fact-a credit of $142m. That is a significant sum of money that has been made available by the Commonwealth Government through its generous advances and its grants system.
Under the agreement the States are required each year to pay back to the Commonwealth one fifty-third of the annual advances. When we look at that we note that each year the agreement is in existence obviously there is a requirement to pay back more funds. But there is no basis in logic or in justice to compare the repayments of interest and redemption by a State for a total lending period of 35 years with the aunual contribution of the Commonwealth Government. If those honourable members who sit opposite advance that claim quite obviously what they are suggesting in fact and in essence is that if one gets a loan from someone with a firm commitment to repay it, one does not have to repay it. I repeat that through the generosity of the Commonwealth

little while until my colleague the Minister for

Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I thought that the honourable gentleman was referring to a Schools Commission report. I think it is worth drawing attention to the fact that that Schools Commission report emphasised the very serious matter that many Australian schools, if not all Australian secondary schools, were giving inadequate attention to large groups of young Australians. The Schools Commission made it plain that the schools were providing an adequate education for those with academic talents who might want to go on to a college or a university but that many other young Australians were going through school with a sense of failure and frustration, probably compounded by difficulty in getting work after leaving school. They had a sense of failure and frustration at school because courses and curricula related to that particular person's talents and inclinations were not available. Quite plainly that was a very serious indictment by the Schools Commission of many Australian secondary schools.

Education makes a statement on education matters. That will show the shape of some of the initiatives that the Government believes should be carried out. The honourable member also suggested that the general programs to improve employment opportunities in Australia have not been successful. That, of course, is where I part company with him. I believe Australia has demonstrated very plainly that under Australia's policies private enterprise over the last two years has provided more jobs than it has provided for a very long while. If one compares Australia's economy with the United States, Germany, Britain and a number of other countries, one will find that Australia's economy is growing and employment opportunities are growing whereas in many places around the world those opportunities actually are shrinking. Over the last two years Australia's private enterprise has provided 250,000 additional jobs. SPONSORED TRIPS FOR MEMBERS Mr HAYDEN-I ask the Prime Minister whether the reports are correct which appeared yesterday and today to the effect that two Government members were to be beneficiaries of an overseas trip sponsored by a self-interested media group and that according to one of the Government members-the honourable member for Bowman-'selection for the trip had come through Mr Sinclair's office'. If these reports are correct, is it usual for a senior Minister's office to be used to channel invitations of this nature in circumstances where it could be concluded that the offerer might believe he, she or they stood to gain some subsequent government advantage from the offer being accepted? Are the circumstances surrounding this matter consistent with the declared policy of the Government? Is it the Government's intention to allow this practice to continue in

future? Finally, will the Prime Minister instruct the two Government members concerned not to accept the offer, especially in view of the potential blatant conflict of interest involved and as spelled out in rules 8 (b) and 8 (c) of the code of conduct laid down in the report on public duty and private interest?
Mr SPEAKER-At the outset, I believed that the question would be out of order but I allowed it to proceed in case there were elements of it which were in order. Only one element of the question is in order and that relates to the part of the question addressed to the Prime Minister concerning the actions of the Minister for Communications. I inform the House that it is out of order to ask any Minister a question about the attitude, behaviour or actions of a member of the Parliament.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-As a result of your ruling, Mr Speaker, am I permitted to answer the whole question or only part of it?
Mr SPEAKER-The Prime Minister has asked whether it is permissible for him to answer the whole question. The answer to that is no. However, if there is anything that he needs to say in order to make his answer in relation to the actions of one of his Ministers meaningful, it is relevant to the question and may be said.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-What I will say will all be relevant to that central point. I think I should draw the attention of the House to the fact that there are different kinds of sponsored trips. There are many trips sponsored by governments. There have been leader grants to the United States of America. There have been visits to Japan at the request and as a guest of the Japanese Government. Many honourable members have participated over the years in both of them. I do not think anyone would have any doubt that a trip which is openly sponsored by another government-I emphasise the words 'openly sponsored by another government'-so long as it is within the judgment of a member of parliament, could be accepted. It has happened both ways on a number of occasions. I think we then start to get into a category of sponsored trips which can have some doubt cast over them. It has been customary for members of all parties, over many years, to participate in Qantas Airways Ltd inaugural flights or the launching of a ship. They are. commercial in their nature. I do not think anyone would want to cast any doubt in relation to them.
I think this invitation was one that was cast in the public domain. Nobody was seeking to do it in any secret way. Quite plainly members' travel is a matter that members themselves must decide. I

Motion
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition) (3.23)-1 move:
That this House is of the opinion, that in view of Rules 8 (b) and 8 (c) of paragraph 4.9 of the report 'Public Duty and Private Interest', viz~
An officeholder should not:
(b) solicit or accept any benefit, advantage or promise of future advantage for himself, his immediate family or any business concern or trust with which he is associated from persons who are in, or seek to be in, any contractual or special relationship with government;
(c) except as may be permitted under the rules applicable to his office, accept any gift, hospitality or concessionaI travel offered in connection with the discharge of the duties of his office,
and the clear potential for the conclusion to be drawn that the honourable member for Bowman and Senator Collard could be seen as transgressing those provisions by accepting free overseas travel from an unidentified business consortium, they should reject this offer forthwith.
I am sure there is no need to go over the episodic nature of what I want to present to the House as it has developed so far. The point 1 was making is simply that there is a matter of very high principle involved in relation to the propriety of the conduct of members of parliament. It is hackneyed, J know, to repeat the old axiom that justice must not only be done but seen to be done. Similarly, just as hackneyed but no less important is it as principle to declare that not only should there be proper conduct by members of parliament but also it should be seen as proper conduct by members of parliament. We should take every reasonable and proper step to put as much distance between ourselves and any sets of circumstances which may appear to compromise--even if, in fact, they fail-our integrity and independence as members of parliament.
For that reason the report 'Public Duty and Private Interest' was drawn up. I see many defects in it but it is better than nothing. It is stronger in its proposals than anything the Government is prepared to adopt. It is not our fault that this report has not been fully canvassed in this House. The Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) said

that he wanted a full debate on it. We would welcome that opportunity. But I remind honourable members that it is more than 18 months since the report was brought into the House. I outlined in the wording of the motion, which 1 will not repeat, the concern felt by members of that committee--J might mention that they are quite conservative members-in relation to the potential for a member of parliament, or other people, such as office holders or Ministers, to be compromised by certain circumstances and that, therefore, a code of conduct ought to be followed.
When introducing the report in the Parliament on 22 November 1979 the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) said, among other things:
The Government has accepted virtually the whole of the Committee's recommendations in regard to Ministers, public servants and other otficials.
This would include llS. In that statement 'virtually' means 'effectively' and 'totally'. Recommendation 8 (c) of the report declares that people such as members of parliament should not:
. . . except as may be permitted under the rules applicable to his office, accept any gift, hospitality or concessional travel offered in connection with the discharge of the duties of his office.
There is no other way to interpret the acceptance of sllch an invitation as I have mentioned-the conditions of which are familiar to honourable members - but as concessional travel which is associated with the discharge of the duties of office of those two members, Senator Collard and the honourable member for Bowman. They are both members of the Government's committee on communications. It has been said by the honourable member for Bowman, at least in newspaper reports, and no effort has been made by the Government to repudiate these statements, that the ofTer to him came through the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair). I refer to a report in yesterday's Australian Financial Review which states:
His selcction---
That is, Mr J ull's selection-
For the trip had come through Mr Sinclair's office, he said.
Not only do members have to disengage from this quite undesirable practice but also the Minister is responsible for conduct which is highly questionable in these circumstances on the face of evidence which is publicly available. The Government should take the first opportunity available to repudiate these statements if they are not correct. At least the Minister should repudiate them. He will have the opportunity to do so directly. But the key point at which I am getting is that these sorts of invitations are inappropriate invitations for members of parliament to accept. It seems to

Public Duty and Private Interest

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2525

me that there is a vast difference between an invitation at an official level offered regularly by a government such as the Japanese Government to members of this Parliament, which I understand is reciprocated by the Australian Government, where it is clear that the objectivity of members of parliament is not in any way sullied.

Parliamentary Committee to travel overseas to further its investigations then the cost of any such travel ought to be exclusively supported by Parliament.
The Prime Minister's response came on 20 February. I repeat it. The Prime Minister stated:
I . . . expressed my view to Mr Katter that this invitation should not be accepted.

The Opposition also received an invitation from Mr Rosenhain of Myer Emporium Ltd in the following terms:
I am quite prepared that the same terms and conditions be offered to members of the Federal Labor Party to travel to the same convention for the same length of time, e.g. departing Australia 27 May, returning to Australia in time for Government sitting 9th June.
I have arranged for a response to go today rejecting that invitation. Two wrongs will not make a right. Both the honourable member for Bowman and Senator Collard are quite wrong in their behaviour if they accept these invitations. Sufficient official trips are available to members of parliament. As well there is an overseas travel entitlement for each parliamentary term which would more than adequately meet the travel needs or travel lust of members of this Parliament without allowing themselves to be compromised in the way in which the 'honourable member for Bowman and Senator Collard seemed determined to be.
I repeat, more leisurely, the communications between myself and the Prime Minister on a similar matter in 1978. On that occasion my colleague the honourable member for Newcastle (Mr Charles Jones), as a member of the House of Representatives Standing Committee on Road Safety, contacted me about a letter received by the Chairman of the Committee, the honourable member for Kennedy (Mr Katter). I quote from this letter at some greater length. It stated:
It seems to me that what the letter boils down to is a proposal that a Parliamentary Committee should be sponsored by, and thereby become a beneficiary of, the Japan Automobile Association Inc. in making a trip to Japan. If my information is correct, it might seem that action may have been initiated by a member of the Committee--
The honourable member for Kennedy-
to encourage this offer of sponsorship.
That is a quite improper action in my view. The letter continues:
Mr Jones is concerned about the propriety of a Parliamentary Committee being sponsored in this way by organisations which might well be seen by the public, whether rightly or wrongly is not important, as being self-interested parties in the subject matter being inquired into by that Committee.
I share his concern and I know that those of my colleagues with whom I have discussed this matter share my concern. I should think that the principle is that if it is necessary for a

There can be no quibbling about the directness and the firmness of the Prime Minister's expression of view to the honourable member for Kennedy nor can there be any gainsaying that it was influential, because the honourable member for Kennedy desisted from any further purpose in this respect. The trip was not undertaken. I suggest that if it had been, there would have been enormous compromising of the members of the Committee who were participating in that way. Exactly the same set of circumstances apply in this case. We are surely not in such a mendicant position that we have to go around cap in hand soliciting for trips overseas or holding our breaths trusting that some wealthy and self-interested-I say this without any reservation-sponsor comes forward to promote us on a trip overseas. That immediately compromises our impartiality in any matters before us related to the self-interest of those people.
There is another aspect: Whilst I have the greatest respect for Mr Rosenhain of Myer Emporium Ltd, to the extent that I have been able to gauge his character on the few occasions I have met him, the fact is that he is a member of a consortium which has a vast interest in communications in an area which is directly a concern of the communications committee of the Government and, therefore, one which implicated very much the attention of the honourable member for Bowman and Senator Collard. Again, there is the aspect that the other members of the consortium are unknown to use. There is a very high degree of anonymity about that association. If we put all these scores together, there is the potential for people outside the Parliament who harbour reservations from time to time about just how independent members of Parliament might or might not be, to draw the most seedy and undesirable conclusions and then to broadbrush the lot of us as a result of those conclusions. The conclusions may not be justified but on the basis of superficial evidence there is a case to justify them and to draw the worst possible conclusions. At least, many of those people will believe this and so the suspicions will develop. I repeat: When it is all washed up, we all suffer to some extent as a result of that sort of thing happening. There is no need for any member of this Parliament to become a beggar or a mendicant, looking about for free overseas trips

2526 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Public Duty and Private Interest

sponsored by self-interested parties, hoping that one will turn up or, if one does fall his way, to grab it with both hands and refuse to give it up.
The recommendations in the report of the Bowen Committee of Inquiry concerning Public Duty and Private Interest make abundantly clear the proper standard of conduct in these matters. The standard of conduct which has been adopted in this instance by the honourable member for Bowman and Senator Collard is quite improper.
Finally, the behaviour of the Minister for Communications in acting as a sort of channel, as it were-a post office-in passing on these invitations, if the reports are correct, to the benefactors of this largesse is quite improper too. I believe that there can be no quibbling by members of this House about how they vote on this motion. After some preamble, the motion requires that the honourable member for Bowman and Senator Collard should immediately withdraw from being sponsored to engage in this undertaking-this trip. If honourable members vote against that motion, they vote against consistently proper standards of conduct being applied in matters such as this-proper standards of conduct which applied in the case of the honourable member for Kennedy when on one occasion he was soliciting for a free trip overseas.
Mr KATTER (Kennedy)-If the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) had not said that, Mr Speaker, I probably would not have been interested in this debate. What actually happened and the purpose of my taking part-

Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith)
(3.36)-Mr Speaker, r second the motion and I
will make my remarks now. I will be very brief because I understand that there are time contraints. Let me deal with the matter on the basis of what has already been elicited at Question Time. I would think that the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) is very much against any members of the Parliament, including the Senator mentioned and the honourable member for Bowman (Mr Jull), taking part in the trip that has been mentioned. I refer to the Bowen Committee of Inquiry concerning Public Duty and Private Interest which has been mentioned earlier today. The Committee's report clearly indicates that the terms of reference of that inquiry concerned what

procedures should be followed to determine whether there has been any breach of the high standards which are properly required of those in public office.
If we start by talking about the standards required of those in public office, those standards apply to every member in this Parliament. It just begs the question to argue about whether members of Parliament can accept offers made through a Minister or directly without being tainted by the fact that there were some pecuniary advancements for the member or members concerned. I refer to the Code of Conduct and the Committee's report which states:
The impression should be avoided that any person can improperly influence an office holder . . .
That would apply to a member of Parliament-
or unduly enjoy his favour.
I think it is very clear when we are talking about the Government at the present time that we have to be very stern about the fact that the Bowen Committee was set up way back in February 1978. It reported as far back as two years ago. Nothing has been done about that report by this Parliament. There has been no discussion or no implementation of the Code of Conduct. Is it any wonder that the honourable senator and the honourable member concerned might feel that they can avail themselves of a generous offer overseas because no rules have been laid down?
Ml' SPEAKER-I ask the honourable gentleman to excuse me for just a second. I should indicate to the House that when the Leader of the Opposition moved his motion he included in it a reference to a statement. There was some confusion, I think, on the part of some members of the House as to what the actual terms of the motion were. I propose to read the motion in the form which the Leader of the Opposition has given. The motion stated:
That this House is of the opinion, that in view of Rules 8 (b) and 8 (c) of paragraph 4.9 of the report 'Public Duty and Private Interest', and the clear potential for the conclusions to be drawn that the honourable member for Bowman and Senator Collard be seen as transgressing those provisions by accepting free overseas travel from an unidentified business consortium, they should reject this offer forthwith.
The particular items Rule 8 (b) and 8 (c) read:
An officeholder should not:
(b) solicit or accept any benefit, advantage or promise of future advantage whether for himself, his immediate family or any business concern or trust with which he is associated with persons who are in, or seek to be in, any contractual or special relationship with government;

Public Duty and Private Interest

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2527

(c) except as may be permitted under the rules applicable to his office, accept any gift, hospitality or concessional travel offered in connection with the discharge of the duties of his office.
The impression should be avoided that any person can improperly influence the officeholder and unduly enjoy his favour.
r hope that clarifies the matter for the House. I
call the Deputy Leader of the Opposition and indicate to him that I will subtract the period I have spent in explanation from the time allowed to him to speak.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-Thank you, Mr Speaker. I was making the point that the Bowen Committee reported on this and other matters as far back as July 1979. It is virtually a censure of the Government to find two years later that there has been no implementation or intelligent discussion of that report. If we had implemented the code of conduct recommended by the Bowen Committee, whether by statute or under Standing Orders, it would be very clear that no member of parliament could avail himself of offers such as those made to an honourable member in this House and a senator in the other place.
Mr Donald Cameron-You could not even accept a tea cosy under those rules.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-As the honourable member has pointed out, he could not accept any gift at all. There are rules and regulations governing the sorts of gifts that Ministers may accept. Gifts to Ministers create the impression that there is a way to buy favours. We cannot run a democracy if that is the case. We are talking about the implementation of cable television. Any of us who have had an interest in the communications field in the past, as many of us have, know that it is the most profitable aspect of television. It is highly centralised. Ownership is controlled. It will make enormous profits for a select group of people. In the time of the Labor Government enormous influences were brought to bear. People wanted cable television implemented in Australia because it would benefit certain proprietary interests, not the general public. There is great advantage in having cable television. It gives all sorts of benefits to those owning the cable and deciding what comes through it.
The honourable member and senator concerned in this matter will not find out anything new. If they want to know about cable television they will find that people involved in the telecom-. munications system in Australia have enough input into this area. They know what it means, how it is implemented, who owns it elsewhere in the world and why these multinationals want to bring it to Australia and own and control it. This

is not in accordance with a number of principles which we hold on this side of the House. We should consider communications from the point of view of public ownership, public control and public benefit, not private ownership, exploitation and profit. If the two gentlemen concerned accept this offer, saying that they will give us information that we cannot get ourselves, they will leave themselves wide open to the belief that they are beholden to those who paid their way. It would be a stupid thing for them to do. It would determine whether they held their own seats and what public opinion of them would be.
Not one of us in the Parliament can escape the fact that if we accept a trip from anybody, other than a trip provided by the Parliament, it will be deemed by public opinion that we owe something to those offering the trip. Of course we would. It is un businesslike of the Myer Emporium Ltd or anyone else to suggest that this is an intelligent thing to do. The Myer Emporium is interested mainly in domestic and retail sales. Communications come within the national network in respect of which a fair amount of public money has already been expended in setting up the base frame. It is important that nobody should intrude into this area by offering a fringe benefit in order to gain profits for shareholders or some select group. What we think should have been done has been made very clear by my leader.
The Parliament went to a lot of trouble to set up the Bowen Committee. It set out its terms of reference in 1978. The Committee reported in 1979. Nothing has been done since. There is a responsibility on the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the whole Cabinet to implement quickly in the Parliament the codes of conduct recommended by the Bowen Committee, whether by statute or Standing Orders. This would remove the necessity to move a motion in this House urging two members of parliament, in their own interests and in the interests of the dignity and stature of the Parliament, not to accept an offer of private travel. Anybody in his right mind would know that such offers should not even be considered. The offer has been made and the members concerned are obviously interested in going. It makes it so much worse that we have to take the time of the Parliament to move a motion suggesting that they do not go. If they go their seats should be forfeited. They say that they will come back with clean hands and that they will have learnt something they could not have learnt in Australia. If they want to go, let them get some intelligent advice from the communications research group in Australia.

Mr JULL (Bowman) (4.3)-Prior to my entering--
Mr Hayden-I raise a point of order, Mr Speaker. Two speakers in succession from the Government side have received the call in this debate. Would it not be appropriate for there to be a speaker from this side of the House?
Mr SPEAKER-Nobody from the Opposition stood.
Mr JULL-Prior to my entering this Parliament, 1 had a career in radio and television which spanned some 131 years. During that time J covered a multitude of areas of experience, ranging from journalism to the management of a metropolitan television station. During the period I was employed by a television station, 1 undertook a number of study tours. In fact, I visited the British Broadcasting Corporation twice. J also visited Independent Television, the National Broadcasting Corporation, the Columbia Broadcasting System and the Tokyo Broadcasting System. It was of interest and of great value to me to be able to learn during those visits of the tremendous advances that were being made in the electronic media. In fact, I have a working knowledge of cable television.
It is true that earlier this year Senator Collard and I were approached by a representative of the Myer organisation who suggested that it would be advantageous for us to go to the cable television conference which will be staged next weekend in Los Angeles and which will be attended by some 8,000 delegates from around the world. It is true
that Senator Collard and r approached the Minis-
ter for Communications (Mr Sinclair) and discussed it with him. It is also true that we had some doubts as to whether this trip would be viewed as some sort of pay-off. An invitation was duly extended, as the Minister has rightly pointed out. Because of the incredible explosion that will occur in this country in terms of communications during the next decade, Senator Collard and I decided on balance that it would be of very great advantage-not necessarily to our committee, although it would be, and not necessarily to the

Government, although I am sure it would be-to this Parliament if we were to attend.
One has only to consider the standard of debate that goes on in this House every time a communications Bill comes into the place to realise that an appalling lack of knowledge exists in this area in this House. Indeed, some of the classic speeches have been made in the Senate by the Opposition's spokesman on communications, Senator Button. Indeed, it would be of great advantage if Senator Button in fact attended this conference because he certainly has very little knowledge of the way cable television operates at this stage. The idea of going on this journey was for us to learn about and to communicate what is happening with regard to the tremendous international telecommunications explosion which is now occurring. In fact, we looked at the possibility of examining the developments that are occurring with satellites. Australia, obviously, is entering a period in which it will launch its own satellite. We will then have a communications system which will be of great benefit to each and every Australian, especially Australians in outback areas. At no time did J ever feel that my position would be compromised. Certainly it would not be compromised to any greater extent than it was during the 131 years that T worked in radio and television. It was not compromised during my time in that job when the opportunity for overseas travel came up. Certainly, I would not think my position would be compromised any more than that of a number of journalists throughout this country who take inaugural flights on a very regular basis.
Mr Cohen-You are treading on thin ice now.

We are arguing against this power being put in the hands of private consortia such as the group that is seeking to send the honourable member off on this trip. Before the start of the Tenth Session of the Law of the Sea Conference, which was held in New

In short, the reports of the Australian delegations to the Law of the Sea Conference represent what I believe is a successful attempt by successive Australian governments to make information, on a complex subject of great significance to Australia, comprehensible and accessible to the Australian community. I commend them to the attention of honourable members. The Law of the Sea Conference is perhaps the largest, certainly the longest running, diplomatic conference that has been convened since the founding of the United Nations. It is an ambitious attempt by the international community to try to bring order and certainty to an area covering seven-tenths of the earth's surface-an area where legal doubts and uncertainty currently exist. The draft Convention, which is now being considered by the Conference, has been painstakingly put together over the last six or so years. It contains over 350 articles on every aspect of the Law of the Sea. There are articles defining the limits of national jurisdiction over the territorial sea, exclusive economic zone and continental shelf; there are articles providing for freedom of passage through straits used for international navigation and through archipelagos; there are articles on the protection and preservation of the marine environment and on the conduct of marine scientific research; and of considerable significance for the future, the Draft

York from 9 March to 24 April this year, there was reason to hope that it might be possible to conclude the negotiations on the outstanding issues, so that the way would be cleared for a Law of the Sea Convention to be opened for signature at a special meeting of the Conference in Caracas, perhaps later this year. As honourable members may be aware, however, the new United States Administration decided to review its policies towards the Law of the Sea Conference and indicated that it would not be able to participate in further substantive negotiations until that review was completed. Because the Conference is proceeding on the basis that the provisions of the new Law of the Sea Convention should, if possible, be adopted by consensus, one of the consequences of the American decision was that it was not possible to conclude the negotiations at the Tenth Session. As I indicated in response to a question in this House on 30 April, the Government has taken available opportunities to explain to the United States Government that, while we fully understand its need to review its policies on the Law of the Sea, we hope that the review will be completed quickly so that a Convention, in which Australia has vital interests, can be adopted as soon as possible. Indeed, I spoke in these terms to Secretary of State Haig during my visit to Washington in March.

Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) -by leave-The statement by the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) is important. He referred to the long history of the Law of the Sea negotiations which have gone on for six years to eight years. Tremendous efforts have been made by over 150 countries to bring the negotiations to a successful conclusion. Yet, we find ourselves in this position, without any consultation, of the United States effectively stalling the completion of the Convention on the very eve of its success. We have to be very critical of this situation because the reason given was that the new Reagan Administration virtually wanted time to consider its position. In the process of so doing, the Administration also sacked all its negotiators. The point that ought to be emphasised is that these negotiations have been going on, as Tsaid, for six years, seven years or eight years.
Today I gave notice of a motion which I hope we will pass. It related to the Law of the Sea and to the fact that we will want this House to say that the Australian people want the Convention adopted. We would like this to be done when the Tenth Session resumes in Geneva in August 1981. We want to do that to convey to the United States the views of the Australian nation. I add that a motion in similar terms was passed unanimously by the Canadian Parliament. I think that we can say on this matter that we have a bipartisan issue and that we want to see it carried into effect on the basis of Australia's fundamental interests. It is important. The Government should applaud our views on this matter. Australia has a lot of economic and strategic interest hanging on the successful conclusion of these negotiations. For a start, the territorial sea will cover 12 miles and not three miles, as is now the case. In addition, there will be a 200-mile economic zone which will give us effective, if limited, sovereignty over an additional million square miles of territory off the Australian coast which undoubtedly is rich in mineral, hydrocarbon and marine resources. It will guarantee freedom of passage for our merchant fleet and navy through international straits and archipelagic waters and it will provide for internationally regulated and ordered exploitation of the resources of the high seas and deep sea bed which

will be not only to our advantage but also to the advantage of underdeveloped and poorer countries. There will also be protection and preservation of the marine environment.
The Opposition can think of nothing more important than that element. But, in the process of expressing our views, I advert to the fact that the Government has given away a lot of its national interest by legislating domestically in an area which I think might well tie the hands of our negotiators in the international conference if they are asked whether their own Government has passed legislation pursuant to its sovereignty virtually to give title for the offshore areas to the States. We are not here to berate the States but, if we are at a negotiating table, at least we ought to be consistent in our views. If, in the national interest, the Law of the Sea Convention is adopted speedily, for the reasons I have mentioned, how ridiculous we would look because the territorial sea for the first three miles would belong to the respective States and the next nine miles would belong to the Australian national government. That is how inconsistent our legislation is. In regard to the protection of the marine environment against dumping, there will be no legislation to restrict dumping outside the three-mile limit but if we adopt the Law of the Sea Convention we will not have that constraint. It is important that we clearly make the point now that, unless something is done urgently, we will face continual delay. There is some suspicion that the Reagan Administration deliberately decided not to go ahead with the adoption of the Convention because of certain financial interests in the United States which were concerned about a restriction on seabed mining.
Mr Jacobi-Oil companies.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN Oil companies. If that is the position, it is reprehensible conduct. We have to say so forcefully because, if 150 countries of the world can agree, why is it that the major superpower can say that it has to have another look at it? It does not help its own interests from the point of view of its leadership in the world and it will not engender support from many of the nations that have spent so much time trying to rationalise the many problems associated with these measures. We regard the whole of the Law of the Sea Convention as extremely important. Many of the world's resources are connected with the sea and difficult questions concerning the balancing of interests between national and international considerations must be resolved. We pay tribute to our own representatives who have played such a splendid part but we ask again: How tenuous is their situation if we have a divided national position and not a united international

In other words, we are saying internationally that we want the Law of the Sea Convention to give clear sovereignty to our Australian nation; yet, as I have said, in the process we have become prone to fragmenting the many parts of this Convention.
