SINAI PEACEKEEPING FORCE: AUSTRALIAN PARTICIPATION
Ministerial Statement
Mr STREET (Corangamite-Minister for Foreign Affairs)-by leave-The purpose of this statement is not to announce a decision by the Government on Australian participation in the proposed force-Australia has still not yet formally been invited to participate in the force---but to set before the House the facts and information additional to that available when I last spoke on this subject on 14 May, and to indicate the issues which the Government will take into account in reaching a decision. Honourable members will be aware that the focus of Middle East peace negotiations over the last three and a half years has been the process begun by President Sadat when he visited 1erusalem and addressed the Knesset in November 1977. President Sadat's unprecedented and dramatic visit to lerusalem led to negotiations between Egypt and Israel, with the United States also involved, which culminated in the Camp David Accords in September 1978 and the Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty of March 1979.
Essentially the Camp David Accords and the peace treaty can be divided into two parts. The first dealt with the Israeli occupied territories of the West Bank and Gaza and set out a procedure for implementing 'full autonomy' for the inhabitants of those two areas after the establishment of an elected self-governing authority. The Government regrets that progress on this aspect of the

peace process has been disappointingly slow. Nevertheless this aspect of the peace process, while proving extremely difficult to implement, has helped to isolate the issues involved and has contributed to a greater understanding, by those outside the Middle East as well as those directly involved, of those issues. It must be emphasised that the undertakings concerning the return of occupied territory in the Sinai must be met if progress on these complex issues is to be made. The Government, for its part, believes that successfully carrying out the accords as they relate to the Sinai could be crucial in influencing those sections of the Arab world which have strong reservations about whether the complex problems of the Middle East can be resolved by means other than conflict.
Undoubtedly the major achievement of the peace process has been the bilateral peace treaty between Egypt and Israel. This has proved that Israel and an Arab country, despite 30 years of bitterness and war, can negotiate a substantive peace agreement involving basic concessions on both sides. There is no doubt that as a result both Israel and Egypt have received substantial benefit. Israel's security interests have been enhanced substantially while Egypt has regained a good part of the Sinai and is now able to concentrate on domestic economic reconstruction. This is a significant achievement and must not be put at risk. Progress on the second part of the accords, the bilateral aspects of the peace treaty, has been good. Diplomatic relations have been established, Ambassadors exchanged and civil aviation, commercial, telecommunications and other links have been established. In addition, and most importantly from the Egyptian perspective, Israel has withdrawn from approximately two-thirds of the Sinai. Egypt now controls all of the Sinai west of an interim line running from EI Arish in the north down to Ras Muhammed on the southern tip of the Sinai. Annex I of the peace treaty stipulates that Israel will complete its withdrawal from the Sinai 'not later than three years from the date of exchange of the instruments of ratification of this treaty'. Hence by the end of April 1982 Israel is scheduled to have withdrawn completely from the Sinai. It is quite clear from the text of the treaty that Egypt, Israel and the United States envisaged that United Nations forces would supervise both the Israeli withdrawal from the Sinai and the implementation of the security arrangements agreed on in the treaty. Article VI of Annex I stipulates the role and functions of such United Nations forces.
Since the signing of the Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty in March 1979, the United Nations has

2538 REPRESENTATlVES 26 May 1981

Sinai Peacekeeping Force

been unable to give any mandate which would enable UN forces to operate in a peacekeeping role in the Sinai. The United Nations Emergency Force-- UNEF-which was carrying out peacekeeping operations in the Sinai ceased operations in July 1979 when the Soviet Union indicated that it would veto an extension of its mandate. Honourable members will be aware that an Australian helicopter unit was based at lsmailia as part of UNEF. The United Nations Truce Supervisory Organisation-UNTSO-to which a number of Australian officers are seconded, still has about a dozen or so observers in the Sinai but could not be expected to assume the tasks of a Sinai peacekeeping force without a drastic revision of its mandate and a substantial increase in its numbers in the Sinai. Again it is clear that the Soviet Union, which has consistently opposed the Camp David Accords and the peace treaty, would not agree to an extension of UNTSO's mandate. In recent months there have been informal discussions in the United Nations about the possibility of obtaining a United Nations peacekeeping force to oversee the final Israeli withdrawal from the Sinai and to implement the peacekeeping functions as envisaged by the treaty.
On 21 April the Deputy Prime Minister and Minister for Foreign Affairs of Egypt, His Excellency Kamal Hassan Ali, requested the President of the Security Council to initiate, as a matter or urgency, consultations with members of the Security Council. The purpose was to explore the role of the United Nations, and specifically whether the Security Council would monitor and supervise the withdrawal of Israeli forces from Egyptian territories, and participate in the reciprocal security arrangements through the establishment of the force and observers, provided for under the Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty. On 18 May in a letter to the Egyptian Deputy Prime Minister and Minister for Foreign Affairs, the Japanese President of the Security Council, Mr Nisibori, replied to the effect that following consultations with individual members of the Security Council, including the Soviet Union, the members of the Security Council were unable, for various reasons, to reach the necessary agreement to facilitate the withdrawal of the Israeli forces and to participate in the security arrangements provided for in the Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty. I seek leave of the House to incorporate in Hansard the letter from the Egyptian Minister for Foreign Affairs, Kamal Hassan Ali, to the President of the Security Council and the President's reply to the Egyptian Minister.
Leave granted.

The letters read as follows-

Following are the texts of the Egyptian letter of 21 April to the Irish president of the Security Council and of the reply dated 18 May from the Japanese president of the council:

Begins: Excellency,

18 May 1981

With reference to the letter dated 21 April 1981 from your excellency addressed to the president of the Security Council for the month of April, I should like to inform your excellency
that the individual consultations I have undertaken lead to the conclusion that the members of the Security Council are unable, for various reasons, to reach the necessary agreement on
the proposal contained therein.

Accept, Excellency, the assurances of my highest consideration.

MASAHIRO NISIBORI President of the Security Council His Excellency Mr Kamal Hassan Ali Deputy Prime Minister and Minister for Foreign Affairs of the Arab Republic ofEgypt Cairo (Egypt)
21 April 1981
Excellency,

Upon instructions from my government. I have the honour to bring to your attention a matter which is directly related to the maintenance of peace and security in the Middle East region.

Ever since the inception of the United Nations, the organization has been called upon to ensure the maintenance of peace and security in the Middle East. At this time, when serious attempts are being made to bring the Middle East closer to a comprehensive peace, it is legitimate to expect that the United Nations, particularly. the Security Council whose primary responsibility is the maintenance of international peace and security, should continue its established practice in undertaking its constructive role to ensure the durability, and consolidation of peace and securi ty in the Middle East.

The Security Council was seized with the situation in the Middle East since armed hostilities erupted, on November 22, 1967 the Council unanimously adopted resolution 242, a resolution which is considered the universally agreed basis for a just and comprehensive settlement to the Middle East problem. Security Council Resolution 242 (1967) required that the establishment of a just and lasting peace in the Middle East should include the application of both the following
principles:

Quote: (1) withdrawal of Israel's armed forces from territories occupied in the recent conflict.

(2) termination of all claims or states of belligerency and respect for and acknowledgement of the sovereignty, territorial integrity and political independence of every state in the area and their right to live in peace within secure andrecognised boundaries free from threats of acts of force.
unquote . . .

The resolution further prescribed, as security arrangements:

. . . guaranteeing the territorial inviolability and political independence of every state in the area through measures including the establishment of demilitarized zones . . .

In accordance with this resolution, the Secretary-General appointed a special representative to promote agreement and assist efforts to achieve a peaceful and acceptable settlement. Towards this end, the special representative submitted, on

Sinai Peacekeeping Force

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2539

February 8th 1971 an aide memoire, to Egypt and Israel. The aide memoire called upon Egypt and Israel to enter into a peace agreement as a first step towards a comprehensive settlement of the Middle East problem. Such an approach was endorsed by the general assembly with increased majorities when it adopted resolutions 2799 (XXVI) and 2941 (XXVII).
In this context, Egypt and Israel concluded a peace treaty on March 26th 1979 and registered it with the Secretary General on 15 May 1979 in accordance with article 102 of the charter. Consistent with the underlying principles and basic provisions of security council resolution 242 (1967) the treaty stipulated that Israel will withdraw its armed forces behind the international boundary between Egypt and Israel by April 1982. Within this context and to ensure the durability and further consolidation of peace and security, Egypt and Israel agreed to reciprocal security arrangements and a request that the United Nations provide forces and observers.
In the light of the above, the government of the Arab Republic of Egypt views it timely for the Security Council to consider, on an urgent basis, the modalities for carrying out the required responsibilities for ensuring peace and security in the Middle East.
Towards this end, my government has instructed me to approach your excellency, with a view to consulting the members of the Security Council in order to ascertain the role of the United Nations, and specifically whether the security council, would undertake to facilitate the withdrawal of the Israeli forces from Egyptian territories' and to participate in the reciprocal security arrangement herein referred to through the establishment of the force and observers provided for in the Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty.
In this connection, it may be important to confirm that Egypt's request emanates from its solemn commitment to the principles of the charter of the United Nations, from a deep conviction that the presence of the United Nations is the most appropriate formula for ensuring the maintenance of peace and security, and that this request falls within its sovereign rights in liberating its territories and pursuing a policy aimed at establishing a just, comprehensive and lasting peace in the Middle East.
As we have been conducting informal consultations with Security Council members for several weeks, I would appreciate receiving at the earliest possible date, the results of your excellency's consultations.
Accept, Excellency, the assurances of my highest consideration.
(Signed) KAMAL HASSAN ALI Deputy Prime Minister and Minister for Foreign Affairs of the Arab Republic of Egypt His Excellency MrNoelDorr President of the Security Council Unite! Nations, N.Y. 10017
Mr STREET-I deeply regret that the letter of the President of the Security Council effectively rules out, for the immediate future at least, any United Nations involvement in the withdrawal of Israeli forces from the Sinai under the terms stipulated by the Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty. United Nations involvement in this crucial area is therefore not an option. The House will be aware that Security Council Resolution 242, which was agreed on after the 1967 Middle East war, called

for an Israeli withdrawal from occupied territories. Egypt has expressed the hope that a nonUnited Nations multinational force will only be of a temporary nature and that the way should be kept open for its replacement by a United Nations force at a later stage. Against the possibility that the United Nations would not agree to fulfil the role envisaged for it, President Carter in letters dated 26 March 1979 to both President Sadat and Prime Minister Begin undertook, and I am quoting directly, 'to ensure the establishment and maintenance of an acceptable alternative multinational force'. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard the letters from President Carter to President Sadat and Prime Minister Begin.
Leave granted.
The letters read as follows-
THE WHITE HOUSE Washington 26 March, 1979
Dear Mr President: I wish to confirm to you that subject to United States Con-
stitutional processes:
In the event of an actual or threatened violation of the Treaty of Peace between Egypt and Israel, the United States will, on request of one or both of the Parties, consult with the Parties with respect thereto and will take such other action as it may deem appropriate and helpful to achieve compliance with the Treaty.
The United States will conduct aerial monitoring as requested by the Parties pursuant to Annex I of the Treaty.
The United States believes the Treaty provision for permanent stationing of United Nations personnel in the designated limited force zone can and should be implemented by the United Nations Security Council. The United States will exert its utmost efforts to obtain the requisite action by the Security Council. If the Security Council fails to establish and maintain the arrangements called for in the Treaty, the President will be prepared to take those steps necessary to ensure the establishment and maintenance of an acceptable alternative multinational force.
Sincerely, JIMMY CARTER His Excellency Mohamed Anwar EI-Sadat, President of the Arab Republic of Egypt

Sincerely, JIMMY CARTER

Mr STREET-It has been suggested that the formation of such a force is not a part of the Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty accords. Nothing could be further from the truth. President Carter's letter is and remains an essential docu~ ment accompanying the peace treaty ratified by both Egypt and Israel. The failure of the United Nations to fulfil the role envisaged for it in the peace treaty represents the basis for the current endeavours by Egypt, Israel and the United States to fulfil their obligations to the peace treaty through the establishment of an alternative multinational peacekeeping force.

In the absence of any effective United Nations peacekeeping force the United States concluded an agreement in September 1979 with both Egypt and Israel on interim arrangements in which the already existing Sinai Field Mission, which is an American establishment, staffed by American civilians, would continue its monitoring functions in the Sinai. The Israelis whilst agreeing to these arrangements made it clear that they were acceptable only in the interim period while they still had a substantial force stationed in eastern Sinai. It is essential that the Parliament understands that Israel regards the establishment of an effective peacekeeping force as an integral part of the Treaty, indeed as an essential prerequisite for their final withdrawal from the Sinai.

Put bluntly, in the absence of United Nations involvement, failure to form an effective multinational force would risk provoking a refusal by Israel to withdrawal from the final third of the Sinai. The consequences of such a refusal cannot be overemphasised. It would be a very severe, if not catastrophic, blow to the Camp David peace process and the Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty. Beyond this, and in the absence of any progress on the crucial Palestinian issue, it could imperil future prospects for attaining a comprehensive, negotiated peace in the Middle East. Further, It would seriously dent the faith of two nations which, after decades of conflict and death, have deliberately chosen the path of peace through

negotiation and compromise as an alternative to mutually ruinous conflict.
The fundamental reason why Israel agreed to withdraw from the Sinai was that a satisfactory set of arrangements to guarantee its security had been agreed upon. These arrangements quietened Israel's legitimate fears about its security and gave it the confidence to take the decision to withdraw. With an effective peacekeeping force in place in the Sinai, Israel's southern flank will be secure. It is the Government's hope that in these circumstances the Israelis would move with greater confidence to negotiate on outstanding issues affecting the future status of the West Bank and Gaza, including the right of the Palestinian people to a homeland.
Since President Sadat's visit to Jerusalem in November 1977 he has pursued bold and imaginative policies in the cause of a negotiated settlement with Israel. Any failure to implement fully the provisions of the peace treaty could react adversely on Egypt's ability to carry out its commitments. Moreover, the uncertain and volatile situation in Lebanon reinforces the urgency of dissipating, by peaceful means, the atmosphere of suspicion and conflict which has dominated the Middle East for so long.
Amongst the factors which will be taken into consideration by the Government if and when a formal approach is made to Australia to participate in a multinational peacekeeping force in the Sinai are: First, that Australia's overall interests are served by maintaining the momentum for the peaceful resolution of conflict in the Middle East. Secondly, the Government will need to consider the means available to maintain the momentum for peace. As I stated earlier, the inevitability of a Soviet veto has once again effectively removed United Nations involvement from consideration. Thirdly, is the proposed multinational peacekeeping force in accordance with the Camp David Accords and the spirit of United Nations Resolution 242, both of which enjoy the unreserved bipartisan support of this Parliament? Fourthly, can the Government support the formation of such a force? Finally, if Australia is formally invited to participate in such a force, are the conditions of participation such as to warrant consideration of Australia's involvement?
Australia has established a record of participation in international peacekeeping operations, both within and outside the United Nations context, which the people of Australia can be justly proud of. By their very nature peacekeeping operations involve risks. The safety of any Australian force associated with the proposed peacekeeping.

Sinai Peacekeeping Force

Even as I address the House today negotiations proceed between Egypt, Israel and the United States about the membership, structure and detailed functions of such a force.

On 19 May the Secretary of State replied to my letter and dealt with some of the general concerns and provided answers to some of the specific questions I had raised. Additionally, Australian officials both here and overseas have sought, and are continuing to seek further information concerning the proposed Sinai force. It is apparent

The parties are presently negotiating the establishment of a peacekeeping force of a size sufficient to carry out the functions agreed by Egypt and Israel. Precise details are being worked out at this time. As we understand current thinking, the force is likely to consist of about two thousand men. 2542 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Sinai Peacekeeping Force

the infantry battalions, or they could come from different states altogether. As was envisaged in the peace treaty, it is anticipated that units of the force will be located in the north in the Rafah area and at Sharm Al Shaykh. The contingents would man check points and observation posts and operate patrols in Zone C as defined in the peace treaty. The observers would perform verification functions in Zones A, Band D.
The financial arrangements are yet to be finalised, although the hope has been expressed that those contributing states that are in a position to do so will contribute their services, or some part of them, free of charge. The duration of participation by contributing countries remains unclear. It is not expected, however, that national contingents will be required to serve indefinitely although there is likely to be a requirement that an acceptable force be maintained in the Sinai so long as either party believes it to be necessary. Current planning is based on the hope that various contingents would agree to serve for a specific duration.
As I mentioned earlier, the reaction of other Arab countries will also be important to us. Their major disagreement has been over the mechanism for solving the Palestinian issue which they regard as the core of the Middle East issue. They have not taken exception to, nor do they dispute, Egypt's right to regain its territory from Israel. This, I believe, is a major distinction when considering likely Arab reaction to possible Australian involvement in a multinational Sinai peacekeeping force.
In setting out the above details I have sought to give as full an account as possible of the current state of play concerning the proposed peacekeeping force. I emphasise again, that at this stage Australia has not been invited to participate and I have already referred to the negotiations which are still proceeding between Egypt, Israel and the United States. It is clear, nevertheless, that Australian participation would be welcomed by those countries and that it may only be a matter of time before a formal invitation is extended to Australia.
I believe it would be appropriate to make a further brief mention of Australia's interests in the Middle East. In my statement on 14 May I explained that whilst Australia has limited influence in that area our interest in the maintenance of stability in that region is not limited. The Government believes that this interest is best served by the process of agreement through negotiation and compromise rather than recourse to war. For many years Australia has had important

links with the Middle East. These links included our dependence for many years on the Suez Canal as a major trade route to the Mediterranean and Europe. Thousands of Australians fought in Egypt, Libya, Palestine and Syria in two world wars in the cause of peace and, over the last 25 years thousands of migrants from Arab countries have settled in Australia in search of peace. In more recent times this country has contributed with distinction to peacekeeping operations under United Nations auspices in this region. An opportunity now exists for Parliament to express its views on whether Australia has a possible further role in the same cause. In this context I quote from the speech of the Leader of the Opposition (Mf Hayden) on 21 September 1978, Hansard page 1308, in which he said, inter alia:
Camp David has been a first step but a critically important One. President Carter's initiative in convening the meeting, and his political skill and courage in keeping it going, have created the essential momentum for the next steps, which may well be even more difficult. Our hope is that all the parties, and others who must necessarily become involved now, will maintain the momentum.
Further in his speech he stated:
The Prime Minister's statement on this matter is a carefully considered one, as is proper in such a complex. sensitive and important matter. [ commend him for that and assure him. this House and the nation that on this great matter genuine concern for the achievement of lasting peace in the Middle East is a matter of bipartisan commitment.
In conclusion, 1 hope that, on such an important issue, the debate will be a well informed and rational one. The Government is hopeful that it will be possible to maintain a bipartisan approach towards the Camp David Accords, the EgyptIsraeli peace treaty and the need to maintain the momentum for peace in the Middle East. Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (4.59)-The Opposition notes the remarks made by the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street). We wish to place on record the fact that we are of the opinion that a statement would not have been made at this stage if we had not raised this matter in recent weeks in this Parliament seeking information as to what transpired by way of negotiations between the Government and the United States. The Minister referred to the remarks made by my colleague, the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) which are deemed to be, and were, supportive in 1978. We wish to make it very clear that the remarks made in 1978 were made in the context of what the Camp David Accords might

Sinai Peacekeeping Force

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2543

have been expected to achieve. Obviously, the framework for the achievement of any solution in that area was with the United Nations Security Council acceptance of the Camp David Accords, and the approval of any force in that area by the United Nations Security Council, not only in accordance with the Accords but also in accordance with the treaty and the framework attached thereto. We can place on record that none of that has been achieved. It is a bit improper to suggest, by quoting the Leader of the Opposition's remarks out of context, that we can now say: 'There should be a bipartisan attitude in this matter'.
I want to deal at length with some of the matters. In fact, let me advert to remarks made at the time of the signing of the Camp David Accords. No Arab head of state supported the Camp David Accords or spoke out in support of Sadat. We also have the position, in accordance with the framework, that the factors to be taken into account, says the parties, are in accordance with Security Council resolutions 242 and 338 in all their parts. Those resolutions, as all honourable members know, relate to the fact that the question of peace and stability in the Middle East is to be under the control of the United Nations, and the United Nations Security Council in particular. In fact, the representative of the United Nations was to report periodically as to the progress of the peace negotiations and the peace implementations. Further, the Minister adverted to the Palestinian problem. Let me refer again to the framework for the Camp David Accords. It is stated:
Egypt, Israel, Jordan and the representatives of the Palestinian people should participate in negotiations on the resolution of the Palestinian problem in all its aspects.
Is it not a fact that the Minister's statement raises many more questions of a very serious nature than it purports to answer? Let me deal with them. I refer to the question of the United Nations participation in the Middle East. When I raised the matter in this House and subsequently in statements outside the House, I referred to the importance of the fact that the Camp David Accords of 1978 and the peace treaty of 1979 clearly specified that the United Nations was to playa role. Even more than that, Article VI, paragraph 8 of Annex I of the peace treaty unequivocally stipulated that the United Nations force could not include permanent members of the United Nations Security Council, that is, it could not include troops from either of the super-powers-in other words, troops from the United States of America and the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics were barred. I believe it is abundantly clear that adequate efforts have not yet been made to secure a United Nations force and that there is still a great deal of

room for positive diplomacy in which Australia should actively participate.
The Minister made reference to such efforts. Let us have a look at the facts. The Minister said that there had been informal discussions in recent months. We must ask why only in recent months? It is over two years since these agreements were concluded. In terms of concrete moves in the United Nations, the position is even worse. Let me emphasise this point. The best that the Minister could do was to refer to approaches made on 21 April, a few weeks ago, and on 18 May, a few days ago. Those are the only approaches made to the United Nations. This is incredible!
Are we really being told that formal approaches were made only in the last couple of weeks to have the United Nations participate in the major role which was clearly envisaged for it some two years ago? I am aware of the earlier efforts of the United Nations Emergency ForceUNEF-and the United Nations Truce Supervisory Organisation-UNTSO. I am also aware of the role which has been played in southern Lebanon by the United Nations International Force in Lebanon-UNIFIL.
When we talk about Lebanon, let us place on record that it is only a matter of miles away from this area with which we are concerned. There is the greatest hostility there. The Lebanese community in Australia is divided on the basis of war taking place around Beirut, the problems of the Syrians and their missiles and the question of the Israeli's suggesting that they will take immediate action against the missiles. This is all taking place a matter of a few miles away from the area we are talking about. The point I am making is that the international community, including Australia, should make every effort to secure United ~ations participation in the present phase, if that IS at all possible.
Let us have a look at the Camp David Accords of 1978 and this suggestion of President Carter's letters being part of the 1979 treaty. If we look closely at the Camp David Accords we realise several things. In the Minister's speech he quoted President Carter as undertaking:
. . . to ensure the establishment and maintenance of an acceptable alternative multinational force.
That was done by way of a letter of 26 March 1979. The Minister might also have quoted the preceding sentence which committed the United States to:
. . . exert utmost efforts to obtain the requisite action by the Security Council.

2544 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Sinai Peacekeeping Force

I believe that there is room to do this. Australia should independently be exerting efforts in that direction.
What is more important is the question of the relationship of the letter to the treaty and the Accords. I do not think the Minister is well advised in this matter. He said that the establishment of an American led force is 'part of the peace treaty and accords'. It is not. It is very clear, if we look at the Accords and the treaty, that it is prohibited.
Firstly, I make the point that, whilst the letter was written on the same day the peace treaty was concluded, it is in no way a formal part of the treaty. Secondly, and more importantly, we cannot have an ancillary document to an international treaty which contradicts the treaty and still claim that it is part of the treaty. Let me repeat that Article VI paragraph 8 of Annex 1 of the treaty provides for the forces to be drawn from countries other than either of the super-powers.
Further, and equally importantly, Annex 1 continues:
The parties have agreed as follows:-
With respect to the provisions of paragraph 8 Article VI of Annex 1 if no agreement is reached between the parties they will accept or support a United States proposal concerning the composition of the United Nations force and observers.
But the letter contradicts the treaty on both points. It not only envisages a non-United Nations force but also a force led by the United Statesone of the super-powers. Everywhere we look-in the 1978 Camp David Accords, in the 1979 peace treaty and in Security Council Resolutions 242 and 338-the reference is always to a United Nations force.
Thirdly, the Minister claims that President Carter's letters are essential documents accompanying the Egypt-Israel peace treaty. This is not the case. There is no evidence for that claim; indeed, there is evidence to the contrary. The treaty states that the papers which are attached to the treaty are protocol, annexes and maps. It in no way refers to letters-in particular, the Carter letters to President Sadat and Mr Begin. Those letters are clearly not part of the treaty. Therefore, there can be no reliance placed on them by this or any other government as a legal basis for supporting a non-United Nations force.
The key point in the Minister's statement comes at page 14 of his advance text. The Government's case in making a decision to contribute to forces to Sinai will have to rest on this point, but the point is false. The Minister says that

the mandate for the proposed force will be Article VI of Annex I of the 1979 Egypt-Israel peace treaty. In fact, there is no mandate in that article for anything but a United Nations force.
One can read and re-read Article VI and read and re-read the peace treaty and the Camp David Accords. In Article VI, which the Minister claims to contain a mandate for a non-United Nations force, there is no mention of anything but a United Nations force. There are nine paragraphs in Article VI. In those nine paragraphs there are no fewer than nine references to a United Nations force. There is no reference whatsoever to any other force but a United Nations force. There is not one sub-clause which does not refer specifically to such a force. How then can the Minister claim that Article VI of Annex 1 of the treaty is a mandate for the proposed force? In fact, there is no mandate. If there is no mandate the Minister should admit it. It is irresponsible to claim a mandate where it does not exist. It is misleading to claim a mandate when none exists. It is misleading this House and the Australian people to claim a mandate where none exists.
I refer to the attitude of the Arab world and west European countries. The Minister said in his statement:
The Government, for its 'part, believes that successfully carrying out the Accords as they relate to the Sinai, could be crucial in influencing those sections of the Arab world which have strong reservations about whether the complex problems of the Middle East can be resolved by means other than conflict.
I do not think that is a fair statement. Firstly, it implies that those sections of the Arab world which have strong reservations about the Camp David Accords believe that armed conflict is the only solution. That is just not true. That it is not true is amply and dramatically illustrated by the fact that the Western European allies of the United States are searching at this very moment, and have been for the last 15 months, for an alternative to the Camp David Accords because they believe that the Accords do not provide a realistic answer. To put it at its lowest, there are very serious questions not only in the majority of the Arab states but also in the capitals of Western Europe about whether the Camp David Accords any longer provide a realistic solution.
As to the attitude of the Australian Labor Party, after the conclusion of the Camp David Accords the Australian Labor Party conference welcomed the signing of the treaties as an important step in the search for a just, comprehensive and lasting peace. We emphasise the importance of United Nations Security Council Resolutions 242 and 338, which referred to the role of the United Nations. Further, we emphasised that

Sinai Peacekeeping Force

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATJVES 2545

both the Israelis and the Palestinians are entitled to independent states and homes of their own. The Labor Party welcomes steps towards peace in the Middle East, but we must be sure before we commit Australian troops that we are really involving ourselves in a process which will be constructive and not one that could easily heighten tension between the super-powers and the countries of the region and possibly drag Australia into the maelstrom of fighting in the Middle East.
The Minister claimed that the Government has made no decision. It is quite clear from his statement that, as was the case with Vietnam, this Government is anxious to get involved. Let me point out to the Minister the sort of public reaction any decision to send Australian troops to the Middle East is likely to receive. Editorial writers and commentators in at least six of the largest newspapers have spoken directly or indirectly against this venture, and I suggest that they are espousing the views of the Australian people, irrespective of their politics. They are conservative newspapers and commentators who in the past have supported the overseas adventures of conservative governments. This time they are showing that, unlike the Government, they have learned from past mistakes. These leaders and shapers of public opinion include the Age, which has pointed out:
. . . all of Egypt's fellow Arab nations are opposed to the Camp David pact and that many of the Europeans see the Accords as bankrupt and inadequate to the task of moving towards comprehensive peace.
The Canberra Times stated:
There is no good reason why Australian forces should go all the way to the Middle East just to prove that this country is an ally of the United States.
It added:
Australia ought not to encourage Washington to think that it may be able to assemble under its own sponsorship an international force which is independent of the UN. Australian policy should work independently for the UN force which the Egypt-Israel treaty envisaged.
In the Sydney Morning Herald, Mr Guy Harriott said:
I think we should not touch the Sinai force with a forty foot pole. It is simply too much of a risk.
Mr Harriott added:
South East Asia is within Australia's sphere of direct interest; the Middle East is not . . . I thought that argument had been settled in the early 1950s when Menzies was reluctantly forced to concede that Australia's strategic concern was no longer primarily with the Middle East and the Suez Canel, but with our own region.
Many similar comments have been made elsewhere. I have quoted those today because they are sound and sensible expressions of view, and to warn the Minister of the cristicism he will face

and the storm that will burst about him if his Government decides to send Australian troops to the Sinai. The Minister's statement shows that the Government is set on that course. In the 1960s and 1970s the rice fields and jungles of Vietnam proved to be a political and military quagmire for the United States and Australia. Why should we venture back into that quagmire? In the J980s this Government again seems ready to risk the same sorts of difficulties.
The Minister indicated in his speech some of the factors that will be taken into consideration by the Government if a formal approach is made, and 1 should like to consider those for a moment. Firstly, he said that Australia's overall interests should be served by maintaining the momentum for the peaceful resolution of the conflict in the Middle East. The Labor Party agrees, but we believe that we must be extremely careful about the vehicle we choose before we become involved. The Minister went on to refer to the Camp David Accords and United Nations Resolution 242. ] have already pointed out that those documents refer only to a United Nations force. The Minister asked: Can the Government support the formation of such a force? (Extension of time granted). I suggest that on an issue of such importance to the country, and given our tragic history of involvement with the United States in Vietnam, a more appropriate question would be this: Can the Government and the Opposition support the formation of such a force? Let us consider Namibia. Already we have committed a peacekeeping force of some 300 in what we hope will be the resolution of the dispute between South Africa and the South West African People's Organisation. The Government has not yet explained how we will discharge both obligations, and again I come to the point that both forces will be far from our shores and, apparently, under somebody else's command. At the same time, we are pulling out our forces at Butterworth and apparently rationalising our military commitments in the immediate region.
Tn conclusion, I have made it clear that the Labor Party has a sincere and fundamental commitment to peace in the Middle East on the basis of involvement of the United Nations, as envisaged in the Camp David Accords, and with due regard to thc principles laid down in United Nations Security Council Resolutions 242 and 238. I have also made it clear that the proposal being considered by the Government cannot be said, from the point of view of either international law or political reality, to be in any sense a proposal which involves the United Nations. The Opposition believes that in these circumstances the

2546 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

proposal is fraught with danger and may well contribute to the escalation of tension in the Middle East rather than to a real solution of the problems of that tortured region.

The Government has received timely offers of assistance from professional health organisations. The Royal Australasian College of Physicians, whose members have undertaken much of the original research on respiratory, cardiovascular, renal, metabolic and paediatric problems among the Aboriginal population, considers that Aboriginal health is a major social issue in which its involvement could be increased. The Australian Medical Association has also expressed its concern and offered assistance. The Government appreciates the interest shown by these and other professional bodies and intends to pursue the offers, particularly in relation to evaluating health services to Aboriginals.
The Reports
There have been numerous reports on Aboriginal health. They have all painted much the same
picture. r turn now to the background of the three
reports which the Government has considered. The House of Representatives Standing Committee on Aboriginal Affairs received references on Aboriginal health from the House of Representatives on 1 December 1976 and 10 March 1977. On 16 March 1978 the then Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, Mr 18.n Viner, gave the Committee its terms of reference. I seek leave to have them incorporated in Hansard.
Leave granted
The document read as follows-
House of Representatives Standing Committee on Aboriginal Affairs Terms of Reference-Aboriginal Health
(1) Examine the health problems of Aboriginals with particular attention to-
(a) the prevalence of different types of disease suffered by Aboriginals and Aboriginal communities;
(b) the relationship between Aboriginal health and environmental, social and cultural factors;
(c) the effectiveness of existing health care programs for Aboriginals generally, and the adequacy of Western European-type health services to cope with the health problems of Aboriginals; and
(d) alternative methods of health care delivery that take account of Aboriginals' lifestyles, including camp situations.
(2) That the Committee consider ways and means by which-
(a) persons with appropriate qualifications can be encouraged to assist Aboriginals achieve a better standard of health, and
(b) Aboriginals including traditional healers can participate in the development and delivery of health care services to their own communities, and in any modification of existing services.
(3) That the Committee recommend possible courses of action.

Mr MacKELLAR-I thank the House. Having ~aken evidence through submissions, public hearmgs and community visits, the parliamentary Committee presented its report on 20 March 1979. The trachoma report was prepared by the Royal Australian College of Ophthalmologists. It describes the most extensive screening, diagnosis and treatment coverage of eye disease ever undertaken in this country. The field teams involved in the National Trachoma and Eye Health Program also detected and arranged treatment for a variety of other illnesses. The report was presented to the Parliament by me on 1 April 1980. A program effectiveness review was conducted by Commonwealth officials during 1980 to determine, as far as possible, the health status of Aboriginals and the eiTectiveness of current Aboriginal health programs and of other programs which affect Aboriginal health. The review team drew information about the programs from Commonwealth departments and took into account the findings of the two reports I have already mentioned as well as other related reports. The officials also visited Aboriginal communities and consulted with people directly involved in community-based health care programs. The officials' report was completed during 1980.

Main Findings of the Reports

These three reports are obviously interrelated and

it is appropriate for the Government to consider

them together in its response to the parliamentary

Committee's report. They contain common fun-

damental concerns and recommendations but

differ felt it

considerably necessary to

in detail. We give careful

ahtatevnetitohnerteofotrhe~

major themes of each and the various proposals

advanced. The reports all confirm three things:

The unsatisfactory health status of Aboriginals; the urgent need to improve the environ-
mental conditions affecting Aboriginals' health;
and the importance of increasing Aboriginal involvement in the design, control and delivery of health care services.

Environmental Improvements
Each report referred to the need to increase efforts to improve the environmental conditions in which many Aboriginals live. Much of the ill health experienced by Aboriginals is directly associated with a substandard physical environment. The parliamentary Committee recommended that 'the highest priority be given and immediate action taken to provide clean and adequate water supplies to all Aboriginal communities'. The trachoma report found in rural Australia that when thtl hygiene or Aboriginals

2548 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Committee on Aboriginal Affairs

approached that of non-Aboriginals so did the prevalence rate of the diseases measured. So for those people living in unhygienic conditions it recommended that appropriately designed and equipped houses be provided and that houses should contain a water supply to cater for all necessary uses and should also have efficient water-based sewerage systems and garbage disposal facilities. The officials' report also recommended that greater priority be given to the provision of housing, water supply, sanitation and electricity.
The disruption of Aboriginals' traditional lifestyle has given rise to a number of widespread health problems, especially where Aboriginals have been obliged to live on reserves or settlements, camps, or in rural or urban areas, under conditions of poverty. The problems include poor water supplies, dangerous sanitary conditions, overcrowding and limited access to nutritious food. The spread of illness and disease has been facilitated, especially respiratory, ear and skin i~ fections, and intestinal parasite illnesses. The disruption of their society and lifestyles and its effects have also produced despair, chronic disadvantage and alcohol abuse. This situation is not amenable to short term solutions. Government programs over many years have sought to improve services and facilities in such ways as will encourage Aboriginals to assume responsibility for decisions about their lifestyles and their futures. The effort must be maintained and expanded while the problems remain as serious as they are.
Once preventive measures begin to take effect, succeeding generations will not be saddled to the same extent with the wide spectrum of diseaseseye, ear, respiratory, gastro-intestinal, skin, et cetera-that are less frequent in the nonAboriginal population. Of course, in the meantime, health services play an important role by alleviating distress and improving the quality oflife. It is our intention that all children, including Aboriginal children, will have a much better start to life than has been the case for so many in the past and which is so important in the long term for improved health status.
Aboriginals themselves have stressed the prime importance of adequate housing. That priority has been, and continues to be, reflected in the programs of the Department of Aboriginal Affairs. For example, since 1975-76, the Government has provided $298.2m for Aboriginal housing, including hostels. During the last full financial year, 1979-80, 1,632 houses were built or purchased for Aboriginals and 205 home loans provided throughout the States and the Northern

Territory with Commonwealth funds totalling $59.9m. The last Budget provided in excess of $72m for Aboriginal housing and accommodation. These substantial increases in funds for Aboriginal housing show how determined we are to respond to Aboriginals' own priorities.
The figures give the lie to uninformed claims that funds have been cut. The Government has considerably expanded funds for Aboriginal housing and accommodation even while these reports have been under consideration. Those increased funds are flowing into this priority area of need. We have not delayed action; we have accelerated it. Much remains to be done and we do not deny that. But the Government's achievements in Aboriginal housing are substantial and are growing each year. In fact, in some States, the time is now within sight when the housing backlog will have been overtaken.
Increasing attention is also being given, within the Department of Aboriginal Affairs allocation, to programs administered under the general heading of community management and services for municipal-type services to Aboriginal community organisations, reserves and settlements. Here our role has been essentially a topping-up of State and local government activities. However, the Commonwealth expects the States and the Northern Territory also to honour their responsibilities towards their Aboriginal citizens. Aboriginal people, like other citizens living in a particular State or Territory, are entitled to expect basic municipal services to be provided by State or local government. The Commonwealth has done much to help overcome past neglect, but it is not, and should not be regarded as, the only source of funds for providing services to Aboriginal people. Whilst we expect the State and Territory governments to take up this challenge, we understand that a special effort will be needed. That is what the Government had in mind in committing an extra $50m over five years for environmental health improvement.
I mentioned earlier that the efforts which have been made have not always been as effective as we would have liked. The Government is looking to shared funding arrangements under this program in a joint Commonwealth-State effort to eradicate at least the worst of the environmental conditions under which many Aboriginal communities in rural and remote areas live. Finding, storing and distributing clean water supplies and installing waste disposal systems are costly exercises. We must get our priorities correct and make sure that Aboriginal communities' priorities are fully known. The technology used must suit the community environment. Good planning here may

2550 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Committee on Aboriginal Affairs

The parliamentary Committee recommended that 'Aboriginal cultural beliefs and practices which affect their health and their use of health services. . be fully taken into account in the design and implementation of health care programs'. The Government supports this view. The people best equipped to advise on relevant cultural beliefs and practices and the appropriate adaptation of health services are Aboriginals themselves. They are also best equipped to undertake important bridging roles in health service delivery. Their responsibilities in the health care services which they use must be rapidly expanded.
In supporting Aboriginal health programs, the Commonwealth will require greatly increased Aboriginal involvement in the design, control, management, delivery and evaluation of services. There is a need for the States and the Northern Territory to provide greater opportunities for Aboriginals to play significant and expanded roles. To achieve this goal, increasing numbers of Aboriginals will have to. be trained for, and employed at, all levels. Aboriginals must also take a real and decisive part in policy and program decision making. The way each State government meets this goal may be different. We are firmly committed, however, to ensuring that the goal is met, and we will be taking these questions up with State Ministers. In the same spirit, I should like to mention that some State colleagues with responsibilities in Aboriginal affairs have, in the past, expressed their concern that, in administering programs on behalf of the Commonwealth, they may not have enough flexibility. For example, they may wish to reallocate funds to reflect changing circumstances. The Commonwealth would be prepared to examine this problem with any of the States which wish to redirect priorities towards mutually-acceptable policy goals, and especially Aboriginal involvement.
It is unacceptable that, at the present time, there are no qualified medical practitioners of Aboriginal descent, and insufficient numbers of Aboriginal people qualified for other professional and para-professional roles. We propose to have discussions on a number of fronts to make sure Aboriginals have greater opportunities in these areas. It is vital that Aboriginal doctors, nurses, administrators, nurses' aides and other health care staff should be available. We acknowledge that the necessary education and training will take time. In the short term, however, we can also look at redefining existing practical responsibilities of Aboriginals presently employed in health care. Both the States and the Aboriginal medical services need to be involved in the provision of opportunities through the National Employment

and Training System and other programs. Nor should the role of traditional healers, who are intimately involved in services in traditional communities, be overlooked. Opportunities must be found to make use of their particular skills, which can be built on or adapted according to circumstances. We shall continue to give strong support to the employment of Aboriginal health workers, who have important preventive and primary care roles to perform, now and in the foreseeable future.
Availability and Awareness of Choices Most of us would take it for granted that we can choose the kind of health care we think appropriate. The parliamentary report makes it clear that more needs to be done to make a range of choices known and available to Aboriginal people. One difference between Aboriginals and other groups in Australia is that Aboriginal people are not fully aware of the range of health care options available. The parliamentary Committee recommended that 'Aboriginal communities be given the opportunity to determine the type of health service that will best suit their needs and available resources.' We endorse this view and commend it to State governments. The Government will propose to the States and the Northern Territory discussions between officials, and with Aboriginal people, on this matter of choice. These discussions will be looking for a cooperative basis for increasing Aboriginal awareness of the health service options and expanding the opportunities for Aboriginal communities to influence the types of services provided to them. This is a fundamental commitment and one which is supported by each of the reports on Aboriginal health to which I have referred. In fact, the Commonwealth and Victoria and Aboriginal organisations are now co-operating in a State government working party which has these very objectives. There is also scope for improving the relationship between general practitioners and Aboriginals, and increasing the use of primary care services by Aboriginals.
I mentioned before Commonwealth grants to Aboriginal medical services. These organisations provide primary health care services which are acceptable and accessible. Some of them have also begun to provide preventive services and ancillary programs in response to community needs, expressed through the close involvement of many Aboriginals in the services. The Aboriginal medical services are a fine example of Aboriginal initiative and of readiness to assume responsibility to improve their own health status. The Government endorses their valuable role in this field and

Committee on Aboriginal Affairs

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2551

commends also professional co~operation between Aboriginal medical services and State services so that their work is complementary. The Aboriginal and Islander Medical Service in Darwin, which is funded jointly by the Commonwealth and Northern Territory governments and works in closely with existing health services, is a good example of what can be achieved through co-operation. The Government will be prepared to consider provision of assistance to similarly innovative approaches elsewhere.
We shall, of course, continue our existing support to Aboriginal medical services. We shall also consider, together with the States and Aboriginal people, the expansion of complementary community-based services where they offer the most effective form of health care, and work towards an expanded range of options for primary IT '!dical care. Aboriginals must be involved to the fUllest possible extent in selecting the most appropriate health care options. This policy is consistent, the Government believes, with a cardinal principle of the Government's health policy, that people should have freedom of choice in an integrated and comprehensive system. That is something the Government wishes to see for all Australians, not the least of whom are the Aboriginal people.
The officials' report had proposed that future funding arrangements should be focused away from State programs to Aboriginal medical services. The proposal envisaged this being done at a predetermined rate or by reference to a set formula. The Government looked carefully at the proposal. We considered these issues when we were drawing up the new arrangements for general health care funding and administration. Conscious of the fact that many Aboriginals are dependent, to some extent, upon the States' general health care systems, we took the view that we should direct more effort into encouraging the States to involve Aboriginals in the planning and provision of all health services to their people. In the case of Aboriginal medical services, of course, this is already a settled matter.
Aboriginal Advisory Body The Commonwealth, for its part, in demonstrating that Aboriginal involvement is so central to Government policy and so crucial to improving Aboriginal health, will seek ways of ensuring that it received the best possible advice from Aboriginals in relation to Aboriginal health matters. We acknowledge the advice that has been provided

from time to time by the National Aboriginal and Islander Health Organisation, a federation of Aboriginal medical services. However, we also wish to have a more formal arrangement for advice on the whole range of health and health-related problems and needs, including medical needs. We shall therefore discuss, with the National Aboriginal Conference, arrangements to ensure that the Government receives appropriate and comprehensive advice about Aboriginal health, through the Conference. It would be fitting for the National Aboriginal Conference, as an allAboriginal elected body, charged with that responsibility of transmitting the views and advice of Aboriginals to the Government, to undertake this special task.
The parliamentary report recommended that a new unit be established in the Department of Aboriginal Affairs. It was proposed that a group of experts on middle level technology should be set up to advise on, amongst other things, the most practical means of providing communal and household facilities in Aboriginal communities. The Department of Aboriginal Affairs is essentially a policy co-ordination and funding body. It does not have specific functional expertise but rather draws on the resources of other departments or agencies when professional advice is needed. The same is true at the State and local levels. Where necessary, private consultants may be engaged. The Government considers, therefore, that it would not be appropriate or necessary for the proposed special group to be located within the Department of Aboriginal Affairs.
A second proposal was for an evaluation team to have responsibility for maintaining a check on the effectiveness of Aboriginal healtha care services and programs, and for the provision of standardised statistics on Aboriginal health. The Department of Aboriginal Affairs has in its central office structure an evaluation branch, which conducts evaluations as an entity distinct from the rest of the Department. That branch will be able to participate, as it has in the past, in evaluation of programs and projects, in close consultation with Aboriginal people. The Department of Aboriginal Affairs will continue to draw on resources already available, including those in other specialist departments as the need arises in undertaking evaluations against defined objectives. It will be seen, therefore, that the objective of the Standing Committee is capable of achievement under present arrangements.

HOUSE OF REPRESENTATIVES STANDING COMMITTEE ON ABORIGINAL AFFAIRS,
RBPORT;- ABORIGINAL HEALTH Government response to the Committee's Recommendations Canberra, May 1981

Committee on Aboriginal Affairs

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2553

HOUSE OF REPRESENTATIVES STANDING COMMITTEE ON ABORIGINAL AFFAIRS Report: 'Aboriginal Health'

Recommendations

Response/ comment

The Physical Environment1. 'The highest priority be given and immediate action
taken to provide clean and adequate water supplies to all Aboriginal communities.

Endorsed. In addition to the ongoing programs of the Department of Aboriginal Affairs which provide housing, water, sanitation and other community services, the Prime Minis-
ter announced in his election policy speech a commitment to provide an additional $50m over 5 years to meet highest priority needs for water supplies, sanitation and other environmental improvements.

2. 'A determined effort be undertaken to eliminate as soon as possible the unhygienic sanitary pan system in Aboriginal
communities. '

Endorsed. There is a need to provide sewerage systems where these do not exist, and to ensure that the system provided
matches the needs of the Aboriginal community.

3. 'An advisory group within the Department of Aboriginal
Affairs comprising experts in middle level technology be established, and in consultation with relevant departments and the Australian Institute of Aboriginal Studies to research vari-
ous options for the provision of community and household facilities; consult with Aboriginal communities to assess their needs; advise them on the most appropriate facilities that best
suit their needs; monitor implementation of its advice to communities; facilitate employment of technical advisors by Aboriginal communities; and report to the Minister for Aborigi-
nal Affairs.'

Professional advice on appropriate technology is available to
Aboriginal communities and organisations from relevant Government departments and, where appropriate, private consultants. As far as possible, these arrangements are decentralised, to ensure their responsiveness to communi-
ties. The Department of Aboriginal Affairs does not itself provide professional expertise and would therefore be an inappropriate location for an advisory body on technology.

4. 'Priority for Aboriginals living in towns and moving into towns away from the fringes, be given to the provision of funds for meeting and upgrading their housing needs.'

Housing accommodation or shelter is a high priority for Aboriginals, not only for those moving into or living in towns.
Existing policy affirms the importance of Aboriginal involvement in decisions regarding the number, location, design and arrangement of housing projects. Priority is cur-
rently given to the provision of housing for Aboriginals living on reserves.

5. 'The Department of Aboriginal Affairs consult relevant
Commonwealth and State departments, local authorities and Aboriginal communities and organisations to define responsibility for safe water, public hygiene services, housing stan-
dards and inspection of premises in Aboriginal settlements
and communities, particularly fringe camps.'

Endorsed. Such consultations will be part of developing a program for the expenditure of the additional funds com-
mitted by the Commonwealth for improvement of the physical environment of Aboriginal communities, as referred to in the comment in recommendation I above.

Cultural Factors6. 'Aboriginal cultural beliefs and practices which affect
their health and their use of health services such as their fear of hospitalisation, their attitudes to pain and surgery, the role
of traditional healers and the differing needs and roles of Aboriginal men and women be fully taken into account in the de-
sign and implementation of health care programs.'

Endorsed. Current policy supports the adaptation of services
and programs to meet adequately the cultural beliefs and lifestyles of Aboriginals. The Government believes that the Standing Committee has correctly identified the major cul-
tural factors affecting Aboriginal health. These will be specifically drawn to the notice of States and the Territory in the course of discussions on Aboriginal health program funding arrangements.

On Health Care Programs-
7. 'Savings to the State-funded health services which result from the Commonwealth-funded State Aboriginal preventive health programs be directed to the further development of
preventive programs.'

The Committee's concern that State authorities should accept
some financial responsibility for improving the health of Aboriginals is endorsed. The States have already been informed that the Commonwealth expects State Govern-
ments to provide at least a pro-rata share of general revenues for Aboriginals in their own programs and those supported by the Commonwealth, and to apply the 'priority of need' principle in all such programs. In the negotiations referred to in comments on recommendations 5 and 6, the
matter of joint Commonwealth/State funding will be thoroughly discussed.

2554 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Committee on Aboriginal Affairs

Recommendations

Response / comment

8. 'An independent evaluation team responsible to the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs be established to evaluate the effectiveness of all Aboriginal health care services and programs in accordance with the World Health Organisation's definition of health and the principles of self determination, and to establish suitable criteria so that standardised information can be collated and that funds be provided for the purpose where programs are funded by the Government. '
9. 'The full range of choice of the various types of programs delivering health care to Aboriginals be maintained and, where appropriate, support increased.'
10. 'Aboriginal communities be given the opportunity to determine the type of health service that will best suit their needs and available resources and that a Task Force be established to place the full range of alternative health care services before them.'
11. 'An inquiry be held into the implementation of the policy of self-determination as it affects community development. '
12. 'Aboriginals be involved to the fullest possible extent in all stages of the provision of health care services and that the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs assess the number of Aboriginals required, the time it will take to train them to assume responsibility for the health of their own people and, to this end, develop, in consultation with relevant Ministers, suitable training programs.'

It is agreed that there is a need to standardise criteria for evaluation and to collate information. The Government supports reviews and assessments of Aboriginal health programs, and expects that their conclusions will be used in the modification of existing services and the development of new Aboriginal health services.
The idea of conducting evaluations against the World Health Organisation's definition of health and the principle of selfmanagement is acceptable to the Government as a broad principle. The Government has reservations, however, about the usefulness of evaluations which are conducted without more specific terms of reference or criteria. These only provide evaluation criteria that are very broad and di fficult to relate to the very large number of projects which may comprise 'all Aboriginal health care services and programs'.
A structure already exists within the Department of Aboriginal Afrairs to develop and co-ordinate assessments and program reviews. The Department has within its Central Office an Evaluation Branch which conducts evaluations as an entity distinct from the rest of the Department. The Committee's objective-the establishment of an independent evaluation team-is, therefore, capable of achievement under present arrangements. Aboriginals are to be involved in any evaluation.
Endorsed. The degree of increased support will of course depend on available resources and on competing priorities in Aboriginal programs generally.
The Government is committed to expansion of Aboriginals' involvement in all aspects of health services affecting them, including in the design, control, management, delivery and evaluation of health services for Aboriginal people. The Government supports the development or expansion of Aboriginal responsibility in relation to State Aboriginal health programs. The recent initiatives of the South Australian and New South Wales Governments in these aspects are examples. In the context of these developments, there is no need for a 'Task Force' approach. Nor does the Government see a centralised body as necessarily the best means of ensuring what the Standing Committee has in mind.
Not endorsed. The Government's policies aim at Aboriginal self-management and self-sufficiency. The Department of Aboriginal Affairs assesses, on a continual review basis, the impact of the Government's policies and programs in Aboriginal affairs on community development.
The principle that Aboriginals should be involved to the fullest possible extent ,in the provision of health care services for them is strongly supported. The Government also supports the provision of suitable training, whether academic or on-the-job, to ensure the greatest possible Aboriginal participation in health care for Aboriginal people. The Commonwealth has funded training programs for Aboriginal health workers in State Health authorities and stands ready to discuss new initiatives.
The Government is particularly concerned to see the development of Aboriginal health professionals-doctors, dentists, nurses and para-professionals. Special measures may be needed to encourage Aboriginal students to go on to such training and Commonwealth Departments will take what steps appear appropriate.
Beyond the measures needed to train and employ suitably qualified Aboriginal people as health professionals, there is also a need to employ Aboriginal people in administrative and clerical positions, at all levels in Commonwealth and State authorities which have responsibilities in relation to Aboriginal health. The Commonwealth Government has taken several steps to ensure these objectives are met and will commend a similar approach to the States.

Committee on Aboriginal Affairs

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2555

Recommendations
13. 'Training hospitals for nurses, university medical faculties and other tertiary institutions introduce into their curricula, both at undergraduate and graduate level, a component which deals with Aboriginal health.'
14. 'Comprehensive orientation courses be conducted for all non-Aboriginal staff recruited to serve in Aboriginal communities before commencing duty, that they receive regular inservice training, and that a formal diploma or certificate be provided by a tertiary institution for professionals actively involved in providing health care to Aboriginals.'
15. 'Governments introduce special allowances and entitlements which recognise the unusual working conditions and the geographical, social and professional isolation experienced by personnel working in predominantly Aboriginal communities in remote areas.'

Response/comment
Endorsed. Whilst some hospitals and medical faculties already provide courses or segments on Aboriginal health, there needs to be a concerted effort to ensure that Aboriginal health and related matters are adequately dealt with in medical training institutions. These proposals will be taken up and actively pursued with the Tertiary Education Commission and with appropriate medical learned societies.
Orientation and inservice training courses are already provided by most State and some Aboriginal organizations, but there is a need for more comprehensive and more regular training courses for non-Aboriginal staff. The proposal for a formal diploma or certificate course will be pursued in the discussions envisaged in relation to Recommendation 13.
Health personnel already receive the general standard allowances paid in recognition of isolation and residence in a tropical climate, but they do not receive, in their conditions of service, acknowledgement of the special difficulties they experience. Consultation with the States, Northern Territory and non-government organisations will be necessary.

Mr MaeKELLAR-I present the following paper:
Report of Standing Committee on Aboriginal Affairs: Government Response, Ministerial Statement, 26 May 1981.
Motion (by Mr MeVeigh) proposed:
