That so much of the Standing Orders be suspended as would prevent the honourable member for Melbourne Ports speaking for a period not exceeding 28 minutes.
Mr HOLDING (Melbourne Ports) (5.50)-It has taken the Government over two years to respond to the recommendations of the House of Representatives Standing Committee on Aboriginal Affairs contained in the report on Aboriginal health which was tabled in this Parliament on 20 March 1979. It is interesting to note that the reference was given to the Committee by the then Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, Senator Chaney. As the report was directed to that Minister, I would have expected that a reply to the House would have been forthcoming from him. It is interesting to note that one of the implications that flows from the statement by the Government is that it now clearly sees problems of Aboriginal health not so much as the responsibility of the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs (Senator Peter Baume) but rather the responsibility of the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar). That is not a view shared by the Opposition. For that reason, I am the principal spokesman for the Opposition on this matter rather than the shadow Minister for Health, the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett).
In the course of events following the tabling of the House of Representatives Committee report, two other reports have been made. A trachoma

report was presented on 1 April 1980 and in late 1979 the Government established its own Program Effectiveness Review Task Force to report on Aboriginal health. The Government has been sitting on the recommendations contained in the effectiveness review report since early last year. I believe that it says something about this Government's view of open government that because the program effectiveness review recommendations do not comply with the Government's predetermined attitudes under Fraser federalism, the document is not before the Parliament today. It is really extraordinary to contemplate that the Government will take a House of
Representatives committee report containing a large number of recommendations and refer it for review. There is nothing wrong with that. A great deal of work was done by the effectiveness review committee but having regard to the importance of this matter, on what basis is it the case that the conclusions of that review committee are not directly available to the parties and not even available to the members of the House of Representatives Committee that produced the report?
I wrote to the Minister seeking the comments of the review committee. They were not available to me, as they were not available, I suspect, to any other member of the Committee. In order to get my copy, I had to obtain it, as 1 suspect other members did, through a Government department leak. Why should a government which is genuinely concerned about Aboriginal health want to take that kind of secret attitude towards a further report which could only widen the nature of the debate, only make more information available to the House and only lead to put other recommendations which ought to be taken into account by the House in its concern to reach conclusions on this very important matter. For many Aboriginals

2556 REPRESENTATlVES 26 May 1981

Committee on Aboriginal Affairs

in the c;ommunity, this is not just a matter of Commonwealth-State relations or of supporting or opposing this Government. For many Aboriginals the kinds of recommendations in all the reports are very much a matter of life or death.
I believe the reason that the House of Representatives has to debate the Ministerial statement today without the benefit of the recommendations of the Program Effectiveness Review Committee is that the recommendations of the Minister involve a very considerable and important shift in the nature of traditional Commonwealth responsibility towards Aboriginal citizens. The House of Representatives committee in its recommendations saw Aboriginal health programs, their innovation and support as being a primary responsibility of the Commonwealth. Of course, in the report comments were made about State and local government. If one looks at the full thrust of those recommendations, one will see that at no stage did any member of that Committee consider that both innovation and support, were not prime responsibilities of the Commonwealth. That is as it ought to be. That stems from the result of the 1967 referendum and from the view put very succinctly, I believe, to the Senate Standing Committee on Constitutional and Legal Affairs by the Department of Aboriginal Affairs. Having regard to the fact that this submission was put to the Standing Committee on Constitutional and Legal Affairs by the Department of Aboriginal Affairs one would be entitled to believe that that view also constituted the Government's view. I seek leave to incorporate that submission in Hansard.
Leave granted.
The document read as follows-
DEPARTMENT OF ABORIGINAL AFFAIRS
Submission to Senate Standing Committee on Constitutional and Legal Affairs
Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islanders [Queensland Reserves
and Communities Self-management1Act
(a) whether the constitutional conception underlying the legislation and the principle and structure of the legislation are consistent with the proper responsibilities of the Commonwealth;
As a result of the referendum held in 1967, the Australian Constitution (section Sl[xxvi]) now provides that the Parliament of the Commonwealth may pass laws 'in respect of people of any race for whom it is deemed necessary to make laws'. The States have concurrent power to legislate in this area but by virtue of section 109, Commonwealth legislation prevails over any inconsistent State legislation.
The Commonwealth Government's policy in respect of Aboriginal affairs calls for the creation of opportunities by which the Aboriginal community may exercise control over its own affairs. In pursuit of this policy, the Department of Aboriginal Affairs has been encouraging the development of autonomous local Aboriginal community organisations which receive grant

funds to enable them to carry out programs which will express and enhance their independence. Such assistance is a positive response to Aboriginal aspirations and without it the Aboriginal community and its individual members would remain dependent upon Government assistance indefinitely and have no opportunity to redevelop the necessary self-confidence to enable them to take their place as equals within the Australian community.
Mr HOLDING-Certainly that view of constitutional responsibility is, as I have already said, the view of the Opposition. Having regard to the importance of this matter, I also seek leave to have incorporated in Hansard a letter of concern written on 8 Apri11981 by the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) to the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) expressing his concern about Aboriginal health.

The letter read as follows-
8 April 1981
The Rt Hon. J. M. Fraser, CH, MP Prime Minister, Parliament House, Canberra, ACT 2600
My dear Prime Minister,
You will recall establishing the Program Effectiveness Review Task Force to examine various reports on Aboriginal health and health services.
Recent representations made to the Opposition have demonstrated that the Government has not met its promised commitments to Aboriginal health services, especially in respect of community-controlled, self-managed services, which are completely in accordance with the Government's stated policy of 'self-determination' for the Aboriginal people of Australia.
It is apparent that, due to the Government's 'New Federalism' policy and a desire to sharply reduce Commonwealth spending that the Commonwealth's responsibilities (following the 1967 Referendum) are in danger of being abrogated in favour of the States.
I now seek your unqualified assurance that it is not . your Government's intention to shift responsibility for Aboriginal health funding wholly or partially to the States.
I seek this assurance in view of two recent developments, namely:
1. Strong pressures on Aboriginal health services to accept Health Program Grants (RP.G.) from the Commonwealth Department of Health Community Health Division (in lieu of continued funding from the Department of Aboriginal Affairs); and
2. The establishment of the Aboriginal Development Commission (which of course, has been given responsibility for Aboriginal enterprises run for profit, but has no charter to provide funds for non-profi~ public services).

Committee on Aboriginal Affairs

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2557

My attention has been drawn to the letter from the Director General of Health to Aboriginal Health services (dated 17 October 1978, ref78j11247) which stated quite c1earlythat:
1. the acceptance of HPG's would prevent the continuance of bulk billing by doctors employed by these services;
2. Aboriginal Medical Services would be given the option of accepting either bulk billing or HPG's; and
3. there would be no compulsion on any service to accept HPG's.
The Government is apparently still using the numerous reports on Aboriginal health which it has received (,Aboriginal Health', House of Representatives Standing Committee on Aboriginal Affairs, 20 March 1979; National Trachoma and Eye Health Program Report, 1 April 1980; and the PER Report) to postpone adequate funding to existing services and new initiatives by Aboriginal communities in the area of health care delivery.
Recent and current Commonwealth funding has been provided solely for primary (clinical) health care and education. This has meant that any service wanting to provide preventive health care programs has been forced to rely on private donations from individuals and local and international aid agencies.
This is in direct contravention of the WHOjUNICEF 1978 Alma-Ata Declaration which, inter alia, recommended that:
'All governments should formulate national policies, strategies and plans of action to launch and sustain primary health care as part of a comprehensive national health system and in co-ordination with other sectors.'
The Alma-Ata Declaration also clearly defined the relationship which should exist between primary, preventive and other forms of health care delivery.
The other assurances which I also seek are:
1. The continued commitment of Commonwealth funding, via the Department of Aboriginal Affairs, to community-controlled Aboriginal health services; and
2. The continuation of current bulk billing arrangements for those services who choose to use them, without prejudice to their Grant-in-Aid from DAA.
Yours faithfully, BILL HAYDEN, M.P.
Mr HOLDING-The letter simply emphasises the concern of the Leader of the Opposition on behalf of the Opposition and emphasises the importance of the Commonwealth, through its constitutional and fiscal responsibilities, seeing that self-managed Aboriginal health programs receive an appropriate level of funding. I believe that the shameful aspect of the Minister's statement to the Parliament today is that whilst recognising the unsatisfactory health status of Aborigines, the urgent need to improve the environmental conditions affecting Aboriginal health and the importance of increasing Aboriginal involvement in the control and the delivery of health care services, at every point the Commonwealth's own constitutional responsibility and moral obligations to our Aboriginal people are demeaned and those responsibilities and duties are subjugated to an appropriate State area. To put it more bluntly, what this Government is about is turning Aboriginal health care initiatives back to the States even

though the Minister and the Prime Minister know that at least two States are on public record as perceiving self-managed Aboriginal health services-to use their own term-as a 'form of apartheid'. It is interesting to note the kind oflogical slide contained in the Minister's own statement. Whilst supporting the concept as stated on page 8 of his statement, he described those very services as 'rather than segregated services'. That is the kind of logical slide between the view of some States such as Western Australia and Queensland which seek self-managed Aboriginal services as a form of apartheid and the kind of term used in the Minister's statement. When comparing the range of services, he gives support to a generality of such services but uses the very interesting phrase 'rather than segregated services', in regard to self-managed Aboriginal services. More than two years ago the House of Representatives Standing Committee on Aboriginal Affairs reported:
'The level of Aboriginal ill-health will only be reduced if there are dramatic improvements in the physical environment, if there is maximum participation by Aborigines in all stages of the planning and delivering ofhealth care'.
One year later the National Trachoma and Eye Health Program came to much the same conclusion, after practical experience in the field throughout Australia.
The Program Effectiveness Review Task Force reached the same conclusion. Therefore there have been three reports, two of them public and one wrapped in a cloak of secrecy, which all reached the same conclusion.
Sitting suspended from 6 to 8 p.m.
Mr HOLDING-Prior to the suspension of the sitting, I was making the point that the ministerial statement on Aboriginal health involved the diminution of Commonwealth initiative and a shift in emphasis towards the States. It also involved a shift in departmental control from the Department of Aboriginal Affairs to the Department of Health. In order to centralise the Opposition's concern on both these points, I wish to move an amendment to the motion 'That the House take note of the paper'. I move:
That the following words be added to the motion:
'and that this House reaffirms(1) the continuing constitutional responsibility of the Commonwealth to fund, and give continuing support to, the raising of the health standards of its Aboriginal people, and (2) the continued commitment of Commonwealth funding, via the Department of Aboriginal Affairs, to community controlled Aboriginal health services.
I believe that amendment will give the House an opportunity to express itself in terms of the real

2558 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Committee on Aboriginal Affairs

issues that we believe arise from this ministerial statement.
The underlying theme of the statement-it runs throughout it-presupposes that there is complete accord between the States and the Commonwealth on the objective for administering health services for Aborigines. That is not the fact. I can do no better in order to give a graphic illustration of the facts than to relate the history of Professor Hollows and the work that he did in the whole trachoma area. Quite apart from the events of the last few days, I am certain all members of this House, as well as the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, would acknowledge the very real task done on behalf of the people of Australia by Professor Hollows in dealing with the problems of trachoma amongst our Aboriginal people.
By the time that program was over, the team had seen more than 100,000 people, some of them several times. More than 15,000 Aborigines had participated in month long treatment programs and more than 1,000 people had had eye operations, some performed in mobile hospitals erected by the Army. Eight million pieces of information had been collected and a computer analysis of the results demonstrated conclusively the environmental base of the disease. As Professor Hollows pointed out in his report, Aborigines were living outside the reach of present health care delivery systems, even where such systems were present. He often saw million dollar air conditioned hospitals and clinics in the middle of the squalor of the Aboriginal camps. Professor Hollows has said that Aborigines were still alien and remote from those services.
Professor Hollows' report, which was prepared for the Commonwealth Government, has been with the Government since April 1980. Since then the Department of Health has been trying to negotiate with the States the sort of continuing program that he called for. As the Minister for Health and the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs would know, the States have not complied. They have not been prepared to co-operate and they have made it clear that, even where they accept the findings of Professor Hollows, they want to run the programs themselves, irrespective of the international repute and capacity of Professor Hollows and his team. Professor Hollows has stated that the States want to do more of the same; they want to carry out programs which do not work and which will not work, and to freeze out the vital ingredient in any health care program that is concerned to uplift the standards of our Aboriginal people, that is, the critical question of Aboriginal participation. That was his

experience and none of those argumentscompelling arguments they were, extracted in the field by a noted Australian authority-has been dealt with or even referred to in the ministerial statement.
What does the Government's own bureaucracy say about Commonwealth-State attitudes to Aboriginal health? The program effectiveness review-whose report should have been in the hands of every parliamentarian in this Housewas an internal review of the issues raised by both Professor Hollows and a parliamentary committee and referred to the critical question of Commonwealth-State co-operation in raising health standards. I wish to quote from this confidential document. It is no wonder that the Minister was not prepared to make it public. It shows the paucity, the mendacity and the nonsense which is involved in the ministerial statement. At page 14 of the review, carried out by departmental officers at the request of the Prime Minister, it is stated:
The States have adopted a different stance about Commonwealth responsibilities, based on their interpretation of the agreements-that the Commonwealth is predominantly responsible for financing Aboriginal health programs, that the States are responsible for health policy and planning and for the co-ordination of health services, and that the States' general responsibility for health should not be abrogated by the Commonwealth's Aboriginal health policies.
It is not clear how far the State authorities subscribe to the principle of Aboriginal self-management. Insofar as the principle may apply to States Grants programs, the States are adamant that policy, planning and management of health care must be undertaken by properly qualified personnel, and this tends to exclude Aboriginals because of their lack of training and particularly of formal qualifications.
So, the Government's own internal bureaucracy agrees with the very conclusions that Professor Hollows made in the field. It has summarised those conclusions on page 15 of this confidential report.
If members of Parliament want to get their hands on it and read it, they have to go cap in hand to the Press Gallery and say: 'Please, the Prime Minister regards this report as so confidential and so damning of his policies that we have to get it as if it had fallen off the back of a truck'. This policy document goes on to say:
The PER saw three main areas of policy difference existing between the Commonwealth and the States:
appropriate division of financial responsibility and the role of supplementary funding;
co-ordination of policy and planning; and the emphasis to be given to Aboriginal self-management and the level at which Aboriginals should be involved effectively.
This report complemented and supplemented the report of the parliamentary committee; but, more

Committee on Aboriginal Affairs

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2559

important than that, it came out with very specific proposals. It recommended:
Effective Aboriginal involvement in developing and implementing health programs be a key factor. This would entail setting up a national Aboriginal health body responsible to the ministers for health and aboriginal affairs.
Where is that to be found in the ministerial statement? It continues:
That States be granted less funds and the Aboriginal run medical services be given much more.
It could not be any more specific than that recommendation that the States be given less funds and Aboriginal conducted services be given more. But, that is not the principle we find in this ministerial statement. Further proposals are:
That Aborigines be represented on hospital boards in areas with substantial Aboriginal population.
That more Aborigines be employed in training in the Aboriginal health field.
It goes on to say:
More than $62m is spent on Aboriginal health in general State programs, including hospitals, but the report--
This is the report of the Government's own advisers-
claims that this is 'not effective'.
It is not just a question of the proposals outlined by the Minister today being bad health policy. It is also a question of its being bad medicine. That is the reason this report has been buried and is not available directly to the Parliament so that it can form a consensus on it, so that it can go forward and deal with this critical problem, this matter of life and death which reaches right into every Aboriginal community in Australia.
The Government's own program effectiveness review has made it perfectly clear that the health status of Aborigines is still bad. The effectiveness and perpetuation of many current State-run programs is clearly open to doubt. It suggests that the Commonwealth, by funding the States in this way, is throwing good money after bad. It is interesting to note that the Minister, at page 11 of his statement, gives what he regards as the new model which the Government believes ought to be adopted. He said:

and substantial statement which refers to effective Aboriginal health care models, the Minister said that the new structure operating in Darwin was an excellent working model, when the Aboriginal community was not effectively involved in the decision to establish it. Not once in this very substantial statement did the Minister indicate as an effective working model-I name only one of them-the health service conducted by the Central Australian Aboriginal Congress in Darwin. It treats 30,000 Aboriginal patients a year at a cost of $12.50 per patient. The spokesman for the Northern Territory Administration, Mr Tuxworth, said that the cost to the Territory in a Territory-run operation is $40 per out-patient service. Should not this Minister say that perhaps we ought to be going for the service that seems to be working, which has the confidence of the Aboriginal people and which, most importantly, can be shown by the Aboriginal people to be run with cost savings and more effectively than the glitter and show of large structures which often are built to perpetuate the view of the prevailing State bureaucrat?
I believe those are the issues. In considering this ministerial statement, not the least of the points we have to make is that the Minister has said: 'We should hand it over to the States. We will work it out together. It will be all right.' It might have been all right 12 months ago, but if there is any area in present Commonwealth-State relations where there is not merely chaos but confrontation on almost a daily basis it is health care. The Minister for Health from my own State, a man whose political philosophy is the same as that of this Minister, said only today that if Commonwealth funding continues at the present level Victoria will have to cut back on its health services. Is the Minister for Health serious when he brings in a proposal and says to the Minister for Health in Victoria: 'Despite the fact that you will have to cut back on services, we have an entirely new policy for the treatment of Aborigines which will cost you more than you are paying at the moment'? Have honourable members ever heard such palpable nonsense?

The Aboriginal and Islander Medical Service in Darwin, which is funded jointly by the Commonwealth and Northern Territory governments and works in closely with existing health services, is a good example of what can be achieved through co-operation. The Government will be prepared to consider provision of assistance to similarly innovative approaches elsewhere.
I do not propose in the short time available to me to be overtly critical of that service. I simply say this: The Aboriginal community was not consulted on the establishment of that service. I also point out that it is odd that in this very lengthy

It is not necessary that Government members support this statement. Perhaps it is not so important that the Parliament support it. TRADING BANK MERGERS Discussion of Matter of Public Importance Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-Mr Speaker has received a letter from the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The Government's apparent intention to approve proposed mergers of major trading banks regardless of the serious national implications of such mergers.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (8.20)-ln the last three weeks we have witnessed the greatest attempt at restructuring our financial institutions for many years. That restructuring has involved merger negotiations by all the major private trading banks with the result that at this stage two mergers are now formally proposed. Those mergers are the Bank of New South Wales with the Commercial Bank of Australia and the National Bank of Australasia Ltd with the Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney Ltd. These mergers have enormous implications for Australia. They are the largest takeovers in our history. The amount involved in the Bank of New South Wales and CBA deal is $700m and the amount involved in the National Bank takeover of the CBC is $450m. For that reason the Opposition believes it is important that the matter be discussed in this Parliament.

TARIFF PROPOSALS Mr MOORE (Ryan-Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs) (8.27)-1 move:

Leave granted; debate adjourned.

Customs Tariff Proposals No.7 (1981).

BILLS RETURNED FROM THE SENATE The following Bills were returned from the
Senate without amendment or requests:
Excise Tariff AmendmentBi111981. Pig Slaughter Amendment Levy Bill 1981.
ASSENT TO BILLS Assent to the following Bills reported:
Customs Amendment (Tenders) Bill 1981. Customs Securities (Penalties) Bill 1981. Customs Undertakings (Penalties) Bill 1981.

The Customs Tariff Proposals 1 have just tabled relate to proposed alterations to the Customs Tariff Act 1966. Proposals No.7 (1981), which operates on and from tomorrow, contains tariff changes resulting from the Government's decision on recommendations by the Industries Assistance Commission in its report on wheelchairs. The Government has accepted the Commission's recommendations that the duty rates applicable to wheelchairs and parts will be reduced to 20 per cent over the next two years. This rate represents a duty reduction of 14 per cent for mechanically propelled and 16 per cent for manually operated

Garden Island Modernisation Stage 2

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2561

wheelchairs. Before reaching this decision the Government gave careful consideration to the need to strike a balance between reducing costs for wheelchair users and providing a level and form of assistance appropriate to the existence of a viable local industry.
I draw the attention of honourable members to the Government's announcement in the 1980-81 Budget of a new program under which a range of aids to daily living will be provided to disabled people. This program is intended to benefit disabled people not eligible under existing Commonwealth, Commonwealth-State or State funded programs. A range of aids including conventional wheelchairs, manual and motorised, will be available on indefinite loan under the program without cost to the recipients. The necessary administrative arrangements for this program are currently being developed under the authority of my colleague the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) with a view to the program commencing later this year.
The proposals also contain changes which remove a tariff anomaly relating to raised nap cotton fabric known as flannelette. A new definition allows concessional entry of only genuine flannelette in accordance with a Government decision taken last year following the Industries Assistance Commission report on textiles, clothing and footwear. The new definition will ensure that cotton fabric with minimally raised nap falls within the quota system, as intended; but to avoid disruption to the industry making up bed linen, importers of raised nap sheeting which does not meet the flannelette definition will be issued with supplementary quota for the remainder of the quota period to 31 December 1981. A summary setting out the nature of the changes has been prepared and is being circulated to honourable members. I commend the Proposals to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Hurford) adjourned.

GARDEN ISLAND, NEW SOUTH WALES, MODERNISATION STAGE 2
Reference ofWork: Public Works Committee Act
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction) (8.28)-1 move:

That, in accordance with the provisions of the Public Works Committee Act 1969, the following proposed work be referred to the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works for consideration and report: Garden Island, N.S.W.- modernisation stage 2.
This proposal is for the second stage of an overall modernisation program which is aimed at improving the effectiveness and efficiency of the dockyard and the fleet base, providing each with a capability of operating independently without duplication, improving the aesthetic qualities of Garden Island while minimising undesirable effects on the environment and preserving historic buildings. Advance works and stage 1 modernisation works at Garden Island have already been reported on favourably by the Public Works Committee and approved by Parliament. The stage 2 works will comprise: Woolloomooloo fleet berth wharves; west dock workshop modules B and C; guided missile launching system overhaul facility; conversion of building 30 to a ready-use store; conversion of buildings 89 and 90 to an office complex; production administrative centre and dock refit berth support facility; surface preparation and preservation facility and oily bilge waste treatment plant; groundworks, and reticulated engineering services. The estimated cost of the proposed works is $79m at April 1981 prices. I table plans of the proposed work.
MI' HURFORD (Adelaide) (8.29)-The Opposition supports the reference of the second stage of modernisation program for Garden Island to the Joint Parliamentary Committee on Public Works. We have always supported the examination by that Committee of the Parliament of propositions such as this which come from the Government. We trust that the work of the Public Works Committee will be facilitated by the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) and his Department so that the report can come back to this Parliament and an early decision may be taken by the Government.

Mr CUNNINGHAM (McMillan) (8.31)-ln continuing my remarks on the Fish (Export Inspection Charge) Bill and the Dried Fruit (Export Inspection Charge) Collection Bill I reiterate what I believe to be the Government's reason for imposing these charges. This Government is intent on raising funds to add to its revenue or on cutting expenditure to achieve the same aim. That is what this Bill sets out to do. For the last four months I have sat in this House and listened to ideological rhetoric which could lead one to believe that the Government is about lowering taxes and charges. Nothing could be further from the truth. This measure, although only dealing in small amounts of money, is another measure designed to collect funds from two industries which can ill afford to pay the extra charges.
The Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) said that the purpose of the Bills is to have the fish and dried fruit industries pay half the costs of the services provided. The Bills set out the maximum amounts which can be charged. The previous speaker on the Government side, the honourable member for O'Connor (Mr Tuckey), outlined the clauses of the Bills relating to these maximum charges. I will repeat them for the benefit of those who are listening at present. He said:
Clauses 6 (3) and (4)-
This is the Fish (Export Inspection Charge) Bill-
give details of the maximum charges allowed under this Bill. They are 15c a dozen for oysters and 30c per kilogram for fish other than oysters. In the dried fruit levy Bill, clause 6 (2) also allows for differential treatment and clause 6 (3) sets the maximum charge under the proposed Act at $11 a tonne. However, fish and dried fruit producers should not be alarmed by the amounts referred to as these are truly the maximum charges.
Fruit growers and fishermen would not want their livelihood to depend on this Government keeping any commitments. When the Government says at this stage that the charges are only 50 per cent it will be just a matter of time before we see a change. The honourable member for O'Connor also said that the maximum charges are introduced in line with the present policy and give a sensible life to the Bill. We could ask what he means by the words 'a sensible life to the Bill'. Virtually that means that any time in the near future when the Government sees fit, it can take necessary measures to collect the other 50 per cent thus doubling the charge in a very short time. Those are the policies to which he is referring. At this stage the charge is only 50 per cent of the services provided. No doubt, along with most of the other commitments made by this Government it will

At this stage I would like to refer to a few other points concerning the fishing industry. Recently, the Australian Fishing Industry Council and the Victorian Fishing Industry Council were formed. The fishing industry has as much potential for development as any Australian food industry. The December 1980 edition of the official Commonwealth journal Australian Fisheries shows the value of fisheries production at an estimated $28lm. Exports of Australian fisheries products to the year ended 30 June 1980 were worth a record $256m. It can be seen that this industry is worth a lot to the export income of this country. However, the journal states:
Because of rising costs, particularly fuel costs, most fishermen were probably worse off in 1979-80 than in the previous year.
This applies particularly to the many fishermen catching the high value species such as prawns and rock lobsters where neither catches nor the pace of price increases matched those of the previous year. So from the 1980 edition of the Australian Fisheries we see that there is not a great deal of room in the industry for the Government to be imposing extra charges at this time, particularly when we are constantly told by this Government that it is reducing taxes and charges. The very facts point to the opposite happening.
In applying these charges to the industry the Government is adding to the already large increases in fees which at least one State government imposed at the end of 1980. I refer to the Victorian Government which increased fees for abalone divers and scallop fishermen by up to 100 per cent. Not only is the industry copping it from the Federal Liberal Government but also it is copping it from the Victorian Liberal Government. The fishing industry should be encouraged to expand. Whilst we are exporting fish to the value of $256m, we are importing fish to the value of $150m. The Government would be better advised to concentrate on fostering the industry as it has great growth potential which should be assisted by decreasing costs rather than unnecessarily increasing them.
I do not want to take up a lot of the time of the House tonight. In the time that is available to me I just want to make a couple of comments. I find it rather alarming that, with only a couple of weeks of this parliamentary session left, we have 47 notices on the Notice Paper with many more to come in. Time will be vital. We will need it to discuss some of the more important issues. At this stage I would rather leave my remarks at what I have had to say and I urge the Government to

also recommended:

Customs Amendment Bill

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2567

The responsibility for three Nomad aircraft dedicated to coastal surveillance by the Bureau of Customs should be transferred to the Australian Coastal Surveillance Centre.
We do not disagree with that recommendation either. Going further at page B383 the Commission noted that it was unable to ascertain with any precision the role of these three Customs aircraft. It noted in particular that the speed of the Nomad is very low. Some people say that someone on a hang-glider can almost rush past a Nomad. In fact, its speed is somewhere in the vicinity of 150 to 200 knots, while the only established case of an aircraft being used to drop narcotics in Australia involved an aircraft with a much higher speed capacity-in excess of 200 knots. We all know that that plane was detected because the damn thing crashed. No one caught it; it crashed quite by accident in Central Australia. The Department told the Royal Commission that the planes were response vehicles, 'response' being the operative word, and I would like the Minister to explain what response means. The Royal Commission was not convinced, and probably was not very amused either. I note that, in answer to a question in Estimates Commitee D on 29 August 1980, the Department again referred to these planes as response planes.
There we have it. Firstly, planes are operated by an agency which the Royal Commission recommended should not operate them. Secondly, they operate in areas of Australia which the Royal Commission found should no longer be the focus of attention. Thirdly, they operate in roles which the Royal Commission could not ascertain. That is not a very splendid record of service and devotion, I might say to the Minister. Added to that, we now have a fourfold extension of the surveillance area, which clearly means that the capacity to provide surveillance with such minimal resources is totally inadequate.
I suppose that this is what small government is all about. I hope that more and more Australians will realise that when the penny-pinching philosophy called small government really surfaces, it is an unabashed abdication of government responsibility. In this area of surveillance of our coastline as a means of keeping narcotics out of Australia and out of the reach of Australian kids, I am sure that the Minister shares my concern that the resources available to him are not adequate to do the job. In some other areas the Opposition welcomes the legislative improvements in respect of the role of Customs. We hope-l fear in vainthat the Government will match these im-
t provements with an appropriate commitment of
resources. notice the Minister is smiling. I am sure that he agrees with me.

I turn briefly now to the second group of amendments provided in the Bill. These clarify the powers and procedures of the Customs Bureau and, again, the Opposition has no objection to them. One of the more important provisions in this group is that which makes principals liable for the acts of employees involved in the unauthorised movement of goods in a Customs area. An adequate defence is provided by the Bill to protect principals who take reasonable precautions and exercise due diligence in respect of such goods. The Opposition contacted the Customs Agents Federation regarding these provisions because obviously they have an important effect on the members of that organisation. 1 am happy to report that the Federation has no objection to them, but it should be noted that that is just as well because there appears to have been no consultation between the Federation and the Government. It seems to me singularly appropriate that, where legislation affects business in various ways, there should be consultation, but that seems not to be this Government's philosophy. It is trying to reduce consultation, and apparently it made no effort at all in this case. That is probably a failing of this Government in many other areas. Where changes are made which affect industries, the people who work in them and the people who run them, consultation with those industries should be mandatory before the decisions are made. You, Sir, might well remember the singular victory you had in this House in the case of the wine brandy growers and the certain consultation that was lacking in that area. I suggest to the Minister that he should not neglect this important need. If he does talk to the Customs Agents Federation he will find that its members do have problems with clearing goods through Customs. He should look at ways of improving this situation. I am sure that he will not find that so-called small government is the answer in this case either.
The third major series of amendments in this Bill relate to the powers of Customs to deal with illegally imported vessels, particularly refugee boats, which are condemned or otherwise unseaworthy. Problems arose with the Customs Act two or three years ago when several dozen refugee boats in Darwin Harbour presented a major disposal problem. I can well remember being in Darwin at that stage and seeing the harbour festooned with these odd craft which people had used to escape to Australia. Amending legislation passed last year with the support of the Opposition and these further provisions add to those new powers. Of course, the Opposition supports the provisions which give the Bureau of Customs the right to destroy those boats if necessary.

2568 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Customs Amendment Bill

Finally, the fourth major provision of the Bill is that to amend section 226 to extend the validity period of customs tariff proposals from six months to 12 months. The Excise Amendment Bill, which is also a subject of this cognate debate similarly provides for an extension of the validit; period for excise tariff proposals. Customs tariff and excise tariff proposals are of fundamentalimportance to the Australian economy. They have two fundamental roles. Firstly, they are taxes, and vitally important taxes at that. Many of the excise tariff proposals relate to the crude oil levy, which, of course, has been a massive tax slug for one thing, but which has also been a massive advantage to the Government in reducing the deficit. The Government has trumpeted loudly about its great efficiency in reducing the deficit. We of the Opposition and the Australian public know that the deficit has been reduced only by means of the levy which has come out of the pockets of the public. Total customs and excise taxation revenue estimated for the current year is $7,762m, nearly one-quarter of the Government's total tax revenue.
The second fundamental role of customs and excise tariffs is the implementation of industry policy. Tariffs, of course, are the principal means whereby Australian industries are protected from import competition. The jobs of hundreds of thousands of Australians are at stake every time a customs tariff amendment Bill is passed by this House. We believe that legislation of such fundamental importance to the taxation and industry policies of Australia should not be tampered with lightly. However, the Government is proposing to halve the frequency with which the principal legislation enacting changes in tariff rates is to be dealt'with and debated in this Parliament. Tariff and excise charges can already be implemented months in advance of parliamentary approval, but the Government wants to extend the period during which it can collect these taxes without legislative backing in the forms of customs or excise tariff amendments. The Opposition cannot agree to those provisions. We cannot accept that a government should reserve for itself an extended capacity to impose retrospective legislation to collect taxes while, at the same time, failing to act retrospectively against tax avoiders. This Government has been anything but backward in adopting a holier than' thou attitude when it comes to retrospective legislation with regard to tax avoidance. However, here we find the Government cooly, coldly and blandly implementing this provision in this House to impose taxes retrospectively.

We do not believe that the Parliament's role should be further downgraded in this way. We acknowledge, of course, that it is not possible to change tariffs by legislation enacted in advance of the date such changes come into effect, but there is no reason to extend beyond a full sitting of the Parliament the period before legislation is introduced. If income tax on government spending decisions were treated in such a cavalier fashion we would have a constitutional crisis. The Opposition will not agree to these changes dealing with one-quarter of the Government's revenue. In summary, the Opposition welcomes the improve-
ments in customs administration provided by this Bill in such areas as the clearance of goods, surveillance against illegal imports and dealing with illegally imported vessels. However, we oppose the Government's attempts to reduce further the role of this Parliament in scrutinising and determining excise and tariff rates in Australia. To assist the passage of this Bill and to assist the Government to clear the backlog of legislation, we will be doing our best not to hinder the House too much. We intend to oppose clause 28 of the Customs Amendment Bill in the Committee stage. We intend to oppose the Excise Amendment Bill in its entirety at the second reading stage. We will divide on each occasion.
Question resolved in the affirmative.

Bill read a second time.

CUSTOMS TARIFF (ANTI-DUMPING) AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 6 May, on motion by Mr Moore:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr MOORE (Ryan-Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs)-Mr Deputy Speaker, may I have your indulgence to suggest that the House has a general debate covering the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Bill and the Customs Amendment (Security) Bill as they are associated measures. Separate questions will, of course, be put on each Bill at the conclusion of the debate.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- There being no objection, that course will be followed.
Mr JOHN BROWN (Parramatta) (9.31)~The purpose of these Bills is to bring the Australian anti-dumping law into conformity with the revised codes of conduct for international trade agreed to at the Tokyo round of the Multilateral Trade Negotiations. It will not be any surprise to the Government to find that the Opposition supports these measures because Australia's first venture into anti-dumping legislation occurred in the time of the illustrious Whitlam Government. So we are not about to oppose the Government's sticking to what we originally introduced. The Bills amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 in accordance with the revised General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade code on anti-dumping, subsidies and countervailing measures. These two codes are among several considered at the Tokyo round in the context of non-tariff barriers to trade.
One of the two principal objectives of the Tokyo round was to secure a series of multilateral agreements on the conduct of international trade.
r think it is fair to say that the Tokyo round
appears to have been successful in respect of its other main objective which was to achieve a substantial reduction in tariffs applying to industrial products. The first instalment of these tariff reductions took effect on 1 January 1980. The major industrial nations reduced tariffs on industrial products. Over the eight years to 1988, tariffs are to be reduced by about one-third. I can only applaud that as being a major breakthrough. This success on the tariff front has moved the centre of attention towards non-tariff barriers to trade. It is

It should be recognised that reductions in nontariff barriers to trade are of vital importance not only to Australia but also to the economy of the entire world. Growth in trade has normally been associated with the growth in national incomes, increased productivity and an increased standard of living. Provided trade is conducted on a fair basis, it will almost always prove mutually beneficial to its participants. In this context the maintenance of Australia's anti-dumping law in accordance with internationally agreed principles is firmly supported by the Opposition. The purpose of the anti-dumping law is to ensure that trade is conducted on a fair and efficient basis. It is not a back door method for controlling imports or for erecting barriers to trade. However, we must always be aware of the fact that indiscriminate use of anti-dumping provisions invites retaliation against our own exports. There have been instances in recent history of this occurring, not necessarily in Australia. At the same time, indiscriminate use limits the effectiveness of our anti-dumping investigations because it prejudices cooperation with our trading partners.
The Opposition notes and welcomes the support expressed by a number of industry organisations for Australia's accession to the anti-dumping code. Of course, it is essential that this responsible attitude is taken if international agreement is to be maintained. I will refer later in my remarks to the subsidies and countervailing measures code to which Australia has not yet acceded. Overall, the new code probably makes it easier to prove a case of dumping against an exporter. The GATT code considers that a product is being dumped:
. . . if the export price of the product exported from one country to another is less than the comparable price, in the ordinary course of trade, for the like product when destined for consumption in the exporting country.
This is a very long-winded way of explaining the position. What is basically meant is that if country A wants to export to country B, then country B has the right to expect that the export price quoted by country A is in fact the price used for the sale of that commodity in country A. The code additionally requires that dumpings cause or threaten to cause injury before dumping action is appropriate to be undertaken. Clearly, the key concepts in the dumping law are export price, normal value and injury. It is the revised definition of these concepts which is at the heart of the new code and its amending legislation.
Clause 3 introduces new provisions which draw a distinction between export prices arising from

Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Bill

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2575

legislation that updated our anti-dumping legislation in line with the then existing GATT AntiDumping Code. Therefore, there is no reason in principle for us to oppose this Bill.
Given that Australia must be a trading partner of a great number of nations, the necessity for orderly marketing practices ought to be only too evident. As the trading parties are off-shore to Australia and, therefore, are not normally subject to the local laws governing their trading behaviour, it has been recognised for years that internationally binding rules are required if orderly and equitable trading relations are to obtain. That point needs to be made clear about the nature of this legislation. We are talking about fair and equitable trading relations. That could not happen unless all parties to GATT brought the anti-dumping legislation into line with the Code to which they were signatories. Obligations to do so were imposed upon all signatories to the GATT Code. As has been said, the practice of dumping is reprehensible. Whilst in the short term consumers in an importing country might benefit from dumping practices, the long term continuation of such practices will no doubt be to their disadvantage. The short term increase in the choice of goods and lower prices will be offset by the disruptive effects upon the economy of the victim country. In all cases, the competing domestic producers will be forced to reduce their prices and employment opportunities or accept lower profits.
Secondly, we ought to put this legislation through to ensure that Australian industries are given adequate protection from the dumping practices of either predatory trading nations or less scrupulous foreign business houses. Some of the business houses alluded to by the honourable member for Murray, who has disappeared from the chamber, are business houses that have a foot in both camps as it were and, therefore, are quite happy to see the situation continue because they are able to do the dumping under the lap. In principle, those members of the Government who have recently inveighed against industry protection in favour of free trade must feel rather uncomfortable with this piece of legislation. It must be stated that there is nothing intrinsically evil in government interference in the trade from one country to another. Some members of the Government would like to propagate the view that there is some evil in government oversight of industry. Without such oversight, as my colleague the honourable member for Parramatta has indicated, trading relations would become chaotic. I hope that as yet the Government has not entirely succumbed to the influence of the free

traders. For example, we reject as unfair the practice of dumping and believe that we require international agreement, such as the GATT code, to enable us to police this practice. If all things were equal it might have been possible to have free trade, but the great differences that exist between ourselves and our trading partners in so many ways will make free trade a disaster for Australian industry and, as a consequence, the welfare of Australian markets, workers and families.
As we know, dumping occurs when goods are exported at a price that is less than the normal value in the country of export. The normal value is generally defined as the price of like goods sold for home consumption in the exporting country in arm's length transactions by the exporter. The definition I have just quoted to the House enables us to establish a legal bar to more obvious injurious trading practices. Our international obligations require us to agree to legislate accordingly. It is for this reason that we of the Opposition support this legislation. However, as in most areas of human relationships, there is a tendency in some instances for exceptions to rules to intrude. This is a typical area. There are some practices which, whilst not necessarily fulfilling all the criteria for being called dumping, nevertheless need to be brought to the attention of the House because they do impose hardships upon sections of Australian industry. Again, the honourable member for Murray alluded to those. The differences between them and dumping are of degree only. I refer, of course, to imports of certain goods from New Zealand.
The House would be aware that in recent days the Deputy Prime Minister and Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) has been meeting with the New Zealand Prime Minister, Mr Muldoon, to negotiate a closer economic relations agreement between the two countries. Generally the relations between our two countries have been cordial throughout our history. It is to be hoped that this relationship will continue. However, I am unable to see how this will continue without some strain if the current negotiations result in opening the access to Australian markets of New Zealand products that will undercut the Australian equivalents. Strictly speaking, this does not fulfil all the criteria for a trading practice to be termed dumping, but the combination of factors will nevertheless result in the destruction of a number of very important primary industries in this country. Two major industries are of concern to me. I refer firstly to the potato industry and then, of course, to the fat lamb industry. Both of these industries affect the

2576 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Customs Tariff(Anti-Dumping) Bill

livelihood of many specialist farmers in my electorate of Ballarat.
Mr Ian Robinson~You don't know your subject.
Mr MILDREN~It is rather unfortunate that the honourable member for Cowper knows so little about the concerns of people such as those of Ballarat. At the moment, if no cognisance is to be taken of the particular circumstances of the potato industry, for example, imports of potatoes from New Zealand would undercut the price of Australian potatoes to such an extent that the industry would be crippled. Under Australian dumping law, dumping could be said to occur when injury caused by a trading practice is seen to cause material injury and it must be demonstrated to be so. For example, losses of sales, depressed prices or retrenchments must be due principally to imports at non-competitive prices, and the injury must be demonstrated by the major proportion oflocal industry.
Given the above, it is impossible for the injury not to occur should potatoes be fully able to be imported from New Zealand. Why is this so? Firstly, the real cost structure of each country is very different. The contract price of potatoes grown in New Zealand is the equivalent of $A42 per tonne. In Australia the contract price is $97.60 per tonne. The lower price of New Zealand potatoes is assisted by lower wage structures and government incentives. Secondly, the variations in currency values give the advantage to New Zealand potato growers. The depressed value of the New Zealand dollar makes it worth approximately 77c in Australian currency. The Australian dollar is a more buoyant currency and, therefore, movement tends to disadvantage Australian producers even more. Thirdly, the issue of government subsidies to potato producers further widens the gap between the possible price of potatoes imported into Australia from New Zealand and the minimum price Australian producers could obtain for their produce before suffering economic injury.
In the Ballarat potato growing region alone, the loss of such an industry would place an excessive burden upon the capacity of local industries to survive. The potato growing industry will not be the only one to suffer. There are over 300 producers throughout this area and approximately 1,200 people are directly involved in one way or another in the industry. In a direct sense, the industry is worth to that area approximately $14m, and it stimulates a further $15m in local business. I am sure that I do not have to convince you, Mr Deputy Speaker, of the hardship that a policy of

quasi dumping would have upon local potato producers. It should not be without interest to the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), in his capacity as the honourable member for Wannon, that there are many potato producers in his electorate who would also be so affected. It is very important for points such as these to be made. As I have said, the practices might not be in direct contravention of the anti-dumping law but the effects are the same. Perhaps it is because of the inadequacies of the anti-dumping law that we have.
Any dumping or quasi-dumping practices must have serious effects upon Australian producers whether they be primary producers such as potato growers or fat lamb producers, vegetable growers, processors, or the clothing, textile, footwear and engineering industries all of which are particularly vulnerable in this country. It is very important for the House to recognise that individuals, their families and the viability of towns and cities are endangered by dumping and quasi-dumping practices. Mr Deputy Speaker, I assure you that the economy of a city such as Ballarat would be rocked should the potato industry collapse as a result of the importing of potatoes produced in an economic climate different from that of Australia. Potato harvesting and packing machinery would be made redundant and so would the industries that produce that machinery. We cannot allow that sort of thing to happen. I do not have to remind the House that the multiplier effect would mean a reduction in the work force in the retail industry of rural and provincial towns. I hope that members on the National Country Party benches~for want of a more appropriate name~ would show some concern about this issue. I highlight the effects of a quasi-dumping practice to bring some notes of caution to those people hell bent on liberalising our marketing arrangements and also because dumping practices in general have all these effects which are evil.
Mr MOORE (Ryan~Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs) (J 0.13)~in reply~I wish to repond briefly to those honourable members who have taken part in the debate and to thank them for the effort that they have put into their speeches and the contributions that they have made. I wish to take issue with him on one point. The great era of dumping did not arrive with E. G. Whitlam at all. In fact, if one looks at

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2579 ANSWERS TO QUESTIONS

The following answers to questions were circulated:

National Crimes Commission
(Question No. 174)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 27 November 1980:
(1) Further to my question without notice on 11 October 1979 (Hansard, page 1905) urging the establishment of a National Crimes Commission, in view of the appalling escalation of organised crime in this country, has the Attorney General's attention been drawn to press reports of 6 February 1980 that the Australian Federal Police Force is studying a report compiled by a group of Federal and Victorian Members of Parliament on systematic penetration of organised crime?
(2) If so, does the report-
(a) call for the establishment by the Federal Government of a National Crimes Commission to identify the leadership and structure of organised crime in Australia; and
(b) suggest a number of significant amendments to the taxation, company and takeover laws, as these amendments may be the best way to deal with syndicate leaders of organised crime?
(3) If the report is as stated, will the Attorney-General take immediate steps to ensure that the suggested amendments to taxation, company and takeover laws will include measures to block syndicate leaders of organised crime?
(4) If the study finding confirms the appalling escalation of organised crime, will the Attorney-General give urgent reconsideration to my request to establish a National Crimes Commission?
Mr Viner-The Attorney-General has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) Yes.
(2)-
(a) I understand that the report calls for the establishment of a National Crimes Commission.
(b) I understand the report suggests that organised crime can best be dealt with through taxation, company and takeover laws.
(3) The matters referred to do not fall within my administration.
(4) The Government does not propose to establish a National Crimes Commission but rather to tackle identified problems in the area of organised crime with selective investigative machinery involving the State Governments as appropriate. For example, during the past couple of years, in response to suggestions made by the Williams Royal Commission of Inquiry into Drugs and the Woodward Royal Commission into Drug Trafficking, the Australian Federal Police have joined with the police in several States to investigate specific areas of drug trafficking. Also, last year the Commonwealth joined with the Victorian Government in the establishment of a Royal Commission of Inquiry into the Federated Ships Painters and Dockers Union in relation to the activities of crime syndicates on the wharves. Finally, the Government intends, in conjunction with the Governments of the States of New South Wales, Victoria and Queensland, to establish in the near future a judicial inquiry with the powers

of a Royal Commission, to investigate and report upon the activities of the Clark drug syndicate in Australia.
Boeing 707 Aircraft
(Question No. 286)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 4 December 1980:
(1) What specific servicing and/or modifications (a) have been or (b) are currently being carried out in 1980-81 on the two Boeing 707 aircraft Nos A20-624 and A20-627 used by the Prime Minister.
(2) What is the (a) specific item cost and (b) total cost in respect of each aircraft referred to in part (1).
(3) Is it a fact that the aircraft are being recarpeted and/or fitted with 8 foot beds; if so, (a) what is the estimated cost of supplying and fitting each bed and (b) who is it expected will use the beds.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Both RAAF B707 aircraft undergo normal scheduled servicings during any twelve month period which include a periodic servicing (annual), a Check 3 servicing (three monthly), an A servicing (monthly) and before-flight/afterflight servicings as required. In respect of modifications to the two aircraft, I refer the honourable member to my answer to Parliamentary Question No. 5604, which appeared in Hansard on 18 September 1980 (page 1612). I am further advised that the on-board cargo handling system was delivered in early January 1981 and will be in service by May 1981. Additionally, auxiliary power units are being acquired, at an estimated cost of $1.32m, for fitment to the two RAAF Boeing 707 aircraft in June 1981, to meet a Defence operational requirement.
(2) The RAAF does not compile individual cost records of servicing carried out by its own personnel. Based on recent servicings performed by QANTAS the overall cost of services is as follows:
Check 3 Service-$29,560
Periodic Service-$408,920
The annual cost for each aircraft for QANTAS servicings is therefore estimated as $527,160. However, variations to individual servicing costs do occur depending on the actual work content of each servicing.
(3) There is currently no plan to re-carpet the RAAF B707 aircraft. However, the aircraft are re-carpeted as necessary to meet normal RAAF servicing standards, i.e. carpets are replaced as dictated by wear and tear. In the VIP configuration the B707 aircraft has two divans. The two divans supplied by QANTAS with their VIP fit have been demonstrated to be extremely uncomfortable on long distance flights and were difficult to remove and replace. Therefore RAAF (No. 486 Squadron) has manufactured two replacement divans which can also be used as fold-out beds. The estimated cost of materials is $120 for each divan. The new divans are fitted to the B707 aircraft in the same position as the old divans and are the same overall length.

Artificial Insemination
(Question No. 315)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 4 December 1980:
(I) Is the National Health and Medical Research Council investigating the po"ible use of (a) 17-beta-oestradiol silicone rubber implant and (b) progesterone-releasing intra-uterine device for use with livestock.
(2) If not, are any Commonwealth Government bodies investigating the use of the devices referred to in part (I).
(3) If investigations are being conducted, at whose instigation were the inquiries initiated.
Mr MaeKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The National Health and Medical Research Council has assessed the toxicological aspects of the use of 17-betaoestradiol in a silicone rubber matrix controlled release device. Progesterone-releasing intra-uterine devices for the synchronisation of oestrus in breeding herds to facilitate artificial insemination have also been assessed toxicologically.
(2)See(I).
(3) The submissions relating to 17-beta-oestradiol and progesterone were submitted by companies as part of the normal process of regulatory control of veterinary drugs.
Petrol Prices
(Question No. 345)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 4 December 1980:
Will he declare petrol in the Australian Capital Territory a declared item under the Prices Regulation Ordinance 1949.
Mr Hodgman--The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Government has determined as part of its Review of Commoowealth Functions that the position of Controller of Prices pursuant to the Prices Regulation Ordinance 1949 will be abolished.
Accordingly, I do not intend to introduce price control on petrol in the Australian Capital Territory.

Nomad Aircraft
(Question No. 542)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(1) What are the reasons for delays in the production of the Nomad aircraft by the Government Aircraft Factory.
(2) How many unfilled orders ~re presently on Government Aircraft Factory order books.
Sir Phillip Lynch-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
On 15 December 1980 I announced that the Government had approved the production of a further 55 Nomad aircraft, bringing the total number of aircraft authorised to date to 200.
Over 100 Nomads are operating in Australia, Papua New Guinea, the United States of America, the Philippines, Indonesia, the Marshall Islands, Guam, Samoa, the Caribbean, Chile, Surinam, Sabah.
Nomads are being used in regular passenger transport and charter operations, in various Military Command, Communication and Support Roles, in Coastal Surveillance, in Aerial Medical Roles including Flying Doctor Service, in Aerial Survey, in Geophysical Search, in Float-Plane Operations, in General Utility and Cargo Carrying Roles.
The aircraft is being operated successfully and profitably in extremes of climate which include Papua New Guinea and Alaska.
Concerning the specific matters raised by the honorable member, I am advised that:

(I) A number of factors have contributed to the slowing down of the Nomad production rate during 1980 and 1981; these include:
(a) the unexpectedly longer lead-times for basic materials, parts and equipments from overseas suppliers due to the pressures of a buoyant overseas market for aircraft, have resulted in a shortage of supply to the production line;
(b) the need for the urgent incorporation of aircraft modifications to improve the service life and acceptability of the aircraft: and
(c) difficulties in the incorporation of special equipments for aircraft for the USA market which involved both local production and purchase of items from the USA which are hard to obtain.
The situation was further compounded during 1980 by industrial unrest, including a month-long strike at the Government Aircraft Factories in March 1980 which had disruptive effects on production extending well beyond the period of the strike itself.
(2) Nineteen for delivery in 1981 and four for delivery in 1982.
Ashton Joint Venture Project
(Question No. 585)
Mr Holding asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on25 February 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the editorial 'The kangaroo from the veldt' in the Australian Flnancial Review, 19 February 1981.
(2) Is there any substance to the allegations made in the editorial, that the Ashton joint venture is 53.6 per cent foreign-owned.
(3) Has the Government or the Foreign Investment Review Board placed any conditions on the partners in the Ashton joint venture project: and specifically, what provision was made for (a) adequate consultation with all affected Aboriginal groups and (b) assessment and control of the environmental impact of the project.
(4) What timetable has the Government set for Ashton Mining NL to achieve 51 per cent Australian equity.
(5) What will be the frequency of Ashton Mining NL'sdiscussions with the Foreign Investment Review Board on its progress towards achieving 51 per cent Australian ownership.
(6) Did the Government give the Ashton joint venture any special consideration due to the involvement of the Malaysia Mining Corporation Berhad (MMC) , a company partly owned by the Malaysian Government; if so, what was the nature of that special consideration.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) My attention was drawn to the editorial 'The Kangaroo from the veldt' in the Australian Financial Review of 19 February 1981 and I wrote a letter to the editor which was published in the Australian Financial Review on 24 February 1981.
(2) The Ashton joint venture comprises CRA Ltd 56.76 per cent, Ashton Mining Ltd 38.24 per cent and Northern Mining Corp NL 5 per cent. Major foreign interests hold 61.1 per cent of CRA and 60.35 per cent of Ashton Mining. Accordingly, foreign equity in the Ashton joint venture is at present 57.8 per cent. However, under the Government's naturalisation provisions of its foreign investment policy, undertakings have been given by the parties concerned that Australian ownership in CRA and Ashton Mining will be increased to 51 per

2582 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

cent. As a consequence, foreign equity in the Ashton joint venture will be reduced to 48.4 per cent.
(3) As the Ashton joint venture project is to be developed by two naturalising companies and an Australian company, the patties are not required to notify the Foreign Investment Review Board of proposals for development of the venture. Accordingly, there are no conditions including matters such as Aboriginal consultations and environment protection attaching to the project at this stage. However, arrangements for the marketing of product from the project could affect the equity and control arrangement for the joint venture and will be examined by the Government in terms of its foreign investment policy prior to the project proceeding to development.
(4) The Government does not specify a firm time limit for a naturalising company to meet its obligations to increase Australian equity to the required level of 51 per cent. The Government expects this level to be achieved over the shortest period consistent with sound company management.
(5) Discussions between the Foreign Investment Review Board and Ashton Mining and its major foreign shareholders will be held annually.
(6). No. The Government saw no reason why it should treat Ashton Mining differently, on the ground that it has a connection with a Malaysian Government agency, from other companies seeking naturalisation. To have done so would have involved discrimination against a company which in all respects complied with the relevant terms of the naturalisation provisions. It is the Government's objective that a naturalised or naturalising company should operate with a predominantly Australian board and should be controlled by that board. Ashton Mining was able to satisfy the Government on all aspects of these requirements.
Aerodrome Local Ownership Plan
(Question No. 612)
Mr Jull asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 25 February 1981:
(1) What criteria are considered in determing whether Commonwealth funds should be provided under the Aerodrome Local Ownership Plan for maintenance and development projects.
(2) Which airports are financed under this scheme in each State and Territory.
(3) What negotiations have been undertaken with local authorities in relation to the transfer of Cairns Airport to local ownership and when is a decision expected in relation to this matter.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honour.able member's question is as follows:
(1) Criteria used in determining whether Commonwealth funds should be provided through the Aerodrome Local Ownership Plan (ALOP) are that the works to be carried out are approved as being necessary for the basic functioning of the aerodrome to meet the foreseeable demands of civil aviation.
Grants are made available to local authorities, mission stations and other bodies accepted as performing the functions of local government. Privately owned licensed aerodromes may be eligible for Commonwealth assistance through the ALOP for approved maintenance projects if the aerodromes are served by regular public transport services.
The criteria applied in determining whether Commonwealth funds should be provided are:
the work is necessary for the safe operation of efficient and effective air services and that the need for these services can be adequately demonstrated

in the case of remote communities, funds can also be made available to ensure a reasonable level of transport and communication and provide for essential services such as medical flights
where a local authority takes over ownership of a Commonwealth aerodrome, funds are provided for the whole cost of bringing the aerodrome movement area to full standard for current and known future aviation commitment.
(2) Aerodromes participating in the ALOP are:
