Nitinol
(Question No. 649)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 26 February 1981:
(I) Is it a fact that Nitinol is an alloy of nickel and titanium which exhibits a wide range of physical changes when subject to low level differences in temperature.
(2) Is he able to say whether research has been carried out in Japan, the USSR, the Federal Republic of Germany and China and in the United States of America by NASA, the MCDonnell-Douglas Corporation, the US Navy, the US Department of Energy and the University of California at Berkeley into use of Nitinol in solid state heat engines.
(3) Has his attention been drawn to the paper 'Proceedings of the Nitinol-Heat Engine Conference' edited by Goldstein and MacNamara (NSWC MP 79/441), published by the US Naval Surface Weapons Centre and the Department of Energy.
(4) Is research and development work being done on Nitinol in Australia; if so, what are the details.
(5) In view of Australia's large reserves of nickel and titanium, will he undertake to have the significance of Nitinol examined as a matter of urgency.

2584 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes. Nitinol is an alloy of nickel and titanium. It has the interesting property that it is a 'shape memory' alloy-that is, when plastically deformed at one temperature, it will, on being heated above a specific temperature, regain its initial shape. In scientific terms this phenomenon is known as a thermo-elastic martemsitic transformation. Other alloys, including those of copper, zinc, and aluminium, display shape memory effects similar to Nitinol. However, they are much cheaper than Nitinol, are much easier to machine and fabricate and therefore find much greater use industrially. Nitinol provides a very good example of the shape memory phenomenon and is widely used in research into this phenomenon. Its high relative cost, and disadvantages vis-a-vis other alloys have meant that its industrial usage remains insignificant.
(2) Research work on Nitinol is conducted in a number of countries, including those listed. Intermittent research on shape memory alloys has been conducted in Australia over the past 20 years. None of the Australian work has been directed explicitly towards the use of Nitinol in solid state heat engines.
(3) I am aware of this publication and would be happy to provide a copy to the honourable member if he so desires.
(4) Research work on Nitinol and related alloys is being conducted in Australia in the Department of Metallurgy of the University of Wollongong, under the direction of Associate Professor N. F. is:ennon. This work has heen supported, in part, by grants from the ARGC ($9,000 in 1981, and $8,000 in 1980). The Australian research on Nitinol, and shape memory alloys in general, is consistent with the scale of the total Australian research effort seen in a world context. More importantly, the priorities of the Australian research community reflect its perceptions of the solubility and fruitfulness of the various research areas.
As noted in (2) above, no research is being conducted in Australia on the use of Nitinol in heat engines although CSIRO maintains a 'watching brief' on overseas developments.
(5) Technological developments which would utilize Australia's large reserves of nickel and titanium would be of significant value. However, as noted above, the current industrial use of Nitinol is negligible and it is unlikely that applications for Nitinol will be developed in the foreseeable future. An urgent examination of the significance of Nitinol could not be justified.
Commonwealth Superannuation Fund Investment Trust
(Question No. 650)
Mr Fry asked the Minister representing the Minister for Finance, upon notice, on 26 February 1981:
(1) Has a firm of accountants been commissioned by the Commonwealth Superannuation Fund Investment Trust to advise it on accounting policy and ways of ensuring equitable distribution of earnings hetween past, present, and future contributors, following the Trust's decision to invest in equities.
(2) Was a firm of actuaries commissioned to produce a similar report.
(3) If so, (a) on what basis were these investigating accountants and actuaries engaged, (b) who was responsible for the decision to appoint them and (c) were CAGEO, ACOA, or any other contributors' organisations consulted before these firms were engaged.

(4) Has any member of the Trust had any dealings with these consultants in the past through private or other interests.
(5) When are the reports expected to be completed and will they be made available to contributors' organisations and individual contributors at the same time that they are made available to (a) the Department of Finance and (b) the Trust.
(6) Does the Australian Taxation Office require nongovernment superannuation schemes to revalue assets regularly to ensure that contributors receive their. correct share of the net worth of the fund on exit; if so, is there a similar requirement for the Commonwealth Superannuation Fund; if not, why not.
Mr Howard-The Minister for Finance has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
The Superannuation Fund Investment Trust is an independent corporate hody established under the Superannuation Act 1976. As such, the Trust has sole responsibility under the Act for the management and investment of the Superannuation Fund, being required only to report annually on the management of the Fund to the Parliament through the Minister for Finance. The Chairman of the Trust has, however, provided the folJowing answers to the honourable member's questions (I) to (6):
(1) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) (a) Both the accountants and actuaries were engaged on the basis of their expertise in the particular area on which the advice was heing sought.
(b) The Trust.
(c) The ACOA Federal Executive was made aware ~ft~e commissioning of the firms of accountants and actuafles In replies to correspondence on the subject received by the then Minister for Finance from the office of the late Senator Knight on 8 July 1980 and 18 August 1980.
(4) The firm of accountants has carried out audit and accounting advice work on hehalf of the Trust since 1976.
The firm of actuari.es has not previously carried out any work on behalf of the Trust.
Other than that mentioned in the foregoing, no member of the Trust has had dealings with these consultants in the past through private or other interests.
(5) The Trust is currently considering the report from the firm of accountants and the report from the firm of actuaries is expected shortly. It is a matter for the Trust, in the first instance, to complete its examination of the reports and to submit recommendations to the Minister on any changes to the accounting arrangements that it considers are warranted. When hoth reports are to hand, the Trust will give consideration to seeking the views of staff organisations on the issues addressed in the consultants' reports.
(6) The Commissioner of Taxation oversights the practices followed by non-government superannuation schemes to ensure that the levels of benefits provided are not excessive and that appropriate contributions are made. To this end, I understand the Commissioner has regard among other things, to the value of any investment assets owned by a superannuation fund but the basis of valuation of these assets would depend upon individual circumstances. Although the Commonwealth Superannuation Scheme is not subject to the Income Tax Assessment Act 1936, the formulation and operation of the Scheme, including the operations of the Trust, broadly follows the principles applicable to private sector schemes. Consistent with this, I understand that the Trust will have regard to private sector practice when making its recommendations.

Defence Force Personnel Stationed in the United States
(Question No. 792)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister [or Poreign Affairs, upon notice, on 24 Mareh 1981:

2586 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

(I) Has Australia an operative Status of Forces agreement in respect of Australian Defence Forces personnel stationed in the United States of America?
(2) If im agreement does not exist, have negotiations to establish a Status of Forces Agreement been undertaken; if so, when were they taken and what stage have the negotiations reached?
(3) If an agreement does not exist, what is the legal status of these personnel?
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No.
(2) Discussions concerning the establishment of a reciprocal Status of Forces Agreement between the United States of America and Australia are continuing.
(3) The status of members of the Australian Defence Force stationed in the United States is established by the applicable principles of International Law; by any arrangements made between Australia and the United States relating to the presence in the United States of particular members; and by a proclamation iss.ued by the President of the United States on 10 October 1965 under the Service Courts of Friendly Foreign Forces Act. Whilst members of the Australian Defence Force are subject to the laws of the United States of America during their stay in that country, pursuant to Presidential proclamation, Australia has been permitted to exercise Australian jurisdiction within the United States in respect of offences committed by members of the Australian Defence Force in the United States.

The Government hopes that Australian firms operating in South Africa will be guided by the Australian Government's overall policy on apartheid.

Exotic Animal Disease, New Zealand: Procedures
(Question No. 978)
Mr Uoyd asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 31 March 1981:
(I) Further to his answer to my question without notice on 25 March 1981 (Hansard, page 882), is he able to say when the New Zealand authorities first were alerted to the outbreak of suspected exotic animal disease near Timaru which subsequently led to investigation and declaration of an infected place on 11 February 1981.
(2) Is he also able to say why there was such a long delay between the declaration of the farm at 4.30 p.m. on I 1 February 1981 and the official announcement at 9.30 a.m. on the following day; if so, have the New Zealand authorities been questioned about this,
(3) Are there any international arrangements covering the timing and form of these arrangements; if so, what are they.

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2589

(4) If his Department first received information from the media about the declaration, does this mean that there is no agreement between the 2 countries to immediately notify the other; if not, is this situation being rectified.
(5) Why was there a delay from 10.30 a.m. to 11.15 a.m. between the first report and the telephone call initiated by his Departmental officers at that time to New Zealand.
(6) Were precautionary measures taken at Australian airports following the unofficial news from New Zealand, and was any alert sent to the airports or a check made of incoming New Zealand planes after the 10.30 a.m. news but ahead of the confirmation; if so, what measures were taken.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) A Ministry of Agriculture Veterinary Officer first suspected that a vesicular disease was implicated on the morning of 11 February 1981. He called in a second Veterinary Officer from the New Zealand Ministry later that morning. The conclusion was reached on the afternoon of 11 February that a vesicular disease was implicated. This led to the declaration of the property as an infected place at 4.30 p.m. New Zealand time on the same day.
(2) No. The New Zealand Government decided that foreign authorities should be notified at the same time as the Prime Ministerial announcement was made to the New Zealand people (viz. 9.30 a.m. on 12 February). The matter has been discussed with the New Zealand authorities and a Senior Veterinary Officer will visit New Zealand early in May for further discussions.

(6) Additional precautionary measures were in place at Mascot by 11.50 a.m. (i.e. 20 minutes after completion of the call to New Zealand) and in all Australian airports by 12.30 p.m. No alert was sent to airports or any additional check of New Zealand planes implemented after the 10.30 a.m. media reports. Rather, immediate confirmatory action as outlined in (5) was taken. As indicated there, the time between placing the call to New Zealand and connection (viz 10.40 a.m. to 11.15 a.m.) was utilised to plan additional quarantine measures which may have been necessary. There was of course no way of knowing how quickly the connection to New Zealand would be made. It would have been totally irresponsible to implement restrictions before confirmation of the outbreak had been received from the source.
Statutory Marketing Corporations: Industry Representatives
(Question No. 997)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 1 April 1981 :
(1) What are the provisions applying in Commonwealth legislation with respect to the appointment of industry representatives to statutory marketing corporations for primary products.
(2) Is it required that all such nominees should forgo their positions in farm organisations.

(3) Australia and New Zealand are members of the Office Internationale des Epizooties (located in Paris), the rules of which require notification of exotic diseases within 24 hours of confirmation. (This disease was never con firmed). In addition to this, an Australia/New Zealand Technical Committee on Quarantine was set up in 1966 and regular communication on quarantine matters is exchanged by annual meetings of this group. Between meetings, information is exchanged on a direet basis and may involve any of the folIowing matters: changes in vaccination or in importation policies and, in particular, outbreaks of disease. These arrangements are on the basis of bilateral notification 'as soon as possible'.
(4) Yes. Arrangements for notification exist. See 3 above. I am advised that arrangements were made for a telex to be sent to Australia to coincide with the New Zealand Prime Minister's announcement at 9.30 a.m. on 12 February New Zealand time. I understand that the telex left the New Zealand Ministry immediately after the public announcement but, in accordance with normal New Zealand diplomatic procedure, was forwarded via the NZ High Commission in Canberra. The telex did not reach the Australian Quarantine Service until 1.45 p.m. Australian time by which time the news had been received through the media. ,
(5) My officers received the first reports through the media at 10.30 a.m. on 12 February Australian time. The Quarantine Division immediately established that the Department of Primary Industry had heard simultaneously of the incident via a call from Paris where the Office Internationale des Epizooties was, by chance, meeting. The call indicated that an incident had been reported in New Zealand. At 10.40 a.m., a call was placed by the Quarantine Division to the New Zealand authorities to seek confirmation. Connection was not achieved until approximately 11.15 a.m. While awaiting connection the implementation of additional quarantine precautions, was planned in case they proved to be necessary. When connection was achieved the New Zealand authorities confirmed a suspected vesicular disease outbreak and, at the
same time, were advised, broadly, of precautionary measures that Australia would take.

(3) Are there variations from corporation to corporation or to board; if so, why.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The legislative provisions governing the appointment of industry representatives to the eleven primary industry statutory marketing authorities established by statutes which I administer are summarised below.
The appointment provisions for industry representatives are not uniform for all authorities but fall within the following broad categories:
appointed on either the nomination of an industry body/interest group or from a panel of names provided by an industry body/interest group
appointed on either the nomination of a State marketing board or State Minister
appointed upon election by a formal poll of qualified voters.
(2) No.
(3) Yes.
Generally speaking, the appointment provisions (see (1) above) reflect the different circumstances of each industry and the functions of the authority, including the extent to which the powers of the authority remove the rights of individual growers in respect of their products.

Members representing manufacturers of dairy produce are appointed by me from among persons whose names have been submitted by organisations of manufacturers of dairy produce
Each member representing apple and pear growers is appointed by me from persons nominated by the Australian Apple and Pear Growers' Association
Members representing canners are appointed by me from among persons nominated by canners for the purpose, each canner nominating one person
Members representing growers are appointed by me on the nomination of the Australian Canning Fruit growers' Association
Members representing producers of dried fruits are appointed by me from among persons whose names are included in a list of names submitted by the Australian Dried Fruits Association
Members appointed to represent co-operative wineries and distilleries are appointed by the Governor-General upon the nomination of the Co-operative Winemakers' Association
Members appointed to represent proprietary and privately owned wineries and distilleries in the various States are appointed by the Governor General upon the nomination of the respective State associations affiliated with the Australian Wine and Brandy Producers' Association Incorporated
Members appointed to represent grapegrowers supplying grapes to wineries and distilleries are appointed by the Governor-General upon the nomination of the Winegrape Growers' Council of Australia

B52s in Darwin: Quarantine Precautions
(Question No. t002)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 1 April 1981:
Will quarantine precautions be taken against the B52s of the United States Air Force if and when they land in Darwin, Northern Territory: if so, what measures.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Normal quarantine procedures will apply to all United States Air Force B52 aircraft arriving at Darwin, Northern Territory.

The usual quarantine measures are as follows: On arrival the aircraft is boarded by a Quarantine Officer and his assistants. The General Declaration prepared in advance by the captain of the aircraft is sighted and a visual examination made of the persons on board. The quarantine assistants disinsect both the aircraft cabin and holds. Vaccination certificates and other travel documents are examined for compliance with Australia's entry requirements.
When the Quarantine Officer is satisfied that the aircraft and all on board are not a source of infection, a certificate of pratique is granted. Until this time po goods (including refuse and unconsumed foodstuffs) can be landed and no persons are allowed to board the aircraft.
Following.the granting of pratique refuse and unconsumed foodstuffs may be removed from the aircraft under quarantine supervision. Items of quarantine concern are not to be removed without the permission of the Quarantine Officer.

Repatriation General Hospital, Daw Park, South Australia
(Question No. 1030)

(1) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the state of the operating theatres at the Repatriation General Hospital, Daw Park, South Australia, which were built in 1942 but Which have not been upgraded since.

Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 2 April1981:

(2) Does the small size of these theatres provide a number of serious problems, such as (a) increasing the potential for infection because it is difficult to prevent sterile drapes on the trays from being touched by staff who have to move in and out

2596 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

of the operating room and (b) necessitating the operating table to be placed diagonally across the room in certain major surgical procedures to allow X -ray machinery to be brought in.

(3) Are there too few theatres at the hospital which means that (a) they cannot be left to 'breathe' while being effectively
cleaned between cases, (b) it is not possible to reserve at least one theatre for certain particular 'clean' cases and (c) it is frequently necessary for surgery to be performed on 'clean' cases straight after 'dirty' cases such as open bowel surgery thereby greatly increasing the likelihood of infection.

(4) Is the endoscopy room at the hospital where minor procedures are performed, often used for procedures for which it is not equipped, such as cardiac pacemaker insertion.

(5) Is the air conditioning in this room sub-standard, and does it result in the problems of (a) accumulation of dust and bacteria which cause particular concern in eye and ear surgery and (b) the likelihood that operating rooms are either too hot or too cold placing patients under anaesthesia at great risk from such temperature "xtremes.

(6) Are the floors of the operating theatres also substandard, and in particular, does the repeated lifting of the floor coverings and the pooling of water in certain areas create a situation encouraging the growth of bacteria.

(7) Have there been recent problems with the isolation of pathogenic organisms and are the measures taken only temporarilyeffective.

(8) Is there a hazardous communicating passage between lhe 2 main theatres which leads to the carriage of infection between theatres and cross-contamination.

(9) Is the recovery room far too small, being equipped for 4

patients but often having to cope with 7, so that patients have

had to be left in the main corridor because no space has been

availablein the recovery room.

.

(10) Are there suitable areas for anaesthetising patients at the hospital; if not, do patients requiring local anaesthetics usually have these administered in the recovery room, next to patients who may be vomiting, coughing, et cetera thereby adding to the anxiety of the patients awaiting treatment, and are other patients undesirably exposed to the flurry and fright of being wheeled conscious into the operating theatre, against modern standards of surgical practice.

(11) Is some equipment at the hospital stored in corridors because the storage area is too small; if so, has some of this equipment been damaged because of this situation.

(12) Are surgical instruments crowded into cupboards which are totally inadequate for this purpose; if so, are there difficulties in maintenance and in the selection of particular items when they are suddenly and urgently required.

(13) Is there any area where out-patients awaiting surgery can comfortably sit and is there any suitable area where students and staff can be given instruction or teaching; if ilot, does this considerably limit educational possibilities.

(14) Is the development of a new operating theatre block at the hospital urgently required and paramount to the development of the hospital as a whole; if so, will the Minister approve this development as a matter of urgency.

Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

(1) I understand that the operating theatre block at the Repatriation General Hospital, Daw Park, was built in 1942. In 1963 a urology theatre was added and-in 1975 the two major theatres were partially renovated to remove a number. of deficiencies.

(2) (a) While the potential for infection does exist, extraordinary care is taken by the staff to preserve sterility of drapes on the trays during movement about the theatres and (b) Yes.
(3) (a) With the increase in the number of surgical cases in recent years it is necessary to maintain tighter control on theatre utilisation and ensure that sufficient time is available for staff to clean the theatres and to prevent any risk of 'dirty' cases causing infection in 'clean' cases, (b) Yes and (c) Careful and continuous monitoring of infection rates in surgical cases shows that cleaning and scheduling measures undertaken by the staff are effective and that there is no added risk to patients.
(4) Some selected procedures such as cardiac pacemaker insertion are performed in the endoscopy room. However, the surgeon undertaking these procedures ensures that precautions are taken to fully protect the patient.
(5) (a) The existing air conditioning system does create potential for infection. However, regular environmental monitoring is performed to ensure safe bacteriological conditions and (b) The existing air conditioning system does have difficulty maintaining full temperature control during heat wave conditions. Surgical sessions are cancelled when the operating theatres become too hot for surgery in order to avoid unnecessary risks to patients. The anacsthetists and theatre staff take all necessary precautions against the effects of temperature variation on patients.
(6) Ptoblems with the theatre floor coverings have been detected in the regular and rigorous inspections of the theatres. However,preventative measures and close monitoring ensure that the potential for growth of bacteria is controlled.
(7) An organism was detected in the theatres on one occasion. However, this was quickly and effectively controlled. Continuous monitoring and adequate cleaning procedures ensure that the potential for growth of bacteria is adequately controlled.
(8) The two main operating theatres are in direct and open communication with each other, but care is taken to ensure that no cross-contamination occurs.
(9) Due to the limited size of the recovery room there are times when the number of surgical cases results in some overcrowding. However, when insufficient sp~ee is available, patients are returned to their ward where the necessary staff care is provided.
(10) Facilities for the anaesthetising of patients in the 'operating theatre complex are limited. However, despite this shortcoming, patients undergoing anaesthesia are not put at risk. On occasions it has been found necessary to use the recovery room for patients requiring local anaesthetic. It has also been found necessary on occasions to administer general anaesthesia to patienls in the main operating theatres themselves. In these circumstances the staff are careful to minimise any possible distress to patients.
(II) Storage facilities in the operating theatre complex are inadequate and some equipment is stored in the main theatre corridors. Staff are careful to ensure that equipment is protected from damage.
(12) Equipment storage facilities are inadequate and some problems have been experienced. By careful attention to maintenance, this problem is minimised.
(13) Out-patient waiting facilities and areas for student teaching are limited within the operating theatres.
(14) New theatres are required and a proposal for replacement of the operating theatre complex is currently under consideration.

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2597

Broome Aboriginal Medical Service
(Question No. 1035)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 2 April 1981:
(1) Is the Broome Aboriginal Medical Service, WA, at present struggling for survival because of a shortage of funds.
(2) Has his attention-been drawn to a statement by the Western Australian Minister for Health, which suggests that the Service should be treated simply as a private practice ineligible for Government funding; if so, what is his reaction to the statement.
(3) Has the Service approached his Department for funds; if so, what has been his Department's response.
(4) If the response was unfavourable, what does he require of the Service before he is able to give more favourable consideration to the submission.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) My colleague the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs on 9 April 1981 announced the provision of assistance to the Broome Aboriginal Medical Service.
(2), (3) and (4). I am aware of the statement by the Western Australian Minister for Health. Apart from benefiting from assistance already provided by my Department under the direct billing arrangements for disadvantaged patients, the Broome Aboriginal Medical Service sought a Health Program Grant. However, at present the Service is not eligible for assistance in this way. The Government is currently considering future policy in relation to Aboriginal health and it is expected an announcement will be made shortly.
Motor Vehicle Registration Inspections
(Question No. 1060)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 2 April 1981:
(1) What is the capacity per day of registration inspections at the (a) Dickson and (b) Phillip Motor Registry Offices.
(2) Do facilities exist for 3 lanes of vehicles to be tested.
(3) How many lanes have been utilised during each month of 1981.
(4) Are all 3 lanes being utilised at the present time; if not, why not.
(5) What was the average wait per motorist for a registration inspection during March 1981.
(6) Have any estimates been made of the fuel consumption and pollution caused by cars waiting for long periods in inspection queues.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The capacity of the testing stations varies with level of staffing, number of hours worked and type of vehicle. At present staff level and hours of opening (43 hours per week), the theoretical capacity is 1750 motor cars per week at each testing station, or approximately 383 cars each day. From October 1980 to March 1981 Phillip operated at half capacity while certain construction work was in progress.
(2) Each station normally has 4 lanes but Phillip operated on only 2 during reconstruction of the inspection pits.
(3) In each month of 1981 to date 4 lanes at Dickson and 2 at Phillip have been utilised. Since April 3 lanes have been available at Phillip most of the time.
(4) See answers to (2) and (3).

(5) The average wait per motorist during March 1981 was approximately fifty minutes at Phillip and fifteen minutes at Dickson.
(6) No.
Control oCPollution in the Australian Capital Territory
(Question No. 1061)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 2 April 1981:
(1) Is he able to say whether the Koppers timber processing firm is proposing to move to the Hume Industrial Estate; if so, what environmental controls will be applied.
(2) Are the environmental standards of the Australian Capital Territory lower than those which apply in New South Wales.
(3) What provisions exist in the Australian Capital Territory for control of (a) noise, (b) air, (c) water and (d) other forms of pollution.
(4) What powers does his Department have in the control ofpollutionin the Australian Capital Territory.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) In January 1980 Koppers (Aust.) Pty ~tdmade formal application for the lease of a site at Hume suitable for a timber treatment and log processing plant. Negotiations are not yet concluded.
My Department and the National Capital Development Commission are aware of the potentially adverse environmental impact of such a plant. The proposal has therefore been the subject of close scrutiny and controls have been formulated to ensure that any adverse effect is minimised.
The controls cover the discharge of liquid wastes, noise emissions and air pollution. A monitoring program will be established to measure the effectiveness of the safeguards.
The controls will be embodied in the Crown Lease to ensure complete environmental protection in the interim period before the proposed Australian Capital Territory environmental protection legislation is promulgated. Adequate sanctions exist to ensure compliance with covenants of lease.
(2) While there is presently no comprehensive legislation controlling environmental pollution in the Australian Capital Territory, the need is recognised and draft legislation is being prepared based on the advice of an Inter-Departmental Committee on Environmental Quality. The standards will be similar in effect to those in force in New South Wales.
(3) The following Australian Capital Territory Ordinances and Regulations allow for limited control of forms of pollution:
(a) Noise
the Animal Nuisance Control Ordinance 1975 covers noise emitted by animals;
the Inspection of Machinery Regulations made pursuant to the Machinery Ordinance 1949 sets noise levels above which the use of hearing protection devices is compulsory in the workplace;
the Motor Traffic Ordinance 1936 allows for the refusal of registration for a motor vehicle which emits excessive noise. and provides that it is an offence to drive a motor vehicle which emits undue noise;
the Police Offences Ordinance 1930 provides that the emission of noise which causes undue disturbance is an offence;

2598 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

the Small Claims Ordinance 1974 allows individuals to take action to have noise nuisance emitted by neighbours abated.
(b) Air
The Building Ordinance 1972 allows the Building Controller, in considering plans and specifications, to take into account whether the proposed structure makes adequate provision for the disposal of smoke, gas, fumes, and ash;
The New South Wales Smoke Abatement Act 1902, in its application to the Australian Capital Territory, provides that it is an offence for a person to use a furnace which has not been constructed so as to prevent the formation of smoke therein.
(c) Water
The Police Offences Ordinance 1930 provides that the dumping of dead animals and the diversion of watercourses, are offences;
The Cotter River Ordinance 1914 provides protection for the Cotter River catchment;
The Public Health (General Sanitation) Regulations made pursuant to the Public Health Ordinance 1928 provides for the protection of the water supply;
The Canberra Water Supply (Googong 1?am) Act 1974 provides for the protection of that part of the Canberra water supply derived from the Googong Dam;
The Canberra Sewerage and Water Supply Regulations made pursuant to the Building and Services Ordinance 1924 provides that pollution of a stream which is part of the water supply is an offence;
The Lakes Ordinance 1976 provides that pollution of a lake and any stream, creek or river flowing into a lake is an offence.
(4) The legislation referred to in (3) is administered by the Minister for the Capital Territory apart from the Police Offences Ordinance administered by the Attorney-Genera~ and the Public Health Ordinance administered by the Minister for Health. Various powers and procedures are available under the legislation which does apply:
Noise-The Registrar of Dogs has power under the Animal Nuisance Control Ordinance to make application to the Court for an order that an animal not be kept or that specified works be carried out.
The Chief Inspector of Machinery and Inspectors appointed under the Inspection of Machinery Regulations have power to enter any premises when machinery is in operation.
The Registrar of Motor Vehicles has power to refuse registration to a vehicle which does not comply with registration standards. The Police have normal powers to prosecute persons who operate a motor vehicle that emits undub' noise,
The Police have normal powers of prosecution under the Police Offences Ordinance.
Air Pollution-The Building Controller has power to refuse approval for a structure under the Building Ordinance.
The Police or an owner or occupier of premises may bring an action under the New South Wales Smoke Nuisance Abatement Act.
Water Pollution-The Police have normal powers of prosecution under the Police Offences Ordinance;
The Capital Territory Health Commission, Medical Officers of Health and Inspectors have powers under the Public Health Ordinance and Public Health (General Sanitation) Regulations to prosecute offenders;

The National Capital Development Commission has power to plan and make provision for the prevention of pollution of water supplied or to be supplied from the Googong Dam under the Canberra Water Supply (GoogongDam) Act;
The Proper Authority appointed for the purposes of the Canberra Sewerage and Water Supply Regulations and the Minister have power to prosecute offenders;
The Minister for the Capital Territory or the Police have the power to prosecute offenders under the Lakes Ordinance.
In addition a power exists under many leases which enables a measure of control over various forms ofpollution.
Re-targeting of Ballistic and Guided Missiles
(Question No. 1070)
Dr EYeringham asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to President Carter's Directive 59 allowing re-targeting of ballistic and guided missiles to military rather than economic or popUlation targets.
(2) Is he able to say whether this change from countervalue to counterforce or limited nuclear war strategy has been supported by increased accuracy or ice-pick capability in nuclear attacks against missile silos and missile control centres.
(3) Has the Government expressed its view on this initiation of nuclear strikes against nuclear emplacements (INSANE) policy as an alternative to the preceding mutually assured destruction (MAD) doctrine as a deterent to the arms race.
Mr KiUen--The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) See my answer to 18 September 1980 to Question on Notice No, 6374 (Hansard, page 1634) and my statementof5 May 1981.
(2) Presidential Directive 59 does not constitute a change in US strategy from countervalue to counterforce. In addition to my answer to question (1) above, I also refer the Honourable Member to the United States Department of Defense Annual Report Fiscal Year 1982, particularly pages 42-3. I would draw to his attention in particular however the following sentences:
'The countervailing strategy by no means implies that we do not-or no longer-require the ultimate deterrent effect of being able to threaten the full Soviet target structure including the industrial and economic base.'
and
'It (the countervailing strategy) does not caJl for substituting primarily military for primarily civilian targets. '
(3) The premise on which the honourable member's question is based is mistaken.

Social Security Expenditures for OECD Countries

(Question No. 1177)

Dr KI~gman asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 8 Apnl1981:

Is he able to provide the (a) gross national social security expenditures and (c) the latter as

parpoedruccet~ta(bge)

of the former (defining (b) to include expenditures under

public medical care systems and cash payments under public

welfare programs including age, survivor, unemployment and

disability insurance payments) for OIleD countries' if so

what are the details.

',

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

A number of attempts have been made to compare different countries' spending on social security as a percentage of GDP or GNP.

Unfortunately such studies encounter a number of definitional and technical problems. These problems are discussed in an article 'International Comparisons of Social Security E~penditur~', that appeared in Social Security Quarterly, Wmter/Spnng 1977, Vol. 5, No.1.

International comparisons of social security expenditure may be misleading for a number of reasons.

Some of the problems are:

comparisons generally include only direct puhlic sector expenditure and do not include contributions towards welfare outside the public sector;

taxation concessions or deductions may be provided iristead of or as well as cash payments;
other forms of subsidies towards Jiving costs may not be included in the total of welfare expenditure;
differing economic circumstances (e.g. level of unemployment) will require differing levels of social security expenditure.
For these and other reasons international comparisons of social security expenditure are not only extremely difficult to make, but are of limited value for ascertaining the adequacy of such expenditure in meeting the welfare needs of communities.
Expenditure by OECD countries on education, income maintenance and health during the early 1960s and mid 19708 formed part of a study by the OECD in 1978 (PlIblic Expenditure Trends, Studies in Resource Allocation, OECD, June 1978-see especially Table 6). However, the study warned that problems of data availability and their interpretation limit the conclusions which could be drawn. In addition, it was stressed that since the countries concerned differ in terms of their historical experience with public expenditure, their institutions, and their national tastes and values, caution should be exercised when basing conclusions on such international comparisons.

Sale of previously-occupied immovable property; leasing of immovable property; (4)medical and dental services; education; banking; insurance; postal services

(1) The list of items covered is illustrative, not exhaustive. (2) This lists the more important exemptions, but is not a complete list of all exemptions.
(3) Food purchased for on-premises consumption, such as at a restaurant, is taxed at the standard rate.
(4) The leasing of immovable property to taxable persons is a taxable transaction. This allows the lessee a deduction for tax paid to the lessor.
(5) The 'zero rate' treats the taxable item as incurring a rate of zero on its sale, but allows a credit for tax paid on purchases. Thus an item taxed at a rate of zero bears no tax. This is in contrast to an exempt transaction where no tax is charged on the sale, but no deduction or credit is allowed for tax paid on purchases.
(6) The normal rate of VAT generally applicable to all taxable transactions is 19 per cent on the taxable base including the VAT itself. Thus the effective rate is 23.46 per cent on the taxable base excluding VAT itself.
(7) As at 1978.

Answers to Questions

Television Program 'To Market, To Market'
(Question No. 1184)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 8 April 1981 :
(1) Is it a fact that, after 21 years, the ABC Brisbane television segment To Market. To Market, terminated on 3 April 1981.
(2) Has the Queensland Chamber of Fruit and Vegetables protested strongly against the programming decision.
(3) Who made the decision and for what reasons was it made.
(4) What will replace this program and another recently terminated program called Bon Appetit.
(5) Are the replacement programs Australian-made.
(6) Will he intervene to re-instate the program To Market. To Market, perhaps at an alternative viewing time.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The final To Market. To Market program in Brisbane was televised by the ABC on 9 April 1981.
(2) The Queensland Chamber of Fruit and Vegetable Industries Co-operative Ltd requested the ABC to continue the program.
(3) Senior ABC officers of the ABC's Program Division. It was considered that the program no longer adequately served its original objective.
(4) Programs designed to appeal to family viewing audiences. Other consumer affairs programs are to be developed.
(5) Some will be produced in Australia.
(6) The ABC's responsibility to the community as a whole is to present programs which reach the highest possible standard and reflect the wide range of interests found within the community. This is not an area in which I can direct the Commission.

Integration of Handicapped Children

(Question No. 1196)

Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:

(1) Does the Schools Commission recommend that the
philosophy and general application-of integration practice should be the subject ofcontinuing research.

(2) If so, what provision has been made to initiate this research.

(3) How do the approaches of the various State Departments of Education differ in respect of integration of handicapped children.

(4) Does the Schools Commission advocate investigation of

ways in which the Commonwealth may assist in the early

identification of disabilities in children and intervention to

correct disabilities where possible.

.

(5) Is this function being performed by ANSUA.

(6) Will the Schools Commission investigate the work of ANSUA.

(7) What activities have been undertaken during the International Year of Disabled Persons in respect of the following educational programs: (a) training of special education teachers, (b) provision of support personnel, (c) attention to the educational environment, (d) extension of the age range

for the education of handicapped young people, (e) early intervention, (f) transition programs, (g) integration into regular schools, (h) provision for isolated handicapped students and (j) educational services for severely handicapped children who are homebound or who live long-term in residential institutions.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) In its Report for the Triennium 1982-84 the Schools Commission has undertaken to foster research on the integration of handicapped children into regular schools.
(3) As described in the Schools Commission's Report for the Triennium 1982-84 the diverse approaches of the various State Departments of Education in respect of integration of handicapped children include:
(a) the establishment of a flexible placement system which seeks appropriate educational provision for all students;
(b) provision of appropriate education within a wide range of schooling options rather than the adoption of integration as the ultimate goal for all handicapped students;
(c) working to a policy of meeting students' needs with their peers at neighbourhood schools with as little restriction as possible;
(d) maintaining all students, except the moderately, severely and multiply handicapped in regular schools;
(e) the provision of segregated special schools which work towards preparing handicapped students for future social integration in the community.
(4) In its Report for the Triennium 1982-84 the Schools Commission undertakes to investigate ways in which the Commonwealth may assist in early intervention programs.
(5) Yes.
(6) The Schools Commission advises that its proposed investigation will include a study of the work undertaken by such organisations as ANSUA.
(7) During the International Year of Disabled Persons activities which have been undertaken in respect of the following educational programs include:
(a) Training of special education teachers-No specific activities have been undertaken by the Schools Commission. However, funding for such activities is available under the Commission's Services and Development Program.
(b) Provision of support personnel-Basically this is a matter for State education authorities. However, funding available under the Schools Commission's Special Education Program for special schools and classes, integration and the development of programs for severely handicapped children may be used for this purpose.
(c) Attention to the educational environment-Funds available to special schools under the Schools Commission's Capital Grants Program can be used for the provision and improvement of buildings and equipment.
During International Year of Disabled Persons the Schools Commission is funding:
a national co-operative study, involving government and the larger non-government education authorities, of the school facilities required to meet the needs of handicapped children in special and regular schools;

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2607

a national conference to focus on facilities required for handicapped school children.
(d) Extension of the age range for the education of handicapped young people-No Commonwealth funds are available for this purpose through Schools Commission funding. However, recommendations for such an extension have been made in the report on the national survey of special education undertaken for the Schools Commission by the Schonell Educational Research Centre.
(e) Early intervention-The Schools Commission has undertaken in its Report for the Triennium 1982-84 to investigate ways in which the Commonwealth may assist in early intervention programs.
(f) Transition programs-The Schools Commission has drawn attention in its Report for the Triennium 1982-84 to an increasing interest in transition training for adolescent students, an interest which is characteristic of most schools, including special schools. The Schools Commission supports developments in the States and Territories which include the framing of policies on work experience and vocational training; the provision of training in independent living skills and techniques; vocational counselling; and the provision of pre-vocational and vocational courses, daily living skill courses and access courses for vocational study by Colleges of Technical and Further Education.
(g) Integration into regular schools-In 1981 the Government is making available through the Schools Commission's Special Education Program $1.059 million for the integration of handicapped children into regular schools.
(h) Provision for isolated handicapped students-In its Report for the Triennium 1982-84 the Schools Commission has drawn attention to the needs of isolated handicapped children and it has made such children eligible for funding to attend neighbourhood regular schools under the integration program.
(j) Educational services for severely handicapped children who are homebound or who live long-term in residential institutions-In 1981 the Commonwealth Government has made $2.118 million available for the development of educational programs for severely handicapped children.
Depo-Provera
(Question No. 1203)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister representing the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to (a) an article by Jan Mayman in the National Times, 15-21 March 1981 (page 4), pointing out hazards of the use of DepoProvera as a contraceptive and (b) the reported banning of the drug in the United States of America.
(2) If so, will the Minister take steps (a) to recommend to Australian doctors that the drug not be used for Aboriginal women and (b) to ensure that special efforts be made in advising them to ensure that they (i) understand the available alternatives, (ii) have an opportunity to discuss these with an informed person who can communicate effectively with them, with time to make an informed choice, especially in view of the reported high incidence among Aborigines of diabetes and other conditions which allegedly increase the hazards of use of the drug.
(3) If the Minister will not take these actions, will he request that all users of Depo-Provera for contraception be

recorded for statistical analysis of (a) safety, (b) side-effects and (c) reliability.
Mr Viner-The Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Yes.
(2) I am informed that Depo-Provera has been available on the Australian market for many years for use in certain indications but not including use as a contraceptive. The Australian Drug Evaluation Committee which advises my colleague, the Minister for Health, on matters relating to the importation and distribution of drugs in Australia, has considered progressively available information on Depo-Provera for contraceptive use on a number of occasions. The Committee is of the opinion that this use of the drug must be regarded as investigational since there are unresolved questions related to its safety for this indication. This statement is included in the product information distributed by the Company for this drug.
As with any drug which is regarded as investigational, ethics dictate that, where the medical practitioner is of the opinion that special circumstances warrant the use of such a drug, there is a responsibility on his part both for adequately informing the patient or the guardian, and for any adverse consequences of the therapy.
In regard to 'informed consent', I appreciate that special care is required in respect of Aboriginal patients. My Department encourages additional training in cultural matters for doctors and other medical professionals who propose to work in areas where there is a significant Aboriginal population and supports the provision of orientation and in-service courses. In addition, most State Health Departments, often with Commonwealth support, employ Aboriginal Health Workers, several hundreds in all, who have important liaison roles. Both of these measures are designed to achieve the quality of communication necessary to ensure that Aboriginal patients are at ease with and have clear understanding in, their contacts with health care personnel.
(3) I have brought the honourable member's suggestion to the notice of my colleague, the Minister for Health.
Disposal of Authorities to Prospect
(Question No. 1205)
Dr Everingham asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the case of BjelkePetersen v. Federal Commissioner of Taxation (A.L.R. 1963 pages 24-30).
(2) If so, can he state whether (a) this report disclosed actions by the appellant who in effect sold for  12,650 assets whose valuation was based solely on the holding of an authority to prospect which had reportedly cost  2,723 some 8 months before, (b) the judgment noted that the appellant was pursuing precisely the same course with respect to another authority to prospect in the State of Queensland, (c) the course referred to was the formation of a company of which the appellant was in effect sole proprietor so that in selling about half the interest in that company to an independent company he could use their capital contribution to cover dividends decided and paid by and to himself and (d) the company the appellant reportedly formed was regarded as a device to avoid a State lav,: providing that an authority to prospect could in effect not be sold at market value.
(3) Is he able to say whether Queensland law has been amended to prevent similar actions regarding the disposal of authorities to prospect.

United States Tariff on Imports of Raw Wool
(Question No. 1624)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
What action has been taken to obtain reductions of the United States of America tariff on imports of raw wool during the last 3 years.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
One of the Government's main objectives during the recent Multilateral Trade Negotiations was to obtain a substantial reduction.in the wool tariff in the United States of America. The USA is the only major industrialised country to maintain a duty on raw wool imports.

2614 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

The Government was successful in securing a 60 per cent reduction in the tariff on raw apparel wool-the maximum reductionthat the US Administration was empowered to offer.
This reduction is being phased in over three years, from 25.5 cents per lb (clean equivalent) to 10 cents per lb. Phasing commenced on 1 January 1980 and the current rate of duty is 15 cents per lb.

Flexitime Absences during Protest Stoppages
(Question No. 1664)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister Assisting the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
Have any studies been made of the use of tlexitime absences by public servants on days in which Public Service unions have planned protest stoppages or days of non-attendance; if so, what are the findings.
Mr Viner-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
No such studies have been undertaken. Flexitime schemes, once introduced within certain parameters, are a matter for administration by individual departments. In providing departments with advice on the administrative arrangements to be applied in relation to industrial action, the Public Service Board has stated that under no circumstances should staff who absent themselves without authority be permitted to claim any period of their absence as tlexitime.
Central Classification Committees
(Question No. 1666)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister Assisting the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 8 April 1981 :
What central classification committees have been appointed under the Public Service Act during the last 5 years.
Mr Viner-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
None. Although great use was made of these Committees in the immediate post-war years and through the 1950's, they

have not been used since the early 1960's. The establishment and continuing review of employment groups in the Service, following the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission's decisions in the Professional Engineers' Case in 1961, the broadbanding of classification structures, and the tendency to take to arbitration those classification issues which could not be resolved by conciliation, has created a situation in which the establishment of Central classification Committees has not been warranted.

Answers to Questions

letter to the Prime Minister. The Attorney-General considered that some aspects of the report were open to criticism. Particulars of those aspects arc set out in the fourth paragraph of the Attorney-Genera!'s letter set out at page 15 of the Commissioner's Report.

Legal Aid
(Question No. 1671)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Has the Attorney-Genera!'s attention been drawn to a comment in the 1979-80 Annual Report of the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission that unless economies in the conduct of cases be practised by such (private legal) practitioners it is probable that State and Territory Commissions and other legal aid authorities will be obliged to rely to a greater extent on the services of salaried lawyers, especially in criminal trials.
(2) Does this trend accord with Government policy; if not, what is heing done to reverse the trend.
Mr Viner-The Attorney-General has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Yes.
(2) It has always been the Government's view that legal aid should be provided by both private legal practitioners and salaried lawyers. The legislation estahlishing the State and Australian Capital Territory Commissions provide accordingly. The Government, however, does maintain a continuing scrutiny of the effectiveness of existing arrangements for the provision of legal aid, so that funds can be most effectively, efficiently, and economically applied. The Government has provided funds for a cost comparison study between salaried and private lawyers. This study is expected to be completed later this year, and the results will be closely considered. It should be noted that all State and Territory Commissions are independent statutory authorities and hence it will be primarily for those Commissions to decide whether any changes should be made to existing arrangements.

Launceston General Hospital Laundry
(Question No. 1706)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
(1) What investigations were made by the Commonwealth or Tasmanian Governments into the use of commerciallaundry services prior to the decision to construct a new laundry for the Launceston General Hospital.
(2) If investigations were made, whO conducted them and what was the result; if there were no investigations, why not.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) and (2) The Tasmanian Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works in its hearings into the proposal for a group laundry at the Launceston General Hospital received oral and written submissions from the Textile Care and Rental Association of Tasmania supporting the use of commerciallaundry services at Launceston. After considering the submissions and evidence and hearing evidence from the Tasmanian Department of Health Services and a special laundry consultant appointed by the Tasmanian Department of Health Services, the Committee concluded-
'The Committee does not think that commercial laundries are the right solution to future increased demand even though their premises could be expanded. It is felt that there is validity in the opinion that, as far as possible, laundry should be serviced and treated on a hospital site where there are laboratory facilities for testing bacteriological problems and adequate specialised supervision.'
The Committee went on to recommend that a new group laundry be built at Launceston. This recommendation was accepted by the Tasmanian Parliament. The conclusion and recommendation are in line with general practice adopted by Commonwealth and State health authorities for the provision of laundry services for hospitals; approximately 95 per cent of hospital laundry is handled by specialist laundries.

(Question No. 1707)

(1 ) In view of the statement in the 1979-80 Annual Report of his Department (page 19) that there appears to have been an increase in deliberate attempts to smuggle plant material into AUstralia, which offences were prosecuted during 1979-80.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

( I) During 1979-80 229 successful prosecutions were launched against passengers deliberately attempting to smuggle plant material into Australia.
During the six months January-June 1980 there were 126 successful prosecutions. This compared with 73 over the corresponding period during 1979.
Multicultural Teaching Force
(Question No. 1732)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
What action has been taken on the recommendation of the Commissioner for Community Relations contained in his 5th Annual Report for the year 1979-80 (page 88), that trainee teachers should remain at their studies for a further period to acquire some knowledge of a community language and culture, and thus take their place as members of a new multicul- .tural teaching force.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The recommendation in the Commissioner for Community Relations' 5th Annual Report (page 88) to extend the formal training period of trainee teachers was, in another form, considered by the National Inquiry into Teacher Education (NITE). The NITE Report recommended, inter alia, four years initial pre-service training for all teachers. This recommendation, in the light of developments in the most recent years, was not supported by the Ministers for Education meeting as the Australian Education Council in February 1981.
YMCA of Canberra Incorporated
(Question No. 1790)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Has a loan been made to the YMCA of Canberra Incorporated; if so, for what sum and why was it granted.
(2) What are the interest and repayment terms and what security is there in regard to the loan.
Mr Hodgman---The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) A loan was made to the YMCA of Canberra for $2,171,114.31, to finance the construction of a 200 bed young people's hostel at Belconnen.
The first payment of $45,000 against the loan was made on 2 June 1975 and subsequent payments were advanced as required during the course of construction. The final payment was made on 22 February 1977. Under the terms of the lease, the first repayment instalment did not become due until 23 February 1979.
(2) Interest was chargeable at the rate of 5 per cent per annum compounded half yearly with repayments to be made six monthly over a period of 48t years. If, however, payments were made by the due date, a rebate of 1 per cent per annum was allowable on the interest rate i.e., the effective rate became 4 per cent per annum. However, the YMCA's liability in respect of the loan was extinguished in full on 2 February 1981 upon the surrender of the lease.
Aboriginal Housing
(Question No. 1838)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2619

What action has been taken by his Department on the recommendation of the Commissioner for Community Relations in his 5th Annual Report, for 1979-80, concerning the provision of public housing appropriate to the needs, values and lifestyles of Aboriginal people in all States and the Northern Territory.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
There is only one aboriginal community within the jurisdiction of the Minister for the Capital Territory, located at Wreck Bay in the Jervis Bay Territory.
20 government homes were provided at Wreck Bay during the 1940s and are occupied by members of the community on a rental basis.
In 1973 the community formed the Wreck Bay Aboriginal Housing Company Ltd to negotiate with the Commonwealth for a lease of land for the community. In 1975 the Company accepted an offer of lease, but subsequently lodged a list of additional claims rejecting the previously accepted offer.
Negotiations have continued since that time but the matter is still unresolved. Two major obstacles are the community's claims for:
freehold title (which the Commonwealth is specifically prohibited by legislation from granting in the Australian Capital Territory or Jervis Bay Territory) and
exclusive fishing rights to waters adjoining the land offered for lease.
An Ordinance was made in 1977 to enable the grant of a perpetual lease to the Company.
The Department of Aboriginal Affairs has over the past few years provided funds to the Company for the construction of additional houses and other improvements. When negotiations for a lease are finalised I understand that Department will assume full responsibility for funding of the Aboriginal community at Wreck Bay.
Apart from housing at Wreck Bay Government housing as- 'sistance in the Australian Capital Territory in the form of rental housing, rental rebates for government tenants on low incomes, and finance to assist with the purchase of government or private dwellings is available to Aboriginal people who live or work in the Australian Capital Territory under precisely the same conditions as it is available to other members of the Australian Capital Territory community. It is considered that such assistance is appropriate to the needs, values and life styles of Aboriginal people in the Australian Capital Territory.
Aboriginal Land Fund Commission Land Purchases
(Question No. 1845)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister representing the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) What were the 216 individual properties throughout Australia listed by the Aboriginal Land Fund Commission following the Land Need Conference of August 1978.
(2) Does the list, as viewed by the Aboriginal Land Fund commission in its Annual Report for 1978-79, provide a basis for land purchase for a long time to come.
(3) Has the list been passed on to the Australian Aboriginal Development Commission; if so, how is it being viewed by the Commission.

Mr Viner-The Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) The Aboriginal Development Commission has asked that the individual properties listed at the Land Needs Conference of August 1978 should not be made public, because this could prejudice its negotiations to buy properties. A summary of the types of properties in each State which were listed at the Conference was published in the Annual Report of the Aboriginal Land Fund Commission for 1978-79.
(2) and (3) The documents from the 1978 Conference have been passed to the Aboriginal Development Commission. The Aboriginal Land Fund Commission and its successor body, the Aboriginal Development Commission, have regarded the needs identified by the Land Needs Conference of August 1978 as an important basis for the land acquisition program. The priorities established at the 1978 Conference have been modified, however, in the light of subsequent developments.

National Sports Centre at Bruce, Australian Capital Territory

Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) There has been no decision to construct an aquatic centre at the National Sports Centre at Bruce, Australian Capital Territory.
(2) The Deakin Pool, currently being used by the Australian Institute of Sport for training of its swimmers has adequate facilities for this purpose. It is, however, some distance from the Institute and the support facilities. For this reason, it is viewed as unsuitable as a training venue in the long term. Furthermore, the Deakin Swimming Pool is a public swimming facility but at those times when the pool is in use by Institute swimmers, the public is denied access to it. The same

situation would arise with any other pool in the Australian Capital Territory being used as a training facility by Institute swimmers.
