Australian Film and Television School: Liability for Pension Costs
(Question No. 1866)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
Has the liability of the Australian Film and Television School to the ,Commonwealth by way of employer contributions for increased pension costs during the period 1973 to 1976 been determined; if so, what is the liability and how will il be met; Hnol, when is the liability to be assessed,
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am advised that the Australian Film and Television School meets its employer superannuation liability in accordance with the Superannuation Act 1976. Payments are made to the Commonwealth in respect of those employees who are contributors to the Commonwealth Superannuation Scheme. The rate of contribution paid by the Australian Film and Television School and a number of other Authorities does not take account of the cost of pension up-dating. An actuarial assessment is being obtained of the extent to which the present contribution rate differs from that required to meet the full accruing superannuation liability (including the cost of updating). This assessment is not expected to be available for sometime.
Telephone Calls by Staff: Department of the Treasury
(Question No. 1874)
Mr Bungey asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the West Australian of 18 July 1979, page 37, that London metropolitan police reduces its telephone bill by $985,000, or about 20 per cent, by use of a monitoring device which recorded length, time and destination of telephone cal1s and the extension from which the call was made.

(2) Are personal telephone calls, particularly trunk and STD calls, made by staff of his Department controlled; if so, in what way.
(3) Has any consideration been givcn to the introduction of monitoring devices for telephone calls in his Department; if so, what was the outcome of the consideration.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No.
(2) Yes. STD 'barring' on telephone extensions used by officers not having an essential or demonstrated need to make official long distance calls has been in operation in Treasury since July 1980. On these telephone extensions, officers wishing to make long distance calls must place them through switchboard operators. The officer is subsequently notified of the charges incurred in making personal calls and the cost is recouped by the Department. A large majority of Treasury telephone extensions have STD bars.
(3) Yes. It is considered that the present controls are adequate.
Telephone Calls by Staff: Department of Defence
(Question No. 1876)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the West Australian of 18 July 1979, page 37, that London metropolitan police reduced its telephone bill by $985,000, or about 20 per cent, by use of a monitoring device which recorded length, time and destination of telephone calls and the extension from which the call was made.
(2) Are personal telephone calls, particularly trunk and STD calls, made by staff of his Department controlled; if so, in what way.
(3) Has any consideration been given to the introduction of monitoring devices for telephone calls in his Department; if so, what was the outcome of the consideration.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The existence of telephone call monitoring devices such as the one referred to in the West Australianofl8 July1979 is known to the Department and Telecom. Similar devices (eg "PETRA") are being used but are of limited usefulness because of insistence by the Telecom Unions on limiting the data provided by these devices to protect the privacy of the individual.
(2) Measures adopted by my Department to minimise use of departmental telephone services on private calls include:
granting of STD access only to a relatively few senior officers who have clearly recognisable functional requirement.
further restriction of STD access will occur following introduction of the Defence National Automatic Telephone System later this year. This system will allow direct dialling between Defence establishments using a hired tie line network;
control of trunk line and STD traffic through the Department's PABX operators in respect of the vast majority of personnel; ie those not having STD access;
frequent issue of circulars and reminders in telephone directories of the necessity to limit private calls to emergency situations. Where trunk calls are involved the costs are recovered from the callers; and

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2621

installation of STD public telephones at Defence establishments to enable personnel to make private calls at their own cost.
(3) If devices become available on the market that are more effective than those already available my Department will consider using them.
Telephone Calls by Staff: Department of Social Security
(Question No. 1880)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to a report in the West Australian of 18 July 1979, page 37, that London metrolpolitan police reduced its telephone bill by $985,000, or about 20 per cent by use of a monitoring device which recorded length time and destination of telephone calls and the extension from which the call was made.
(2) Are personal telephone calls, particularly trunk and STD calls, made by staff of the Department of Social Security controlled; if so, in what way.
(3) Has any consideration been given to the introduction of monitoring devices for telephone calls in the Department; if so, what was the outcome of the consideration.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) Yes.
(2) All staff of the Department of Social Security are advised by way of standing office instructions that personal telephone calls are to be kept to a minimum and only made with prior approval of a supervisor.
Officers wishing to make a personal long distance call do so by booking the call through their local switchboard operator and are required to reimburse the Department for the cost of the call.
Because of the decentralised nature of the Department (operating out of nearly 200 locations throughout the metropolitan areas and major country centres) there is a constant need for staff to use STD facilities in the normal course of their duties. This requirement considerably complicates the arrangements necessary to bar access to STD processes. Nevertheless the Department has installed STD access barring in those offices where it is administratively practicable and cost efficient and where the switchboard equipment is compatible. As non-suitable switchboard equipment is progressively replaced, access barring will be extended.
(3) Due to the decentralised nature of the Department, it is felt that the number of monitoring devices that would need to be purchased and the staff resources that would be required to co-ordinate, investigate and report on monitored details would not be cost effective and in some instances, particularly in remote regional offices, could prove to be counter-productive.
Telephone Calls by Staff: Department of Transport
(Question No. 1881)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the West Australian of 18 July 1979, page 37, that London metropolitan police reduced its telephone bill by $985,000, or about 20

per cent, by use of a monitoring device which recorded length, time and destination of telephone calls and the extension from which the call was made.
(2) Are personal telephone calls, particularly trunk and STD calls; made by staff of his Department controlled; if so, in what way.
(3) Has any consideration been given to the introduction of monitoring devices for telephone calls in his Department; if so, what was the outcome of the consideration.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No I have not heard of the report mentioned by the honourable member; however, I will endeavour to obtain a copy.
(2) Personal calls made by officers of my Department are controlled by departmental Administrative Instructions which restrict local private calls to a minimum. Private trunk calls may be authorised and costs are recouped from the officer making the call. Central Office, Canberra, telephones generally are STD access barred with access being restricted to senior officers and special areas able to satisfy a rigorous test of essentiality, e.g. computer links and special operational areas. Those elements of Central Office presently located in Melbourne are accommodated in several buildings. Where technically feasible STD access has been restricted on the same scale as Canberra but some areas cannot be access barred because of the age of switchboard equipment. These areas are monitored with the Telecom Australia PETRA, (PBX Extension Traffic Recorder and Analyser) device which has been found to be effective in reducing unauthorised STD traffic.
Most Regional Offices of the Department are access barred on the same basis as Central Office. The exception is the South Australia/Northern Territory Regional Office in Adelaide where technical considerations make access barring impracticable. Monitoring equipment has been used from time to time and a new PABX due to be installed by September 1981 will allow permanent access barring on the same basis as Central Office.
(3) See answer (2) above.
Uniform Allowance: Naval Petty Officers
(Question No. 1898)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Is the purpose of the uniform allowance of 58 cents per day paid to Naval Petty Officers to meet the cost of cleaning and maintenance only.
(2) If so, is the replacement cost of new 8-button uniforms for Petty Officers to be borne by the Navy or by the Naval officer concerned.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Uniform maintenance allowance, which is paid at a rate of 58 cents a day to Petty Officers, is provided to defray the cost of periodic replacement of items of uniform so that the member's inventory is to the approved scale of items in good order and condition.
(2) As the new uniform does not change in design or colour from the uniform it is replacing, the change-over is being effected over a period that has regard to the normal life expectancy of a uniform. The members involved will therefore be required to meet the replacement cost of the new uniforms from their uniform maintenance allowance.

Aboriginal Medical Services

Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:

MI' MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes.

Aboriginal Medical Services
(Question No. 1911)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:

What are the criteria governing the availability of Health Program Grants to community-controlled Aboriginal medical services.

Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable

member's question is as follows:

'

On 17 October 1978 Health Program Grants were offered to the ten established Aboriginal controlled medical services
to meet the salary and associated costs of staff involved in the clinical activities. Other costs are met by the Department of Aboriginal Affairs. The arrangement was introduced against
the then possible non-continuance of bulk billing whicb would have adversely affected the Services concerned. Acceptance of a Health Program Grant precludes continuance of bulk
billing for medical services and avoids double payment which would otherwise be the case. Economic restraints preclude the expansion of the arrangement to other more recently established services.

Aboriginal Medical Services
(Question No. 1913)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) What guidelines does the Government use with respect to the availability of Health Program Grants and how have they been applied in relation to 1980-81 funding from the Department of Aboriginal Affairs to community-controlled Aboriginal medical services.
(2) How will it affect funding to those services who choose to continue bulk billing funding in 1981-82.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) In October 1978 my Department offered Health Program Grants to each of the then 10 established Aboriginal Medical Services as an alternative to bulk billing. In 1980-81 Health Program Grant funds have been made available to 8 of these services, with negotiations continuing in respect of another. These grants are to meet the salary and associated

costs of staff involved in clinical activities, with the Department of Aboriginal Affairs providing the necessary funds to meet administrative and other residual costs.
(2) Services which continue to use bulk billing will need to negotiate with the Department of Aboriginal Affairs in regard to the residual funding required.
Radiation Hazards in Australia
(Question No. 1914)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the Australian of 8 April 1981, that Dr John MacKay has provisionally diagnosed radiation sickness in 16 persons connected with a plane crash on 4 December 1980.
(2) Has the NSW Director of Public Health Services discounted the diagnosis on the grounds that blood tests done on policemen who claimed exposure to contamination were negative.
(3) Is he able to say what tests were done, what were the results, what investigations should have been done and what tests should now be done to eliminate the possibility of radiation sickness developing.
(4) Is he able to say what evidence the policemen gave to substantiate their claims.
(5) Have steps been taken to standardise monitoring of radiation hazards in Australia and to centralise responsibility for enforcing radiation safeguards at Australian Government level; if so, what are they.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes. However the incident referred to occurred on a highway in New South Wales when a semi-trailer carrying a mixed cargo was involved in an accident. The cargo included two radioactive sources. These sources were properly packaged, were not breached in the accident and did not constitute a radiation hazard.
(2), (3) and (4) The authority responsible for this matter is the New South Wales Health Commission which has investigated the incident thoroughly. I understand that the Commission is entirely satisfied that the radiation consequences were negligible.
(5) When radiation accidents do occur local radiation monitoring is always carried out by the appropriate State or Territory health authority. National radiation safety standards have been developed under the aegis of the National Health and Medical Research Council and are, in fact, applied uniformly throughout Australia under the relevant health legislation prevailing in each State or Territory.
My Department maintains a national registry of radiation incidents through the Australian Radiation Laboratory. Whenever a significant radiation incident occurs the relevant health authority provides a detailed summary of its history and consequences to the Australian Radiation Laboratory. The Laboratory arranges for its circulation to warn of potential circumstances which may lead to similar incidents in the future.

Access for New Zealand Butter and Cheese to the Australian Market
(Question No. 1933)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 28 April 1981 :
(I) Has he been giving consideration to a submission by the Australian Dairy Farmers Federation in which they ask (a) that the Australian Government formalise at government to government level the presently existing informal agreement permitting access for New Zealand butter and cheese producers to the Australian market and (b) that the Commonwealth Government exclude dairy products from any phased reduction of trade barriers between Australia and New Zealand.
(2) If so, will he indicate (a) were and (b) in what way the Australian Government will act on those recommendations.
(3) If he will not provide this information, what is the reason.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) and (3) The Government is mindful of the concern which has been expressed by Australian dairy farmers about the implications of moves towards a closer economic relationship with New Zealand including for example, the potential

for trade in dairy products from New Zealand to undermine Australia's domestic marketing arrangements. Special attention is being given to such matters as the impact of dairy support and stabilization schemes in the two countries on trading opportunities for dairy products within the context of a closer economic relationship.
The Prime Minister emphasised in his statement to Parliament following his talks with Prime Minister Muldoon in March 1980 that no commitment to any specific proposal had been entered into at that stage and that no decisions would be taken until a detailed examination has been completed and there had been full consultation with interested parties including State Governments and industries.
Consultations with State Governments, farming and industry groups have been underway since April 1980 with views being sought by the Commonwealth on a number of occasions. The views of the Australian Dairy Farmers' Federation have been noted and are being borne in mind in the negotiations with New Zealand.
The guiding principle, enunciated by the Australian Government, is that it would not contemplate entering into any new trading arrangements with New Zealand except on the basis of equality of fair trading opportunities between industries in the two countries. The Prime Minister and myself have stated publicly on a number of occasions that the Government will not allow Australian agricultural industries to be adversely affected by unfair competition from New Zealand.
Senior Australian and New Zealand officials discussed closer economic relations in Wellington on 11 and 12 March 1981. This meeting was held against the background of recent consideration of the issues by Australian Ministers and separately by New Zealand Ministers. The objective of the discussions was to ensure a full understanding of ministerial attitudes on both sides and to clarify differences in position. Resolution of such differences will be necessary before details of any proposed new arrangements can be made public. I should like to emphasise that the Government has given an undertaking that no substantive decisions will be taken on this matter until full and meaningful consultations have been held with all interested parties.
Further discussions on closer trade and economic links were held 11-\3 May 1981 in Wellington, New Zealand between my colleague the Minister for Trade and Resources and the

2624 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

New Zealand Prime Minister and senior New Zealand Ministers.
Taxation: Contributions to Bjelke-Petersen Foundation Fund
(Question No. 1935)
Mrs Darling asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
(1) Did he state in reply to a question without notice on 28 May 1979 (Hansard, page 2429) that the National Party of Queensland or any other branch of a political party in Australia will not be given any privilege under the tax law.
(2) Has his attention been drawn to recent statements made in the Queensland Parliament that the Bjelke-Petersen Foundation Fund involves schemes whereby donations up to $100,000 are paid to the fund in return for which advertisements are featured in the Foundation's journal National Outlook.
(3) Are companies or businesses which contribute to such a scheme eligible for full tax deductions.
(4) If so, does this constitute a tax loophole by which donations to political parties might be tax deductible.
(5) Does this continued practice conflict with his statement that no political party would be given any privilege under the taxiaw.
(6) If so, will he act to close this tax loophole and advise the National Party in Queensland of his decision.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes. (2) Not specifically, but I am aware that from time to time there have been statements linking donations to the Fund with advertising in the journal. (3) I am advised by the Commissioner of Taxation that, under the general deduction provisions of the income tax law, any amount out laid by a company or a business for more than one purpose is tax deductible only to the extent that it relates to a purpose of producing assessable income or is necessarily incurred in carrying on a business to produce assessable income. I am also advised that if the circumstances are that what is really, e.g., a non-deductible gift is merely characterised as being a business outgoing, no deduction is available. (4), (5) and (6) See answer to (3).

Tax Avoidance
(Question No. 1944)
Mr Hurford asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 28 April 1981 :
(1) Is it a fact that, in addition to the problems caused by the growing tax avoidance industry, another main reason for high taxation rates under this Government is the cash economy, resulting in hundreds of thousands of transactions upon which tax should be payable not being recorded.
(2) Is the Government taking action to include the taxable income of these tax evaders in the assessable net so that other law-abiding citizens can have their tax obligations reduced; if so, what are the details.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) I think it must be accepted that tax evasion resulting from unrecorded transactions that go to make up the 'cash' or 'underground' economy has a bearing on the rates of income tax. The taxation laws are, of course, designed to raise a substantial portion of the revenue necessary to meet essential Government expenditure. As a general proposition, therefore, indulgence by some sections of the community in tax evasion practices, whether through participation in the cash economy or not, would increase the taxation burden borne by the general body of taxpayers who comply with the revenue laws.
I do not accept, however, the implication in the honourable Member's question that taxation rates under this Government are higher than those under previous Governments and draw his attention to the personal income tax reforms introduced by this Government since coming to power.
(2) The Commissioner of Taxation has informed me that Taxation Office enforcement programmes covering investigation and audit activities are directed essentially at the detection of unreported transactions. These activities entail examinations of the affairs of taxpayers, including those engaged in illegal activities, with a view to detecting income not disclosed in income tax returns. Special techniques are employed to detect unreported income, including a process under which the assets accumulated by a taxpayer over a period of time are quantified together with other amounts of expenditure. Such examinations have, the Commissioner said, been successful in recovering substantial amounts of tax which would otherwise have been lost to the revenue. Enforcement activities also include action to detect persons who are obliged, but who have failed, to lodge income tax returns. Experience has shown that persons found to be evading tax through non-lodgment of returns are, more often than not, participants in the cash economy.
A detailed account of the results achieved from all Taxation Office enforcement programmes was contained in the Commissioner's 59th Report to Parliament.
Red Gum Forests
(Question No. 1955)
Mr Lloyd asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 28 April 1981 :

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2625

(1) Following the construction of the Dartmouth Dam, Victoria, what arrangements have been made to protect the future of the Barmah and other major Australian red gum forests along the Murray River from (a) flooding in late summer and autumn when the controlled flow of the Murray is increased to provide water for South Australia, but which will endanger the future of the forests and (b) lack of natural flooding in late winter and spring when water is being stored for late summer and autumn release to South Australia, which will endanger the future of the forests.
(2) What quantity of water is required to satisfactorily flood the forests in New South Wales and Victoria, and if there is no natural flooding, from which States' quota will this water be obtained.
(3) When were the forests last satisfactorily flooded in spring and how often should these floods occur to maintain the health of the forests.
(4) Are the forests currently being flooded; if so, when were they last flooded in autumn.
(5) For how long and how often can this flooding occur in autumn before the forests are endangered.
(6) When and how will the Barmah Choke section of the River be partially by-passed or its capacity be increased to allow the post-Dartmouth autumn flows to be contained.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(l) (a) and (b) At the end of July last year, the River Murray Commission released for public comment a report which identifies river regulation and related forest management problems along the River Murray between Tocumwal and Echuca, associated with the Barmah-Millewa forests. The report examines a number of possible measures to alleviate the problems. Solutions to some of the problems fall outside the Commission's area of responsibility. However, I understand that submissions received from a number of individuals and organisations are under consideration prior to the Commission deciding on what future action it can take to remedy any problems.
(2) As indicated in the report, forest managers estimate that effective watering would require the maintenance at Tocumwal of a flow of at least 24,500 megalitres per day for about one month during the winter-spring period or a consumptive use of about 100,000 megalitres of water on each side of the river in anyone year; at this stage, this is a matter for the decision of the State concerned.
(3) According to River Murray streamflow records maintained by the Commission, the criteria suggested in (2) above were last met in the spring of 1978. Forest interests maintain that prior to intensive regulation of the Murray River for irrigation, winter and spring flooding over most parts of the forests occurred in about 4 out of 5 years and that this regime should be restored by artificial means.
(4) No; I understand that minor flooding of relatively small areas of the forests, but sufficient to limit access, has occurred on two occasions during the current autumn season due to temporary high river flows when irrigation diversions from Lake Mulwala were temporarily interrupted because of rain. Some foresters have stated that under the prevailing dry conditions such minor flooding is beneficial to the forests.
(5) and (6) I understand that the River Murray Commission is reasonably confident that significant containment of summerjautumn flooding can be achieved and such flooding is nOllikely to pose any great threat to the health of the forests. I also understand that a by-pass measure proposed in the Commission's report, Le., the use of Mulwala Canal to bypass water around the Barmah forest reach of the river, has

already been successfully put into effect. Investigations are also proceeding on the need for channel maintenance work to improve the channel carrying capacity.
Surveillance of North-west Coastline
(Question No. 1963)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
(1) Is he satisfied that the granting of the contract for daily surveillance of the north-west coastline to the eastern Statesbased operator, H. C. Sleigh, will be in the best interests of both Australia's need for surveillance and the needs of north-west Australian residents for a complete aviation service.
(2) Was H. C. Sleigh told by an officer of Transport Australia early in January 1981 that they would be getting the contract.
(3) Was Trans-West told that there was still no decision on this tender process, as recently as Monday, 30 March 1981.
(4) Is it a fact that H. C. Sleigh have no agreement between themselves and Trans-West to obtain a maintenance service despite the fact that in the tender produced by H. C. Sleigh, such a service was said to be involved.
(5) Is H. C. Sleigh now endeavouring to operate the service out of Broome, WA, instead of Derby, WA, because of accommodation difficulties in Derby.
(6) If such a relocation is to be permitted will he call fresh tenders.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The criteria taken into account in the selection process were those relating to the performance of the specified coastal surveillance task. I am satisfied that the most suitable offer was accepted.
(2) No.
(3) Yes. The advice was accurate.
(4) I understand that at the time the tender was submitted by H. C. Sleigh such an arrangement was in existence, although it was not mentioned in the tender. I understand also that the arrangement was terminated by Trans-West after the coastal surveillance contract was awarded to H. C. Sleigh. The successful tenderer has now made satisfactory alternative maintenance arrangements at no additional cost to the Commonwealth.
(5) and (6) H. C. Sleigh did seek the consent of my Department to operate from Broome rather than Derby. This request was refused.
The Company was informed that there would be no objection to aircraft and crews being based in Broome and ferried to Derby for operations provided the Company met all expenses consequent upon such a decision.
I understand that H. C. Sleigh has now decided to continue to base its operations in respect of this portion of the contract at Derby.

Commonwealth Revenue
(Question No. 1977)
Dr Klugman asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 29 April 1981:
(1) Was Commonwealth revenue (a) SI8,276m in 197-5-76 and (b) S29,661m in 1979-80; if so, does this represent an increase of 62.3 per cent.
(2) What is the latest estimate for revenue for 1980-81 and by what percentage is this an increase on the 1979-80 figure.
(3) Was the increase in the Consumer Price Index 47.4 per cent between 1975-76 and 1979-80.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes. (2) The Budget estimate for total receipts in 1980-81 is S34,471m, an increase of 16.2 per cent over 1979-80. Collections to date do not indicate any significant variation from the overall Budget estimate. (3) The average level of the consumer price index for the seven capital cities in 1979-80 was 48.5 per cent higher than in 1975-76.
Cost of Reducing Age for Eligibility for the Male Age Pension
(Question No. 1979)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 29 April 1981:
(1) Further to the Minister's answer to my question No. 903 (Hansard, 29 April 1981, page 1782), why does the Minister refer to the estimated cost of paying an income test free pension to all men from age 60.

(2) Did my question at any stage suggest a change in the means test at present in force for age pensioners aged 65.
(3) Will the Minister attempt to answer my original question.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member member's question:
(1) to (3) I indicated in my answer to the honourable member's Question No. 903 (Hansard, 29 April 1981, page 1782) that it is not possible to estimate with accuracy the additional cost of paying an income tested age pension to men aged 60 to 64 years. Information was provided on the additional cost of paying an income test free pension to men aged 60 to 64 years as an indication of the maximum costs that could be involved. Given the lack ofdata on the income distribution of men aged 60 to 64 years and the unknown effect on workforce participation rates of lowering the pensionable age, it could be misleading to provide the estimate required by the honourable member and I am not prepared to instruct my Department to do so.

Fisheries Inspectorate
(Question No. 1014)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 5 May 1981:
(1) What are the inspectorate functions of the Northern Zone Export Section of the Fisheries Division of his Department.
(2) How many staff were being employed in the Northern Zone Export Section as at 30 June of each year since 1975 and in what capacity was each staffmember employed.
(3) How many boats were (a) inspected and (b) tested in each of the same years.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The functions of the northern zone of the export fish inspection service are the same as the other zones which together form an integrated administrative scheme designed to ensure the effective deployment and organisation of export fish inspection staff on a regional basis.
The overriding objective of export inspection service is to ensure that product subject to the Exports (Fish) Regulations meets prescribed conditions and restrictions. In particular, export establishments are required to be constructed and equipped to operate in an efficient and hygienic manner; and products are required to be of an acceptable quality and accurately described. Constant monitoring of registered land based premises and registered vessels is undertaken to ensure the maintenance of standards.
(2) Prior to the formation of the export fish inspection service in 1977, all fish exports inspection duties were undertaken by meat inspectors on an ad hoc basis. A northern zone as such did not exist. The position in respect of staff employed in the northern zone as at 30 June of each year since 1977, and the capacity in which they were employed, is as follows:

Joint Venture Fishing Agreement
(Question No. 1016)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 5 May 1981:
(I) Has the Commonwealth Government approved a joint venture agreement with the Kaohsiung Fishing Boat Commercial Guild of Taiwan, and the Kailis Kaohsiung Fishing Co., concerning the conduct ofdemersal pair-trawl and gillnet fishing operations in the Australian Fishing Zone effective for one year from 1 November 1980.
(2) If so, does this agreement state that the Kaohsiung Fishing Boat Commercial Guild and the Kailis Kaohsiung Fishing Co., shall remit promptly to the Commonwealth Government or State Government or Territory concerned, all fines imposed in respect of contraventions by fishing vessels licensed under the Agreement.
(3) Does the Agreement specify the amount of the fine payable by licensed or unlicensed vessels found to be contravening the terms of the Agreement; if not, what is the reason.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No. The Government has not approved a joint venture agreement between the Kaohsiung Fishing Boat Commercial Guild of Taiwan and the Kailis Kaohsiung Fishing Company.
Vessels of Taiwan are licensed to fish in the Australian fishing zone (AFZ) in the demersal pair-trawl and gillnet fisheries of the north-west shelf, under an Agreement between the Kaohsiung Fishing Boat Commercial Guild and the Commonwealth Government. The Agreement runs for 12 months from I November 1980. The Kailis Kaohsiung Fishing Company is the Australian representative of the Guild.

(2) The Agreement provides that the Guild be fully responsible for amongst otber things, the payment of fines and associated expenses incurred by the Australian Government or State or Territory Government in respect of both licensed and unlicensed Taiwanese vcssels convicted of offences in the AFZ.

Education of Deaf Children
(Question No. 246)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 3 December 1980:
Does the Commonwealth Government support specific programs for the education of deaf children; if so, what programs?
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2629

The Commonwealth Government provides support for the education of deaf children through its Special Education Program, administered by the Schools Commission.
Through this Program the Government provides support for the education of aU handicapped children by supplementing the funding of government and non-government special schools and special classes in regular schools. In the case of Government schools, it does this by making block grants available to each State on condition that these grants be spent on the education of handicapped children. It leaves to the State concerned the determination of priorities within Special Education programs. Grants to declared non-government special schools, are recommended to the Commonwealth Education Minister by State Education Departments for approval. Thereafter grants are applied by the education authorities and schools concerned. Each State has programs related to the education of deaf children.
Expenditure priorities, and consequently the level of funding to be applied to supporting the education of deaf children are determined, therefore, by government and nongovernment education authorities in the States and the Northern Territory.
A further element of the Commonwealth Special Education Program under which deaf children may benefit is the program to assist the integration of handicapped children into regular schools. Under this program priority is to be given to the movement of as many children as possible from special to regular schools. Funds may also be used to enable isolated handicapped children to attend neighbourhood regular schools, to test new ways of fully or partially integrating handicapped children already integrated into regular schools and to facilitate the enrolment in regular primary schools of handicapped children who are beginning their formal schooling.
Tactical Air Defence System
(Question No. 561)
Mr Morrison asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(1) Was Government approval given in August 1975 for the purchase of replacement radars for HUBCAP air defence radars and for a tactical air defence system.
(2) Did the Government defer procurement to TADS in 1978.
(3) When was ministerial approval for the deferment obtained.
(4) When does the Government propose to proceed with the purchases.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) and (3) In the context of the Budget considerations for 1978-79 the Department of Defence considered that procurement of the TADS should be deferred. A submission to that effect was put forward in December 1978 and was approved by Government on 9 January 1979.
(4) The Government has purchased and taken delivery of three Westinghouse radars-two for replacement of HUBCAP air defence radars and one for the TADS. The Government approved the acquisition of the remainder of the TADS in the context of the 1979-80 Budget. Tenders are now being evaluated, and the Government expects to place an order in mid 1981.

Coastal Surveillance: Expenditure by Department of Business and Consumer Affairs
(Question No. 572)
Mr Morrison asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:

Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(b) The amounts actually spent on coastal surveillance are reflected in various items of Divisions 195/1 and 195/2, e.g, salaries, overtime, general expenses such as vehicles, launches, communications, aircraft.
(2) The main items of expenditure and the costs attributed to each item have not been det~rmined as the cost of coastal surveillance is not a specific item of Departmental expenditure. The method of assessment of costs of coastal surveillance will be considered as part of the Government's -review of the coastal surveillance program.
National Health and Medical Research Council
(Question No. 632)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 25 February 1981:
(1) Have any members of the National Health and Medical Research Council retired from, or ceased to be active on a full-time basis, in their respective fields of expertise.
(2) What remuneration and/or allowances are paid to Council members.
(3) By what process (a) are matters relating to public health brought to the attention of the Council and (b) is advice on these matters disseminated to the general public.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Only one member oftheNH & MRC has retired from, or has ceased to be active on a full-time basis, in his respective field of expertise. He is Emeritus Professor R. H. Thorpe, whose appointment to the Council is on the basis of his nomination by the Australian Federation of Consumer Organisations rather than by virtue of his expertise in his professional discipline. Professor Thorpe was formerly the Professor of Pharmacology at the University of Sydney.
(2) Members of Council, who are not public servants, are eligible for sitting fees and travel allowances for attendance at approved meetings, in accordance with Category 2 of the Remuneration Tribunal Determination No.9 of 1980. They are also entitled to air transport and reimbursement for incidental expenses, incurred in attending such meetings, approved in accordance with the Audit Act.
However, recently the Government decided that, as a general principle, remuneration will not be paid to advisory committee members unless certain criteria are met. The criteria are being developed for consideration by the Government and should be available for implementation by 1 July 1981.

2630 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

(3) (a) The Council's attention is drawn to matters related to public health through its Public Health Advisory Committee to which several expert committees in a variety of fields report.
(b) In regard to public health, the Council has as its main rule. expressly provided in the order-in-Council under which it is established, to advise the Commonwealth and States on matters of public health legislation and administration. Its advice on these matters is also disseminated to the public through NH & MRC Session Reports and, where appropriate, by way of Press releases and specially published documents.
Transfer Pricing
(Question No; 746)
Mr Humphreys asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 10 March 1981:
(1) Is he able to say how many trans-national corporations are involved in inter-company transfer pricing between their Australian operations and operations abroad as reported in the radio program AM on 2 March 1981 and in the National Times of 1-7 March 1981.
(2) What is the extent of tax revenue to be recouped following the enactment of the proposed new version of section 260 of the Income Tax Assessment Act.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) It is not possible to provide the information sought with any acceptable degree of accuracy. However, during the year ended 30 June 1980, formal determinations in transfer pricing cases, in accordance with section 136 of the Income Tax Assessment Act and/or the relevant provisions of Australia's double taxation agreements, were made on nine occasions. At the end of that year, several other cases were under consideration for application of section 136 and/or the agreement provisions.
(2) No firm estimate can be placed on the tax revenue that might be recouped following the enactment of provisions to replace the existing section 260. It would necessarily require the passage of considerable time before the efficacy of such new provisions, once introduced, could be tested and determined. However, it has been estimated that taxpayer involvement in avoidance schemes that gave rise to claims for artificial losses and deductions resulted in revenue short-falls of $230m and $280m in the 1978-79 and 1979-80 financial years respectively. An allowance of $300m for tax a voidance has been incorporated in the estimates for 1980-81. These amounts relate to schemes against which specific legislative action has been or is being taken. I should add that the Commissioner of Taxation is contesting claims under these schemes and that the amount of revenue ultimately forgone because of them will only be determined upon resolution of the issues by the Courts.
Standards of Television Advertising for Children
(Question No. 754)
Mr Hodges asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:
(1) Does the Broadcasting and Television Act charge the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal with the responsibilities of preparing and enforcing standards of television advertising for children.
(2) Has his attention been drawn to a report of the National Health and Medical Research Council's Working Party on television advertising of foods directed to children.

(3) If so, is he able to say (a) when the Working Party was established and (b) when and to whom it reported.
(4) What is the current position in relation to the report and its recOmmendations.
(5) Did the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal refer the matter to the National Health and Medical Research Council; if not, who (a) initiated the inquiry and (b) was responsible for the drawing up of the terms of reference.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) The Working Party was established by the National Health and Medical Research Council in June 1979. It has yet to complete its final report on television advertising of foods directed to children. The Working Party has, however, reported to the National Health and Medical Research Council through the Nutrition Standing Committee on the preliminary findings of its study. These findings have been provided to the Minister for Health, to me and to the Chairman of the Senate Standing Committee on Education and the Arts.
(4) See answer to Part (3).
(5) The establishment of the Working Party was recommended by the Senate Standing Committee on Education and the Arts, which proposed that the Commonwealth Department of Health, the Australian Medical Association and the Australian Dental Association and the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal consult, with the object of drawing up a set of proposals similar to those of the Federal Trade Commission of the United States of America.
International Subscriber Dialling Service
(Question No. 845)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 24 March 1981:
(1) Did Telecom Australia recently announce surcharges to be levied upon subscribers seeking (a) an unlisted number and (b) to be excluded from the International Subscriber Dialling Service.
(2) If so, is it intended to exempt (a) recipients of (i) age, (ii) invalid, (iii) service, (iv) widows' and (v) wives' pensions and (b) beneficiaries of (i) supporting parent benefits, (ii) unemployment benefit and (iii) student allowances.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) (a) Telecom has decided to introduce a charge of $10 per annum for silent line services. to be applied from the publication date in respect of all telephone directories issued after 1st July f981. This charge is to help offset the costs being incurred in handling Directory Assistance enquiries in relation to this type of service; (b) Telecom is introducing on a progressive basis a Call Charge Record (CCR) system which will automatically record details of subscriber dialled international calls, including the time the call is made, the number called and the cost of the call. At present, the system is being introduced at selected exchanges in the capital cities. With the introduction of the CCR system at an exchange, ISD will be provided to all subscribers connected to that exchange as a standard facility and a separate total for any ISD calls made will appear on their telephone bills. This is distinct from the ISD facility which has been provided on a multi-metering basis, with the charges for ISD calls being recorded on subscribers' meters along with local and STD calls. Because of this bulk billing feature, the ISD facility has been provided only to those subscribers who specifically request it.

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2631

As mentioned ISDICCR is being progressively introduced as a standard facility and, should any subscriber wish to take less than full advantage of the telephone service, there is a standard fee of $3 per annum imposed by Telecom for restricting the service in the required way.
(2) No. The concessions made available at present to subscribers who qualify for pension entitlements are financed from appropriation to the Department of Social Security. It is considered that any further concession should be financed in a similar fashion.

Immigration: East Timorese
(Question No. 984)
Mr Howe asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 1 April 1981 :
(1) Has he reviewed progress in achieving family reunion for the 600 East Timorese people who it was agreed could come to Australia in 1978.
(2) How many persons have actually arrived in Australia under that program.
(3) Is it a fact that almost all of the persons who have arrived are ethnic Chinese and not indigenous Timorese.
(4) Is it also a fact that most persons who leave do so only because they are able to afford to pay bribes to Indonesian Officials.
(5) Is he able to say if and when the remaining persons in East Timor will be permitted to leave.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(l) and (2) I draw the honourable member's attention to my reply to Question No. 986.
(3) My Department does not inquire into the ethnic origins of applicants for admission to Australia and no definitive answer to this question could be provided. However, an examination of the names of those arriving from Timor over recent years would suggest that while some are ethnic Timorese, the majority are of Chinese ethnic origin. (A similar examination of names suggests that about two thirds of the people on the agreed list were of Chinese ethnic origin--this reflects the ethnic composition of the original evacuees to whom these people are related.)
(4) Timorese require permission from the Indonesian authorities both to travel to Jakarta and later to leave Indonesia for Australia. Allegations have been made to my Department that bribes were sought and accepted by Indonesian officials in return for these permits. These allegations have been drawn to the attention of the Indonesian authorities by the Australian Embassy, Jakarta, with expressions of official concern.

(5) The movement of Timorese on the list agreed between the Australian and Indonesian Governments resumed last October, and since then a total of 38 has arrived in Australia, bringing the total arrivals to date to 343 persons. The Indonesian authorities arc aware of the importance the Australian Government attaches to a speedy completion of this program and the Government will continue to pursue this aim.

Head Office, Department ofIndustry and Commerce
(Question No. 1494)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Are any sections of the Head Office of his Department not located in Canberra; if so, what are the sections and how many staff are in each?
(2) Is it planned to relocate these sections in Canberra; if so, when.
Sir Phillip Lynch-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1 ) Yes. The sections concerned, and present staffing (shown in brackets) are:
Shipbuilding Production Section (9) Shipbuilding Administration Section (8) Ship Design Section (26) Project Australia Secretariat (6) Accounting Policy and Systems (12) Administration (7) Aircraft (6) Automatic Data Processing (58) Buildings and Property (10) Cataloguing (22) Consultative Services (7) Electrical Engineering (12) Industrial (15) Internal Audit (2) Security (5) Staff Development (4).
(2) Decisions to relocate units of Government employment to Canberra are taken by Government on the advice of the Location of Australian Government Employment Committee (LAGE), an IDC chaired by the PSB. The advice tendered to Government by the LAGE Committee is based on submissions by the relevant Department, and consultations with interested bodies such as staff organisations. Any plans to relocate the sections in Canberra is a matter for LAGE consideration and subsequent decisions by Government.

Recycling of Paper: Department of Defence
(Question No. 1579)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 9 Apri11981:
(I) Has any action been taken by his Department to recycle paper; if so, what action.

Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The Department of Defence uses paper re-cycling services for unclassified waste where these are arranged by the Department of Administrative Services. This is the system presently in use in the Defence Regional Offices in NSW and Victoria.
(2) Generally unclassified waste paper not covered by the process in (1) above is disposed of by cleaners contracted through the Department of Administrative Services.
(3) Under no circumstances will classified waste be made available for fe-cycling. Classified waste is sealed in marked bags and burnt under surveillance.
Recycling of Paper: Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs
(Question No. 1587)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 9 Apri11981:
Has any action been taken by his Department to recycle paper; if so what action.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Where possible obsolete forms and other non-classified paper are reused within the Department.
In other cases, used paper is disposed of through Commonwealth waste disposal contracts under the Supervision of the Department of Administrative Services. I understand that non-soiled waste paper is recycled through the Canberra Paper and Cardboard Recycling Company.
Recycling of Paper: Department ofHousing and Construction
(Question No. 1594)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Housing and Construction, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
Has any action been taken by his Department to recycle paper; if so, what action.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The following procedures have been established by the Department of Housing and Construction and are operational for the recycling ofwaste paper:
Out of date minute paper, letterhead and other usable clean waste paper is made into pads and used for note paper.
Bulk pre-printed paper, forms and unusable material is collected by officers of the Department of Administrative Services and delivered to a recycling depot for pulping.
Cardboard and similar clean paper waste is collected by a recycling firm.
Classified material is disposed of by burning or pUlping.
Radio Australia
(Question No. 1634)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
Have studies been made to assess the suitability of Christmas Island for location of transmitters for Radio Australia; if so, what were the major conclusions of the studies.

2638 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Detailed submissions prepared by Telecom Australia concerning the suitability of Christmas Island for use as a transmitting station by Radio Australia were considered by the Independent Inquiry into Radio Australia chaired by Sir Keith Waller CBE. In Section 12.2 of its Report of December 1975, the Inquiry found that this site offered transmission advantages over Darwin and Carnarvon to certain prime target areas; however, in the light of Radio Australia's priorities,
high cost, and the difficulties of operating such a station, it
concluded that these advantages were not great enough to justify establishment at Christeas Island.
The Inter-Departmental Committee which considered the recommendations of the Waller Report, found that these reasons were still valid. Eventually it was decided to restore the Radio Australia facilities at Cox Peninsula.
Fire extinguishers
(Question No. 1635)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
What action has been taken by his Department concerning the finding by the Department of Housing and Construction reported in the 1979-80 Annual Report of the Commonwealth Fire Board, that self-contained automatic halon gas fire extinguishers installed in fume cupboards where acid chemicals are used deteriorate rapidly.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
My Department does not have any self-contained automatic halon gas fire extinguishers installed in fume cupboards where acid chemicals are used.

Servicing of Jet Engine used in 'Spirit of Australia'
(Question No. 1663)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 9Apri11981:
Is the jet engine used by Mr Ken Warby in his record breaking boat 'Spirit of Australia' serviced by Defence personnel; if so, (a) under what arrangements, (b) at what cost to the Commonwealth and (e) who else is receiving similar assistance.
Mr KiUen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
In 1978 agreement was reached between Mr Warby and my Department to a 'one-time' Service of the J34 engine fitted to the 'Spirit of Australia' in preparation for his attempt on the world water speed record. In recent years the J34 type engine

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2639

has been used as the major training engine for engine fitters at the RAAF School of Technical Training, Wagga. These engines are stripped, rebuilt, test run, and calibrated by trainees as part of their training. As Mr Warby's engine was the only one in Australia fitted with an afterburner it provided an excellent opportunity to demonstrate and teach afterburner operations to trainees whilst at the same time directly benefiting RAAF morale.
(a) Mr Warby signed a form of indemnity in which he agreed to provide all materials required to service the engine and to indemnify the Commonwealth and its Servicemen or servants against any claims that might arise as a result of the servicing.
(b) The servicing was provided at no additional cost to the Commonwealth.
(c) Although it is RAAF policy to look for training projects of this nature to provide motivation and interest to trainees, no similar assistance of this magnitude is currently being provided.
Plant, Pest and Disease Survey of Norfolk Island
(Question No.I7ll)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
(1) What results were obtained from the plant, pest and disease survey of Norfolk Island conducted by officers of his Department in 1980.
(2) What was the total cost to the Commonwealth of the survey.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The results of the survey are not yet available. A preliminary report has been prepared and submitted to the Norfolk Island Administration on material collected on the first pest and diseases survey undertaken during February 1980. Material collected on the second survey conducted during November 1980 is currently being processed and it is anticipated that the final report will be available by July 1981 for presentation to the Norfolk Island Administration.
I do not propose to make known any results until the Administration has had the opportunity to examine the final report.
(2) Approximately $5,000.
Health Care Consumers' Association: Grant from the Capital Territory
(Question No. 1720)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 Apri11981:
What is the Health Care Consumers' Association and what qualified it for a grant of $450 from the Capital Territory Health Commission in 1979-80.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Health Care Consumers' Association of the ACT is a community based organisation that aims to assist in the improvement of health and health care through consumer involvement by:
Promoting goodwill and understanding between those who render and those who use health care;
investigating the availability of health care; educating the public on the availability of health care;

assessing from the consumer point of view, the adequacy of the health care available and drawing such assessments to the attention of appropriate individuals or groups. The Capital Territory Health Commission made a Community Health Grant of $450 in 1979-80 to the Health Care Consumers' Association for the purpose of conducting a survey of children's experiences in hospital. This survey was considered by the Commission to be of value to the community and to the Commission. The program was under the auspices of the International Year of the Child Committee.
The grant from the Commission covered part of the cost involved in preparation, printing and distribution of a multilingual questionnaire sent to parents with children in the two Commission hospitals.
Deportation of New Zealand Citizen
(Question No. 1734)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 9 Apri11981:
(1) Was a New Zealand citizen by the name of Collins recently deported to New Zealand; and if so
(2) Can such a deportation be effective and what safeguards exist to prevent re-entry to Australia.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) It is assumed that the person referred to is Brett Anthony Collins.
On 4 July 1980 I signed an order under Section 13 of the Migration Act 1958 for the deportation of Mr Collins. Subsequently Mr Collins applied to the Administrative Appeals Tribunal for the order to be reviewed. On 22 January 1981 the Tribunal affirmed my decision. The following day Mr Collins lodged an appeal with the Federal Court. A stay of deportation was granted on 23 January 1981 pending the hearing of the appeal.
(2) If Mr Collins is deported, administrative action will be taken to prevent his return. Past experience has shown that, by adopting false identities and abusing the travel facilitation arrangements agreed between Australia and New Zealand, some criminal deportees have attempted, or have been able, to re-enter Australia. This is one of a number of reasons which influenced the Government to decide that persons claiming the benefits of'the Trans-Tasman Travel Arrangement will be asked to identify themselves and to satisfy officials at points of entry that they are entitled to enter Australia. Passport checks have been introduced for this purpose.
Immigration Control Functions
(Question No. 1735)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
What actions have been taken to overcome the limitations on manpower and financial resources for immigration control functions hindering efforts to take advantage of information available on the whereabouts of prohibited immigrations as reported on page 52 of Review '80 published by his Department.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs is engaged in a study of prohibited immigrants in Australia and of methods and procedures by which it can more effectively use its resources to control prohibited immigration. It is not desirable that details of methods and procedures be made public.

2640 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

I can say, however, that I have asked the Department to take vigorous action to achieve a substantial reduction in the number of overstayed visitors, to ask potential or actual prohibited immigrants to leave Australia and, as a last resort, to effect deportation when so ordered. 1 have written to the State and Northern Territory Ministers responsible for immigration and ethnic affairs matters and have discussed with them the possibility of denying prohibited immigrants access to certain services provided free to Australian residents or heavily subsidised by Australian taxpayers.
During the period of the Regularisation of Status Program which operated throughout the second half of 1980 the Department's staff was directed to that program rather than to seeking out prohibited immigrants. As I have made clear, people illegally in Australia who did not take advantage of that last opportunity to regularise their immigration status or who were ineligible to apply cannot expect to be allowed to remain in Australia after the program concluded. Action to detect, apprehend and deal with prohibited immigrants has again been stepped up. The rate of detection has increased in recent weeks. In April it was a monthly record. Prosecutions for working without authority have been instituted successfully. It is expected that as a result of the action taken and under further examination it will be possible to reduce the number of prohibited immigrants in Australia.
Deportations
(Question No. 1737)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
Will he expand the table Deportation Orders Made on page 52 of Review '80 to show the causes for the substantially different figures in the table Deportations Carried Out on the same page of that publication.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The difference between figures in the table Deportation Orders Made on page 52 of Review '80, and the table Deportations Carried Out, is attributable to the time lag incurred in many deportations, mainly due to the following factors:
(i) deportation orders made include people whose whereabouts were not known at the time and as it takes a considerable time to locate many of them, their deportation is not necessarily carried out within the same statistical period;
(ii) orders made against criminals are not executed until the deportees complete the custodial portion of their prison sentences; and

(iii) some deportees contest the decision to order their deportation by legal challenge through the courts or through review of the decision by the Administrative Appeals Tribunal.
A further reason for the difference between orders made and deportations carried oui is that it is normal practice for any fresh information or new circumstances to be considered before an order in force for some time is executed. As a consequence some deportation orders are revoked. Precise statistics are not kept.
Visa Documentation
(Question No. 1738)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) How many persons were carried to Australia during the year 1 November 1979 to 31 October 1980 without the required visa documentation, and how many were carried by each carrier.
(2) What carriers were prosecuted in respect of these breaches during the same period.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Two thousand seven hundred and one persons are recorded as having arrived by air in Australia during the period 1 November 1979 to 30 October 1980 without visas, who required visas. The table below provides details. It should be noted that the figure includes persons to whom approval was given to travel without a visa in emergency circumstances. It also covers certain exempt persons e.g. transit passengers who are not required to hold a visa for a stay of up to 72 hours, but who on arrival are assessed as non-bona fide and refused entry. Separate statistics of these categories are not maintained.
(2) While evidence existed that some carriers had brought in immigrants without visas, action against these carriers could not be taken because of defects in the relevant provisions of the Migration Act. These defects were removed by amendment of the Migration Act which came into effect on 1 July 1980. Since that date information has been laid against one carrier and other possible prosecutions are being examined.
(3) Airline companies generally have closely co-operated with the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs with a view to eliminating the carriage of unauthorised persons. Their efforts are reflected in the fact that arrivals without visas in the period about which you inquired amount to 0.13 per cent of the total arrivals.

Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2643

(1) Did he say in answer to my question No. 751 (Hansard, 26 March 1981, page 1071) that 328,730 persons arrived in Australia between 1975 and 1979 with the stated intention of settling.
(2) if so, how many of these persons would have been eligible to apply for Australian citizenship, given the requirements of the Australian Citizenship Act.
Mr Macphee--The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(J) Yes.
(2) Settlers are residentially eligible for the grant of citizenship after 3 years' residence in Australia. Persons with three months' service with the permanent armed forces of Australia are exempted from this general provision. There is discretion to waive the residence requirement in the case of spouses, widows or widowers of Australian citizens, or spouses of persons approved for the grant of citizenship, former Australians, and persons under 21 years of age.
Without a breakdown of the 328,730 settlers arriving between 1975 and 1979 in categories such as the foregoing, it is not possible to give an accurate answer. However, it is likely that about 150,000 of the settlers who arrived between January 1975 and June 1979 would have become residentially qualiflcd to apply for Australian citizenship during that period. Most of those who arrived before April 1978 would have met the residential requirements by now.
Disruption to Australian High Frequency Radio Communication
(Question No. 1932)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice,on 29 April 1981:
(1) How many complaints have been received by him and from what quarters have they originated in regard to disruption to Australian high frequency radio communication.
(2) Is it a fact that this interference (a) comes from an over-the-horizon radar system which can track objects such as aircraft which cannot be detected by conventional radar and (b) is making communications extremely difficult and sometimes impossible where (i) air to ground, (ii) ship to shore and (iii) land mobile radar links are concerned and (c) could blot out mayday distress calls and thereby threaten lives.
(3) If so, (a) what action has been taken to establish the origin of the interference and (b) has he notified the International Telecommunication Union of the United Nations Organisation of the problems being experienced in communications.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) Since I March 1981 there has been a total of 41 complaints received of interference to services, other than amateur, in the HF bands, 4 of which can be attributed to Over the Horizon Radar and the remainder to other sources such as electrical interference, unlicensed stations and other overseas services. The complaints were received from the operators of the licensed services affected.
In addition, fifteen Amateur Stations reported interference to the amateur bands indicating the source to be from an over the horizon radar transmitter at Kiev, USSR. A further twelve amateurs reported interference to amateur bands from a variety of sources, and in some cases, the interference described could well be from other amateur stations.
My Department has received no complaints from responsible authorities concerning interference from over the horizon radar to air-to-ground, ship to shore or land mobile radio

links. No interference has been observed on or near any of the safety channels which are recognised by international agreement.
2 (c) It is possible that difficulty could be experienced with distress calls if these radar transmissions are made on or adjacent to frequencies assigned for distress calls at the time a distress call is made. However, there is no evidence available within my Department to indicate that any distress calls have been impaired to the extent that a call has been lost as a result of such interference.
Departmental monitoring stations have observed OHR signals on 27.880 MHz, the frequency used for distress in the Australian Inshore Boating Radiocommunication Service (IBRS).
3 (a) My Department is aware of the general belief that the OHR signals are coming from a site or sites near Kiev in Russia. My Department's monitoring of these transmissions while not being conclusive, does not conflict with this belief.
3 (b) No. However, my Department has initiated communication with the USSR seeking their co-operation in avoiding interference to the 27.880 MHz safety channel used by the IBRS.

Hydrogen-powered Car
(Question No. 2005)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 29 April 1981 :
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a letter published in the Bulletin, Rockhampton, Queensland, on 18 April 1981, reporting that (a) the Consumers' Solar Electric Power Corporation in Culvar City, California, United States of America, has a hydrogen-powered car using an easily stored ammoniabased liquid hydride produced from hydrogen provided by electrolysis using solar power through photovoltaic cells available at one-tenth of the usual commercial cost, making local fuel production by service stations a feasible proposition, and (b) engine conversion similar to that done for this car cost approximately $US350 at present.
(2) Will he investigate the report and, in particular, seek the views of the Australian National University Research School of Physical Sciences, Department of Engineering Physics, where alternative catalytic processes have been developed using solar heat concentration for producing ammonia and hydrogen.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) Yes.
(2) Representations by the Consumers' Solar Electric Power Corporation concerning their solar-hydrogen production process have been receiving departmental consideration since August 1979. The economic basis of this process is the ability to mass produce photovoltaic cells at less than one tenth the cost of current cells. At present this is only an ambitious goal which the United States Department of Energy Program hopes to realise by 1986. Realisation of this goal would enhance the commercialisation of many technologies which are based on solar cells.
Liquid hydrides as a means of storing hydrogen for use as a fuel are a new concept. This has been bought to the attention of the ANU. They advise that they would require more technical information before they could assess the claims made in the letter for these liquid hydrides. However, I understand that the chemical composition of the ammonia-based liquid hydride called Hy-Fuel by the Consumers' Solar Electric

Power Corporation is confidential pending the granting of patent protection.
Defence Computer Program
(Question No. 2236)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 12 May 1981:
(1) Have reports by the Auditor-General often criticis~d the management of the Defence computer program and Its capabilities; if so, have the problems been remedied.
(2) What stage has the installation of the Univac computer system reached.
(3) Have the Univac computers now being installed by his Department already been superseded by changed technologies.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Auditor-General over the past five years has cri-' ticised the management of the Defence computing program and its capabilities in relation to:
(a) Honeywell Computer Operations-Criticism of Honeywell operations centred on reliability and maintenance of Honeywell machines and, the ability to maintain an essential service until systems were transferred to Univac. These problems were addressed by the installation of an additional Honeywell H8200 in June 1978.
(b) System Development-In 1976-77 the Auditor General commented on an apparent lack of an approved plan detailing a timetable for the transfer of systems from Honeywell to Univac. This area has since been subjected to rigorous estimating, resource and project control procedures. A Schedule of Activity and Achievement was approved in September 1977 and has been updated regularly to reflect the current position. This schedule is endorsed yearly by a high level Policy Committee. In his 1979-80 report the Auditor General noted the continued improvements in project control and that miw systems development and design methodology is planned for introduction in 1981; this is expected to result in further improvement.
(c) Mini Computers-Criticism in this area referred to slippage of target dates, apparent over manning of data preparation operator positions at the larger RAAF bases and problems of site works and accommodation. Development and implementation of systems on mini computers is now progressing substantially according to target dates. More work is being done on data preparation establishment criteria. Site modifications are done as funds allow.
(d) Micrographics-In 1977-78 the Auditor-General referred to Defence placing work with outside contractors instead of the Micrographics Service Bureau of the Australian Government Publishing Service. All micrographic output produced by Defence Computing Division is now processed by AGPS.
(2) Two mainframe computers, models 1100-82, are installed in separate locations. Each is working 10 shifts per week. A third mainframe, also an 1100-82, approved in January 1981, is expected to be installed at a third location by mid 1982.
(3) No. All hardware is current, with the exception of two leased communication controllers which are in the process of being updated. Similarly, all manufacturer's software in use is the current version.
