Ord River Scheme
(Question No. 2270)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 14 May 1981:
(1) What was the specific purpose of the Western Australian Agreement (Ord River Irrigation) Act.
(2) What funds are still available under the Act.
(3) Are the funds in the form of a non-repayable grant or a loan.
(4) For what purpose will the money be used in relation to the Ord River Scheme.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) To provide funds to the State for the development of Stage 2 of the Ord River Irrigation Project.
(2) and (3) $23.8m of loan funds provided for under the Agreement are currently unexpended.
(4) Funds will now only be provided for already approved grain handling and storage facilities.

As a result of these arrangements, shippers of refrigerated meat had the benefit for six months of a 5 per cent lower freight rate than otherwise would have applied. Additionally, there will be no further increase in the Conference base rates during 1981.
(b) The Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation has kept the matter of available capacity and alternative carriers under constant review. The Corporation has from time to time met with representatives of lines serving the Middle East region other than those of the Australia Middle East Gulf Conference. It is understood that these non-conference lines have achieved a reasonable degree of success and have attracted a sizeable proportion of the refrigerated meat volume being lifted to Middle East destinations.
(3) The AMLC has responsibility under its enabling legislation, the AMLC Act 1977, to negotiate freight rates and other conditions of carriage on behalf of the meat industry with Conference Lines and other carriers. The Act provides for Ministerial approval for shipping arrangements made by the Corporation. In the Government's view the AMLC has discharged this responsibility satisfactorily to date.
In addition, the Government is represented at freight rate negotiations between the Australian Shippers Council and Conference Lines under the provisions of Part 10 of the Trade Practices Act 1974.

Freight Charges for Exports to Middle East and Gulf Countries
(Question No. 712)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 5 March 1981:
(I) Is he able to say whether a period of 12 months has elapsed since the formation of the Middle East and Gulf Shipping Conference; if so, have freight charges for the export of frozen meat from Australia to the Middle East and Gulf countries increased since the establishment of the Conference.
(2) Has the Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation been (a) negotiating reduced freight rates to those countries or (b) seeking alternate carriers; if so, with what success.
(3) Has the Government taken any action on the matter beyond that undertaken by the Corporation; if so, what has been achieved.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes; yes.
(2) (a) Alignment of freight rates upon formation of the Australia Middle East Gulf Conference on 1 March 1980 resulted in a substantial effective increase in the freight rate being applied by the shipping line carrying the bulk of meat cargoes to the Gulf area. Subsequently, the Conference reached agreement with the Australian Shippers Council on a 17t per cent increase in freight rates from 10 July 1980. Meat shipper representatives did not accept this increase negotiated in the Shippers Council and the Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation (AMLC) , on behalf of meat shippers, conducted separate negotiations with the Conference. No agreement emerged from those negotiations.
The outcome was that the AMLC, with the approval of the Minister for Primary Industry, invoked powers available in its Act and set a maximum freight rate providing for a l2t per cent increase in meat freight rates from 4 July 1980. Subsequent discussions between AMLC and the Conference on 8 December 1980 resulted in agreement by both parties to a further 5 per cent increase in meat freight rates to apply for one year from 1 January 1981.

East Timor Military Exercises
(Question No. 1152)
Mr Howe asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(1) Is he able to say what is the scale of the current military exercises in Indonesia.
(2) Are there any Australian observers present.
(3) Are there any Australian observers in East Timor observing the military exercises there.

2646 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

(4) Is he also able to say whether, as reported in the Indonesian Press, the East Timorese exercises represent the preliminaries to a major offensive against Fretelin forces.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The recent major Indonesian joint exercise, which was held in eastern Indonesia from 18 March to 1 April, involved the deployment of some 35,000 men together with naval ships and aircraft. The exercise was conducted in three separate locations in eastern Indonesia-Timor, Maluku, and Irian Jaya. The exercise was part of a continuing program of exercises being held throughout Indonesia aimed at upgrading the defensive capability of Indonesia's armed forces. The previous exercise in 1980 was conducted in the First National Defence Area Command which covers Sumatra and West Kalimantan.
(2) and (3) The Australian Defence Attache, together with a number ofother military attaches from other countries resident in Jakarta, observed al1 parts of the exercise at the invitation of the Indonesian Government.
(4) Matters relating to security in the Indonesian province of East Timor are the direct domestic responsibility of the Indonesian Government, and it is not for me to speculate on Indonesian Government intentions. I am able to say, that the Defence Attache's report of the Timor segment of the exercise contained no indication that the exercise was anything more than an exercise. However, it is possible that Fretelin guerilla remnants continue to be active in parts of the province and it would be expected that Indonesian forces conduct operations against them.

Powers of Delegation
(Question No. 1668)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 ;
(I) What statutory authorities or officers possess powers of delegation similar to that requested by the Commissioner for Community Relations in his 5th Annual Report, for 1979-80, at page 9, enabling them to grant legislative protection to persons in the community who give them assistance.
Mr Viner-The Attorney-General has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) In his 5th Report the Commissioner for Community Relations lists briefly recommendations made in earlier reports. In his 4th Report the Commissioner set out more fully the recommendation to which the honourable member refers, as follows:
The Commissioner for Community Relations should have power to delegate his functions as required to Consultative Committees on Community Relations, other organisations, and individuals whom he considers capable of assisting him to inquire into and to settle complaints of racial discrimination lodged with him in writing.
The Racial Discrimination Act as it then stood empowered the Commissioner to delegate powers to members of his staff but not to other persons.
The recent amendments to the Racial Discrimination Act will permit the Human Rights Commission to delegate its functions under the Act to any person and the Commissioner for Community Relations to delegate his functions to any person approved by the Commission.
PVC Workers
(Question No. 1704)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) Has the attention of the Minister been drawn to a report in the United Kingdom newspaper Daily Mirror of II July 1980, claiming that workers in PVC factories are especially susceptible to an often fatal disease, angiosarcoma.
(2) If so,is there any substance in the report.
(3) What action has been taken in Australia to ensure that PVC workers are working in a safe environment and are not in danger from this disease.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Answers to Questions

26 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2649

(I) Yes.
(2) and (3) The National Health and Medical Research Council (NH & MRC) has, for some years, been aware of the human carcinogenic properties of vinyl chloride and of health hazards to workers engaged in the production of vinyl chloride and its conversion to polyvinyl chloride (PVC).
Accordingly, the Council has produced documents recommended for application in the vinyl chloride industry, including--
Statement to employees in the vinyl chloride industry
Vinyl Chloride- -Australian Code of Safe Practice.
These documents, endorsed by the Council at its Eightyfirst Session in 1975 and updated since then, have been widely distributed.
It should be noted, however, that the NH & MRC acts in an advisory capacity only to governments in Australia on matters relating to public health. The question of implementation of the Council's recommendations is, therefore, primarily one for consideration by the individual governments concerned in the context of their own legislative and enforcement requirements.

Micronesia
(Question No. 1928)
Mr Howe asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 28 April1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to the concern being expressed by Micronesian leaders over the Reagan Administration's delay in considering an agreement on the future political status of Micronesia.
(2) Has his attention also been drawn to the fact that this concern has been intensified because of the Reagan Administration's failure to re-appoint Mr Peter Rosenblatt, the head of the United States of America negotiating team for 3t years, thus delaying the notification of the formula known as the Compact of Free Association.
(3) What is the Australian Government's attitude towards Micronesian independence and is it prepared to place pressure on the Reagan Administration to speed up the process towards independence.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) I am aware that a number of Micronesian spokesmen have expressed concern that the Compact of Free Association might be delayed following the decision of the United States Administration to review United States policy on ending the Trusteeship.
(2) I am also aware that Mr Rosenblatt submitted his resignation as Head of the United States Delegation to the Micronesian Political Status Negotiations to the incoming President. This action is normal procedure for holders of executive office in the United States after the election of a new President. A successor to Mr Rosenblatt has not yet been appointed by the new Administration, and the negotiations remain suspended for the time being. However, the failure to appoint a new Head of the United States Delegation need not necessarily delay the endorsement of the Compact of Free Association, although it does appear probable that termination of the Trusteeship this year will not be achieved.
(3) The Australian Government looks forward to the successful conclusion of the long process of negotiation which will settle the political status of the Micronesian entities. We have noted that the process has involved, and will continue to involve, extensive consultation with the peoples of the islands. I see no reason for Australia to make the sort of representations to the United States on this issue suggested by the honourable member.

(5) Who are the members of its board. (6) What Minister takes responsibility for it. (7) Was it establishment notified to the Commonwealth Parliament; if so, why.
(8) Is the ATC the private venture corporation referred to in recommendation 22 of the Myers Report.
(9) Will steps be taken to advise the Australian community of the nature and purpose of the ATC; if so, what steps.

Medical Alert Systems: Taxation Deduction
(Question No. 2083)
Mrs Kelly asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 6 April 1981 :
Will he give consideration to allowing the cost of personal medical alert systems such as Vital Call to be made tax deductible in the interests of achieving effective savings in the provision of hospital or nursing home beds by allowing patients to be supervised effectively at home.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The cost of medical alert systems such as Vital Call which, it is understood, are primarily designed for use by elderly persons, is expenditure of a private or domestic nature which is not deductible for income tax purposes.
While the advantages of such a system for use in many situations are appreciated, the benefit that a taxation deduction might provide would depend upon the taxable income of the person involved. Persons in receipt of higher incomes and least in need of financial assistance would benefit from a deduction while, for example, elderly persons with incomes below the amount at which tax becomes payable would obtain no benefit at all. In view of this, it is doubtful whether the income tax system is an appropriate means of providing relief to the users of such systems.

Tactical Fighter Program
(Question No. 2237)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 12 May 1981:
(1) What is the nature of the additional travel to the value of $.245m provided for in the 1980-81 supplementary estimates.
(2) Does this figure represent a further assessment visit to the United States of America in relation to the new tactical fighter (NTF) program.
(3) What new developments have occurred in the development of the 2 remaining contenders for this role which were not able to be assessed by previous assessment teams.
(4) Have any modifications been made to the F-16's radar and all weather performances.
(5) Has the F-18 program been suspended pending a review of the program by the United States Secretary of Defense as has been publicly reported in the USA.
(6) How many senior personnel who were involved with earlier assessment teams have been replaced or are no longer with (a) the project or (b) his Department.
(7) Are any additional funds to be used to provide new team members with familiarisation briefing in the USA with respect to the 2 aircraft.
(8) Has there been any increases or reduction in civil and RAAF personnel attached full time or part time to the NTF project in (a) Australia and (b) the USA.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(l) On 4 December 1980 I informed the House that the Government saw no alternative but to await evaluation of further data around mid 1981 before adopting any view as to the relative merits of selection of either aircraft, the F-16 or F IA-18 for Australia's new Tactical Fighter. That position has not changed. Additional funding for travel by officers of

2652 REPRESENTATIVES 26 May 1981

Answers to Questions

my Department was required to enable the necessary further evaluations to be carried out.
(2) Yes. (3) and (4) Until analysis of all the necessary information is completed and the Government's decision on the final selection made public, it would be premature for me to answer these questions or others like them. (5) No. (6) On the assumption that senior personnel equate to Group Captain (Clerk Class ll/equivalent) and above, the answer is as follows: (a) Assessment Team Members
(1) replaced-one Group Captain, (Note that the replacement has had previous experience with the Project).
(2) no longer with the project-one Group Captain (post disestablished).
(3) no longer with the Department of Defence-Nil. (b) Higher Defence Staff-following the 180 evaluation
Deputy Secretary C, Mr D. H. Eltringham was

appointed the Permanent Head of the former Department of Productivity.
(7) No. All visits to the United States by officers responsible for assessment are for purposes of evaluation, not for purposes of familiarisation.
(8) (a) There has been a reduction of two RAAF personnel and an increase of one civilian attached full time to the NTF project in Australia. However, one of the two RAAF posts was transferred to the USA for full-time project activities. This produced an increase of one full-time RAAF post in the USA. There were no increases in full-time civilian posts in the USA.
(b) With regard to part time activities related to the NTF project there have been a number of specialist teams involved in the 1981 phase of the evaluation. While the same total number of people as in 1980 have been and will be involved in part-time duty in the USA, the requirement for specialists in particular areas of investigation has resulted in an increase in the requirement for what may be broadly termed scientific specialists. For this reason there will be an increase of three civilians and a decrease of three RAAF personnel when compared to the 1980 evaluation mission.
